# About the Author

Christopher Reid Becker is currently working as a creative product designer/senior UX designer/educator/author as an associate professor at the Art Center College of Design and sr. lecturer in (UX/I) at OTIS College of Art and Design as well with other clients, and start-ups. From doodles on scrap paper to crafting seamless user experiences, his journey in design has been a tapestry woven with a passion for creative, human-centered problem solving and a relentless curiosity about people.

He has been drawn to solving creative problems and delving into the intricacies of human nature. Design became the perfect intersection of his art background and the limitless possibilities that the digital realm offers. It allowed him to channel his creativity into crafting solutions that people not only use but genuinely enjoy.

His design and teaching approach is deeply rooted in human-centered methods. As a design educator, he applies the Feynman technique and finds that teaching both the practice and theory of design keeps him sharp, allowing him to constantly evolve, bringing a unique blend of knowledge and hands-on experience to the table.

As a designer and creative leader, my commitment to the design community extends beyond the studio. Constantly learning and evolving, he engages in workshops, public speaking, and mentorship. This not only keeps me attuned to UX design trends but also allows him to contribute actively to the growth of the design landscape.

# About the Reviewer

Prathamesh D. is a seasoned Principal UX Designer with deep expertise in crafting user-first designs and delivering exceptional digital experiences. With a career built on innovation, user research, and design thinking, his skill set encompasses interaction design, information architecture, design systems, usability testing, accessibility, and product strategy.

A holder of multiple industry-recognized certifications in UX design, human-computer interaction (HCI), Agile product development, and accessibility standards, Prathamesh brings a strategic approach to solving complex design challenges.

Specializing in creating intuitive interfaces and seamless workflows, he excels at collaborating with cross-functional teams to deliver impactful products that drive user satisfaction and business success. Currently contributing his expertise to a leading U.S.- based MNC, Prathamesh plays a pivotal role in the healthcare and fintech sectors, scaling user-centered design practices and leading groundbreaking design initiatives.

# Preface

If you Google Search UX, you will see a barrage of YouTube videos, articles, UX/UI bootcamps, and undergraduate and graduate degree programs. The amount of information is a by-product of the need. If you went back to the early 2000s, UX design was still a thing, but it was only important among a much smaller circle of software and technology circles. However, UX design is now understood by more commoners and has been applied to a wider audience. We are somewhat recognized.

Since technology has seeped into every nook and cranny of our lives and businesses, focusing on users and UX design has led to success for some organizations, when one business creates success, it also garners followers.

You might have picked up this book either because you heard about UX design at your work or, more than likely, UX design showed up in your web search results in some way. It is no longer in the mouths of people, inside the bubble of Tech, but it has traversed and become more commonplace as every company touches the web or software in some way.

UX design is for people who are inquisitive, systems-driven, and tenacious. This book will not give you a cheat code or allow you to skip steps, but if you are new to UX or are just looking to improve your process, you will find this book aligns with your goals.

UX design is constantly changing and the frameworks and strategies explored in this book will help you evolve with the world and help you gain skills.

This book is focused on the design process and bridging the gaps many in the world of design feel, both implicitly and explicitly. There are always gaps, and as designers, we need to work on ways we can improve our work and build better systems for our users and our businesses.

Software instruction is not the focus of the book. We will help with some software tips/ tricks along the way; however, you will be expected to learn software on your own. There are many resources for learning software tools, and I recommend you add those as complementary to the contents of this book, including Figma, Adobe Creative Suite, GitHub, and more.

Through purchasing this book, you are on the path to learning new things. My hope with this book is to shift your mentality to that of a practicing musician, and the book will follow the Feynman Technique, which hopefully you will recognize.

Learning is a lifetime skill, and UX designers need this skill in abundance. As the author of this book, I hope you are willing to participate as a practitioner with me. It is a good one for UX design, and for you to become a UX design practitioner, you will need to engage in the discipline of the design process. This book is here to help improve your discipline practices.

It is organized around a practice methodology. UX designers understand the world through making stuff. Design is a learn-by-doing profession; therefore, this book is predicated on the idea that you, the reader, will make stuff, too. The book is structured into chapters that model the UX design process.

Each chapter has content that is made up of the five levels:

1.  Text and reference material: The writing, culture references, book links, people, illustrations, diagrams, and other content to extend your knowledge.

2.  Software tools: Software procedures, file sharing, and experimentation opportunities.

3.  Hands-on activities: Step-by-step instructions for practicing a specific concept, skill, or method throughout the design process.

4.  Challenges: Opportunities go outside the pages of the text and practice your skills.

5.  Reflections: After action activities, we will reflect on the success/failure of the outcome.

This book is meant to be referenced, so use it for this purpose, including dog-earing pages, writing in the margins, making notes, highlighting and underlining passages, and otherwise making the knowledge yours.

[Chapter 1: Foundations in User Experience Design](#ch01.xhtml) - In this chapter, we provide an in-depth exploration of communication systems. The first chapter lays the foundation for getting into UX and human-centered design for the reader. The reader will recap the essentials and important concepts in UX design, acquiring research, and developing strategies to solve problems in software and beyond. Additionally, in this chapter, the reader will learn about using tools such as Figma and Figjam to document their process and reflect on their learning. The chapter will also familiarize a reader with various tools they should familiarize themselves with through out the book. The reader will be exposed to the limits of what UX can and cannot do. Additionally, in this chapter, the reader will be given a warning about the motivations for pursuing this profession. The reader will be exposed to a reflection activity to unpack and quantify their existing knowledge. Finally,

the reader will be transitioned from theory to the hands-on exercises, pointing them to outside references and other giants in the UX profession.

[Chapter 2: How and Where UX Design is Useful](#ch02.xhtml) - This chapter, extends the foundation of human-centered Design for the reader, and will exposed the limits of what UX can and cannot do. Additionally, in this chapter, the reader will be given a warning about the motivations for pursuing this profession. Finally, the reader will be exposed to a reflection activity to unpack and quantify their existing knowledge.

[Chapter 3: User Experience is a Team Endeavor](#ch03.xhtml) - This chapter dovetails off Chapter 2 to extend how UX design can be useful on a team. The reader will be exposed to the companies, organizations, communities, and conversations of the UX community. Finally, the reader will take action and reflect on five companies they think express effective UX. The reader will be exposed to agile software development and how designers and developers work together to produce solutions. Finally, the reader will take action and expose their stereotypes of developers from culture.

[Chapter 4: UX Design is Human-centered System Design](#ch04.xhtml) - In this chapter, we transition from section 1 to building skills for making software. The reader will be exposed to human-centered methods as well as systems thinking. Finally, the reader will take action and reflect on their understanding of systems.

[Chapter 5: User Research Strategies](#ch05.xhtml) - This chapter builds on section 2 and continues to grow skills for making software. The reader will be exposed to human-centered methods as well as systems thinking. Finally, the reader will take action and reflect on their understanding of systems.

[Chapter 6: Systems Strategies](#ch06.xhtml) - This chapter builds on section 2 and continues to grow the reader's skills for making software. The reader will be exposed to different ways of thinking about and considering complex systems. Starting with information architecture, the user will practice organizing and labeling a hypothetical website. Furthermore, we will diagram a user path through the sitemap, and discuss the user scenario and logic structure. The reader will take action by articulating the goals of a system and illustrating the actions of a user via a user journey. The reader will be exposed to ways to think about requirements gathering. They will be exposed to considering scale and amounts. Finally, the reader will take action by practicing articulating the requirements of a familiar website.

[Chapter 7: UX Design and Failing Fast](#ch07.xhtml) - In this chapter, we open up section 3, where we build on values and grunt work designers and developers must do when making software. The reader will be exposed to ways to make as a way of knowing ethos and be introduced to the idea that a large portion of the design is about being wrong. The reader will take action by confronting their fear of failure and will dive into practice making. The reader will be exposed to ideation strategies, starting with brainstorming. The reader will have an activity to generate ideas and practice divergent thinking from writing to drawing to speaking. Finally, they will take action by analyzing their ideas and voting for them.

[Chapter 8: From 1.0 to 1.1, Prototyping First](#ch08.xhtml) - In this chapter, the reader will be exposed to paper prototyping. The reader will have an activity to generate an idea into a paper flow and quickly test this sketch through Figma. Finally, the reader will connect their screens via Figma for quicktesting.

[Chapter 9: From 1.1 to 2.0, UI Prototyping](#ch09.xhtml) - This chapter catapults the reader into making the solution look complete. The reader will be exposed to UI prototyping. Users will explore UI design systems and apply systems logic through Figma. The reader will have an activity to use material design to recreate their paper prototype with UI elements in Figma. Finally, the reader will be familiarised with design/development consistency tools like Storybook and UI best practices documentation.

[Chapter 10: Apply Usability and Accessibility](#ch10.xhtml) - This chapter catapults the reader into improving their experiences. The reader will be exposed to testing frameworks and validating solutions against accessibility and usability standards. The reader will have an activity to perform a heuristic evaluation on one of their UI screens. Finally, the reader will be exposed to the vast world of user testing tools and experiment with getting feedback.

[Chapter 11: UX, UI to Front-end or Back-end](#ch11.xhtml) - In this chapter, we review many strategies but need to see how they all come together, and this is where that happens. The reader will have an activity to recall the last time they updated an app or noticed a new product feature. This chapter aligns the reader with how a software design team is focused on making and how we get ideas into the world and get the reader into making prototypes into real products. The reader will be exposed to software project management. An activity to generate a business and UX design KPI. Finally, the reader will align a feature with the larger business and communicate its value.

[Chapter 12: UX Design and Development](#ch12.xhtml) - This chapter gets tactical, exposing the reader to the value of handing off design to developers. The reader will have an activity to practice communicating what has changed and why. Finally, the reader will adapt agile development ideas into their solution.

[Chapter 13: Iterations in Design and Development](#ch13.xhtml) - This chapter zooms out and places the skills the reader has learned into context. The reader will be exposed again to the value of UX design iteration and solving problems iteratively. They will be exposed to having to rationalize iterations in the agile lexicon. Finally, the reader will get practice in considering the time required to plan a feature.

[Chapter 14: Recap and the Future of User Experience](#ch14.xhtml) - The final chapter goes cosmic. The reader will be exposed to broader ideas and changes in technology pushing the UX industry. The reader will reflect on what they have learned and what is required of a UX designer. They will explore the role of computers and computer-generated content and practice working with a computer AI-driven assistant. As a way to wrap up and move the reader into the real world, they will end with a discussion of the limits of any profession, as well as the limits that software and computers play.

# Chapter 1: Foundations in User Experience Design

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_1a)

The opening chapter lays the foundation for getting into UX design and human-centered design. Starting with a recap of the essentials, and important concepts in UX design, acquiring research, and developing strategies to solve problems in software and beyond. Additionally, in this chapter, you will learn about using tools such as Figma and FigJam to document the design process and reflect on your learning. The chapter will also familiarize you with various tools we will use throughout the book. This chapter is dedicated to exposing the limits of what UX design can and cannot do.

Additionally, the reader will learn about some aspects of pursuing this profession sand will be exposed to a reflection activity to unpack and quantify their existing knowledge. Finally, the reader will be transitioned from theory to the hands-on exercises, pointing them to outside references and other giants in the UX design profession.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_2a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- UX design and human-centered design 101
  - Origins of human-centered design

  - Onset of UX design and digital products

- UX design learning and reflection
  - Figma software installation

  - FigJam inspiration mood board activity

- Review of the UX design design giants
  - UX design is not a quick paycheck

  - UX design is not a short win

  - UX design is not only for tech

  - UX design is continuous learning

- Likert skill scale via FigJam activity

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_3a)

After completing this chapter, you will have a grounded understanding of the forces that have birthed UX design and human-centered design. We will also gain basic proficiency in using Figma for a hands-on reflection and recall a few meaningful people in the profession.

| |

|----|

| Note: The number of designer roles is changing and includes roles such as User Researcher, UX designer, UI designer, IxD, product design, prototyper, Service designer, front-end developer, UX engineer, Design/DevOps, or any emerging roles like UX/AI, or AR/VR UX, etc. Try and keep up but know that we all share similar skills. |

[UX design and human-centered design 101](#contents.xhtml_sc2_4a)

Designers have existed for as long as humans have been around, and Homo Sapiens have existed for over 300,000 years. We will mostly focus on the last 50 years. Which has been responsible for: The personal computer, laptop computer, the internet, the smartphone, the smartwatch, and so much more. Some radical changes have occurred in that time and much of it has been made possible through the following three factors:

- Humans/designers

- Technology

- Resources/marketplaces

There is a proverb, that says, Necessity is the mother of invention.

The idea of this proverb is rooted in Plato’s quote, Our need will be the real creator, but over nearly 3 centuries, the language has changed, but the idea remains true:

![A person with a beard pointing up Description automatically generated](images/Figure_1.1.png)

Figure 1.1: Plato with his original quote, “Our need will be the real creator”

To this day, it is a very human concept. Humans have needs. In the realm of human existence, various needs and dependencies shape our daily lives. The pursuit of knowledge, evident in your selection of this book, exemplifies one such fundamental human aspiration. Another elemental requirement is quenching one's thirst, a biological imperative.

In this modern era, the mechanisms facilitating the satisfaction of such basic needs have evolved into intricate systems, all meticulously designed and orchestrated by human ingenuity. Consider the simple act of obtaining a glass of water: the vessel itself, the plumbing system connecting it to the kitchen sink, and even the larger municipal water supply infrastructure are all human creations. This intricate web of systems serves a singular purpose—to fulfill the fundamental human need for water.

The importance of water to our existence cannot be overstated, as its absence would inevitably lead to dire consequences. This underscores the significance of the software-driven advancements that underpin many of these essential systems, a testament to our progress in the 21st century.

Nevertheless, there are many more human needs than just water and it is recommended that you keep digging thoroughly into human needs starting with Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs:

![A pyramid of needs with text and images Description automatically generated with medium confidence](images/Figure_1.2.png)

Figure 1.2: Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs triangle diagram

| |

|----|

| Note: Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs American Psychologist Abraham Maslow (1908-1970). Sometimes referred to as the Triangle of Needs. His theory is a great foundation is human needs and an example of the power of a simple graphic. |

The needs we have as humans reflect a few things:

- Our community (where we live, who we interact with, how we participate, etc.)

- What systems do we participate in (what technology do we use, what training do we have, etc.)

- What beliefs do we hold (where we live, our education, our government, etc.)

As designers we should be particularly attentive to our own needs but more importantly the needs of our users. This concept is known as human-centered design it is the idea that a design process should focus on the people it ultimately will serve. Designers who apply human-centered methodologies are vehicles for learning about the human-centered cycle, as design is a learn-by-doing profession. You will make a lot of mistakes, but designers must always learn and grow, even in the face of failure. To be human is to fail and if designers can embrace this, we will ultimately be more human-centered.

[Origins of human-centered design](#contents.xhtml_sc3_5a)

The timeline of human-centered thinking, shown in the following figure, can be traced way back to the beginning of democracy:

![A timeline of a timeline of information Description automatically generated with medium confidence](images/Figure_1.3.png)

Figure 1.3: Human-centered/UX timeline

The timeline of human-centered thinking can be traced way back to the beginning of democracy.

In Greece, Plato sought community input in the design of an equitable city-state and forever changed the world through democratic values. Democracy and human-centered design share a participatory methodology that is predicated on collaboration and debate. In the world of design, we call debate a crit short for critique which when done well is a way for ideas to be refined via a community. We will learn about the design process later, but a key aspect of design is that designers seek feedback. Fast forward to the start of the 20th century. As civilizations emerged and perished over time, with the Dark Ages over, humans have cultivated their ability to travel via trains, and cars, and have even started to fly. Mechanical advancements and human ingenuity have always propelled us forward.

World War I had just ended, and humanity had experienced a terrible combination of trench warfare and advances in technology both mechanical (the machine gun, flame thrower, etc.) and chemical (mustard gas, etc.) Those advances were created in the act of eliminating humanity rather than allowing it to thrive. Far too many soldiers died in the tragic conflict of WWI, and when it ended, many shifts in society were primed by the horrors of trench warfare as well as the advances in technology. A concept that was primed to thrive out of the destruction of war was modernism. Modernism is an art, design, and literature movement that is characterized by freedom of thought, human expression, and a break from classical forms and traditions. In 1918-1933 an art school was birthed in Germany—The Bauhaus. The Bauhaus was founded by Architect Walter Gropius and was founded on the idea that all art can be brought together. Gesamtkunstwerk is German for comprehensive artwork and it was one of their founding concepts. The Bauhaus combined industrial production methods, and modern artistic ideas that include form-follows-function, designer as craftsperson, and usefulness as beauty which birthed an entire movement that continues today.

[TABLE]

Any great school or movement is carried forward by its followers and many modernist thinkers. The legendary instructors called The Bauhaus Home included Paul Klee, Wassily Kandisky, László Moholy-Nagy, Josef Albers, Johannes Itten, Ludwig Mies van der Rohe, and more. The Bauhaus was disbanded by the Nazis in 1933 during the runup to World War II and along with most of Europe, many great modernist thinkers and designers emigrated to the United States. The movement of people carried forth the ideas and ideals of the Bauhaus ethos to a place where it flourished in the coming years. The sharing of ideas and more importantly, the movement of people laid the groundwork for the advancement of human-centered design in both industrial design, and across industries such as architecture, film, literature, and graphic design.

In the 1950s and 1960s, Henry Dreyfuss and his associates applied human-scale principles to industrial products including some universal products like hand-held telephones for Western Electric and the Bell System of utility providers. His design products were so successful they even got their own USPS stamp to commemorate his contribution:

![A stamp with a picture of a telephone Description automatically generated](images/Figure_1.4.png)

Figure 1.4: Henry Dreyfuss telephone from 2011 USPS stamp

Henry Dreyfuss wrote Designing for People—[https://a.co/d/1il1Qte](https://a.co/d/1il1Qte) where he introduced designers to Joe and Josephine his anthropometric characters that define human scale measurements for thinking about how people might function. His illustrations were an expression of his aesthetic and ethical approaches to design principles. Designers to this day apply his practices and reflect his service and commitment to design responsibility. Designers to this day refer to users as Joe and Josephine as an insider call back to Mr. Dreyfuss’s models:

![A drawing of two people Description automatically generated](images/Image50807.png)

Figure 1.5: Henry Dreyfuss Josephine and Joe industrial human measurements

To start with users, when solving problems at the time was novel, he impacted many of his contemporaries in graphic design and beyond including Ray and Charles Eames, Dieter Rams, Paul Rand, Massimo Vignelli, Buckminster Fuller, and so many more.

| |

|----|

| Note: If you are not familiar with any of these names, it is recommended that you learn about them, as they have all made a significant impact on the field of design in their respective disciplines. As this is not a comprehensive list by any means, it is encouraged that you learn more as you go. |

The connective tissue between the work of the 1950s and teaching from design schools, HCI programs, and computer science (CS) continues to resonate into human-centered thinking and bleeds over into other emerging technologies that have been made possible through the advancement in computing power.

[Onset of UX design and digital products](#contents.xhtml_sc3_6a)

In the 1970s and 1980s, computers started to become more useful and relatively more cost-effective, especially on college campuses. Collages started to create CS departments and home-computing enthusiasts started popping up in groups in places like Palo Alto, CA where Stanford University is located. The personal computer started becoming an idea and research groups started to accelerate the ideas of building software systems for everyday people rather than just the engineers who have used them in the past.

Companies like Xerox are helping drive businesses with their successful photocopy machines and setting up Xerox Palo Alto Research Center (PARC). A technology research center that was set up to explore new information technologies and employed some of the pioneers of the personal computer and graphical user interfaces (GUI) including Alan Kay, Mark Weiser, Bill Buxton, Larry Tesler, and many more.

| |

|----|

| Note: If you are not familiar with any of these names it is recommended that you learn about them as they have all made significant impacts on the field of design, computer science, and interaction design in their respective disciplines. |

Xerox PARC is well known for being a source of inspiration for Steve Jobs and Steve Wozniac during their development of the Apple Macintosh personal computer. Steve Jobs demoed a prototype of the Xerox Star 8010 Information System circa 1981 which used a window and GUI paradigm. As a result, Steve promptly appropriated and understood the GUI as the future for all computers. Ultimately, Apple became a massive success and the macOS applies this GUI paradigm which remains a cornerstone of their user experience to this day.

| |

|----|

| Note: If you are curious about the birth of the computer and the internet, it is recommended that you watch- Lo and behold- Reveries of the Connected World by Werner Herzog 2016. [https://youtu.be/q3g3hqNJqpQ?si=5SaKaRPz_6wyM_ud](https://youtu.be/q3g3hqNJqpQ?si=5SaKaRPz_6wyM_ud) |

Human-centered design is the coalescence of several academic fields, including engineering, psychology, anthropology, human factors, graphic design, and the arts. In the 1990s, personal computers started to become more mobile with laptops and personal digital assistants (PDAs) becoming more popular. With the explosion in technology usage as well as the beginning of the internet brought on by Tim Berners Lee in 1989 from the CERN laboratories, the world of software design, and product design, became more aligned. IDEO was founded in 1991 by David Kelley, Bill Moggridge, and Mike Nuttal where they started to evangelize the virtues of human-centered design thinking at the core of their product design consultant agency.

IDEO started building things humans need to use and took off from the pioneering work of Douglas Engelbart, who helped invent Apple’s first one-button mouse. IDEO would go on to work with thousands of companies and would help establish the d.school at Stanford University. A non-degree program dedicated to training design thinking and human-centered design ethos in students from various degree backgrounds. IDEO, the dschool, and countless others have started teaching the foundations of design thinking, which is at the heart of human-centered design:

![A diagram of a design thinking Description automatically generated](images/Figure_1.6.png)

Figure 1.6: Design thinking steps and Double Diamond steps

Design thinking is a framework made up of five steps:

1. Empathy

2. Define

3. Ideate

4. Prototype

5. Test

Although visualized and described as five linear steps, in practice, design thinking is a series of human-centered cycles that constantly fold back on each other and educate designers through the act of participating in the process. Design thinking is sometimes visualized as the Double Diamond which also describes the process in four D’s:

1. Discover

2. Define

3. Develop

4. Deliver

These visualizations use different words but attempt to describe the same design process.

| |

|----|

| Note: We will be practicing human-centered design thinking methodologies throughout this book so be mindful of the steps and attempt to commit them to your memory. |

The success of new technology and the rapid improvements in silicon processing power have led to the rapid adoption of computers and computer systems in our everyday lives. The design thinking pedagogy has been adopted by countless university systems and the number of design firms demonstrating the application of design thinking methodologies is equally as wide and deep. Most notable is IBM, which has applied design thinking strategies from the C-suite down and shifted its company culture to align all its teams around using design thinking methods. This is particularly notable as IBM was a dominating force in the 1950-1980s computer mainframe business and was known to be the primary engineering lead. Nevertheless, IBM still has several engineers. However, the difference is that they now hire and train their engineers and designers to use human-centered methods.

The reality is software design and development are fundamentally a team effort and if all members have similar training in how to solve problems through design thinking, the whole team can move faster. This is not only because they share a common set of tools and methods but because they practice the process and have found success for their businesses’ bottom line. After all, their CEO, Thomas J. Watson (IBM President, CEO, and Chairman of the Board 1952-1971), once say: Good design is good business.

Human-centered design values sharing goals for users and business which leads to more success. Keeping team members efficient, effective, and focused on their users breeds more satisfied users. IBM and other companies did not just suddenly change, it took the industry and their corporate practices, years to turn over a new leaf.

[TABLE]

In the 1990s many shifts brought on using personal computers and software were being worked on and being experienced by the masses who had flocked to improve their jobs, lives, and personal experiences through technology. Donald Norman was pushing human-centered design forward. In 1988, he wrote, The Psychology of Everyday Things, which became The Design of Everyday Things, a best-selling book that popularized the term human-centered design.

| |

|----|

| Note: The Design of Everyday Things is a book every designer should read. It is required reading. If you have not read his book, I would recommend putting this book down and picking that one up first. |

Donald Norman to many is the godfather of UX design, and his writing and consultancy group, the NNG, which was founded between Jakob Nielsen and him, has been pushing the relevance and importance of human-centered design alongside the importance of usability. Donald Noman is currently trying to expand the human-centered design paradigm into humanity-centered design, however we will leave that for you to explore on your own. Human-centered design and design thinking are popular design strategies and will continue to be taught and trained by designers from Tempe to Timbuktu as well as throughout this book.

The impact of human-centered design has only grown over time. In the 2000s, we were introduced to scalable mobile technology and the rapid adoption of internet-enabled devices known as the Internet of Things (IoT). In 2007, the first iPhone was released and with it a shift to apps-based user experiences and mobile-first interactions. All these new devices and improvements in processing power, technology size, product formats, as well as demand from global audiences have placed acute importance on solving the right problems with the right tools. Human-centered design has had a huge part in propagating and supporting all these changes in software technology as well as feeding the unrelenting needs of users around the globe.

[UX design learning and reflection](#contents.xhtml_sc2_7a)

As we just discussed, human-centered design thinking is a series of cycles. Designers learn by doing. However, learning does not occur in the act of doing, but rather in the reflection on the doing, therefore we will practice what we preach, but first let us all get started by using similar tools.

[Figma software installation](#contents.xhtml_sc3_8a)

1. Go to [https://www.figma.com/](https://www.figma.com/):

3. Go to: [https://www.figma.com/education/](https://www.figma.com/education/) and get verified for education access.

a. Figma can be downloaded from the following link: [https://www.figma.com/downloads/](https://www.figma.com/downloads/) as shown in [Figure 1.7](#ch01.xhtml_fig1-7):

[Review of the UX design giants](#contents.xhtml_sc2_10a)

As we have been discussing the history of UX design and human-centered design, there are many people involved in pushing the UX design profession forward. One important thing to remember is that as you read this book you have the luxury of standing on the shoulders of others that have come before you. Should you familiarize yourself with meaningful characters and thinkers in UX design? Absolutely. The following is by no means a comprehensive list of all the thinkers, however, we must understand and learn from their achievements. We get the luxury of standing on the shoulders of professionals. thinkers, authors, and educators who paved a path so that we can go further: The following figure includes Dieter Rams (Product designer), Bill Buxton (Interaction designer), Donald Norman (UX designer), Kim Goodwin (UX designer), Alan Kay (Computer scientist), Breda Laurel (User researcher).

![A group of people standing under a sign Description automatically generated](images/Figure_1.9.png)

Figure 1.9: Go farther on the knowledge of others

Let us look at some important figures in Design:

- Donald Norman (NNG/author/educator): The godfather of UX, author, and lecturer.

- Bill Buxton (Microsoft/prototype evangelist/author): The godfather of interaction design.

- Alan Cooper (Cooper design/author): Father of visual basic, about face—the essentials of interaction design.

- Alan Kay (Xerox PARC/computer scientist/object-oriented programming): Graphical user interface (GUI) pioneer.

- Mark Weiser (Xerox PARC/computer scientist/ubiquitous computing (ubicomp)): Pioneer in ubiquitous computing. The computer and the 21st century.

- Dieter Rams (Braun design/product design/minimalist): 100s of minimalist product design and 10 Principles of Good Design.

- Kim Goodwin (Cooper design/author): Author of Designing for the Digital Age, VP of Design at Cooper.

- Steve Krug (Usability expert/author/speaker): Don’t Make Me Think author/usability specialist.

- Sarah Doody (UX career strategist): UX case studies and UX strategy.

- Brenda Laurel (User researcher/game design/author): Game interaction designer and researcher.

- Jared Spool (User interface engineering/usability expert/speaker): Usability advocate, curriculum developer, and consultant.

- Laurel Klein (UX designer/author): Author of Build Better Products. Lean UX for Startups.

- Aarron Walter (Invision design/author/speaker): Design better.co blog and podcast.

- Luke Wroblewski (UX and UI design courses/usability/author): Google Product designer/UX curriculum/usability advocate)

- Andy Budd (Author/founder/speaker): UX writing, speaking, etc.

- Mike Monteiro (Author/founder/speaker): Author of Ruined by Design, storyteller.

- Brad Frost (FE engineer/author/founder/speaker): Author of book Atomic Design about thinking about web systems and responsive websites akin to the scientific atomic model.:

  | |

  |----|

  | Note: Follow these designers via X (formerly Twitter) and LinkedIn and read what they have written, listen to what they talk about, and follow their contemporaries. There are many others, but this is a good foundation. |

The UX design community is a large group, and it is growing bigger every day. You are encouraged to dive into the other thinkers in this space and increase your list to be much bigger than we just covered. In some corners of the internet and the UX design community you will find the gatekeeper mentality. Those who want to quantify, institutionalize, and certify UX design knowledge to their benefit. Those that attempt to reduce supply to increase demand. Many in the gatekeeper mentality act threatened by the newcomers and feel they can deter people from pursuing their careers in UX design. We need to fervently believe that the UX design pie can continue to grow and that the world would be better off if we were all UX designers. If we were all designers, then it would be easier to help people and share our experiences collectively. However, just keep learning, stay curious, and focus on improving your thinking about users every day and you will improve.

[UX design is not a quick paycheck](#contents.xhtml_sc3_11a)

The UX design community is large and growing bigger every day and there are a lot of resources available, many of them unfortunately are selling a pipe dream. The illusion that you too can learn fast and earn more. If your rationale for learning UX design methodologies is purely economic, it is recommended to turn back. There are much better ways to make money and many other professions that draw in people who are financially driven. UX design is hard and the problems you work on will keep you up at night. If you work hard, show value for users, and improve the effectiveness of businesses, then with it, financial rewards are achieved as well. Throughout this book, we will keep pressing on the idea that designers should understand the value they drive both for their users and their businesses. Hopefully, with this understanding, you can align your compensation closer to the value you can support.

[UX design is not a short win](#contents.xhtml_sc3_12a)

Just as UX design is not a quick paycheck, it is also not a short win. We must do the work. There are UX designers who have been doing this job longer than most who continue to improve their understanding and are constantly pushing themselves to learn more. You will get better in 5/10/15 years, but as we have stated before, there is no mountain top in UX design. If you think we can just read this book and start calling ourselves a UX designer. It is also recommended that we second guess that approach and rather look at this as a pursuit of UX design. Like a wolf who travels 1000s of miles in its lifetime, a UX designer will do the same and learn the skills that will sustain that journey. Today, you learn from this book and tomorrow, you learn form a designer you were introduced to in the previous topic. The next week you experiment with a new software. The pursuit is what makes this profession fascinating, and we want designers who are willing to go the distance not only for their users but also for themselves and the betterment of the world.

[UX design is not only for tech](#contents.xhtml_sc3_13a)

If you are on the journey and fueled up for the long haul, then also know that UX design and human-centered design have applications reaching far beyond technology and software development. One of the reasons UX design has been so valuable is that technology systems and the internet are the biggest drivers in commerce, services, and communication around the globe. Like moths to a flame, we flock to where the need for our skills is the greatest, however, a beautiful thing about HCD/UX design is that it can be applied to a much wider set of industries than just technology or software companies. The reality is that nearly every industry from construction to industrial manufacturing can benefit from working human-centered, whether that be fixing difficult crew scheduling to dangerous machine procedures. The reason the d.school at Stanford is teaching design thinking to every department is the same reason every industry and business has started to employ UX designers. The opportunity to reach your users is too rich and the risk of not addressing your customers’ needs through human-centered design is too high.

Ultimately, the goal of UX design is to help people, and if you start with people and their needs sometimes these can be serviced directly by a technology, like a phone or a computer, other times they are served indirectly by a technology. Whether that be getting food at a market or having their garbage removed each week. A fascinating thing about UX design is that it allows designers to consider the systems and how some systems are 1-to-1 while other systems create a mesh effect. Tech is a big system and has an impact on all the other systems it intersects with. Due to this, UX design can focus on the intersection of technology, user, and business and when done well can be an invention and innovation driver as well as a method for reducing pain for each factor.

[UX is continuous learning](#contents.xhtml_sc3_14a)

If you are on the journey and fueled up for the long hall, then also know that UX design and human-centered design have applications reaching far beyond technology and software development. One of the reasons UX design has been so valuable is that technology systems and the internet are the biggest drivers of commerce, services, and communication in our world. In only 34 years the internet and countless devices that rely on its connection have fundamentally fueled every step. Billions of people carry essentially supercomputers around in their pockets, and that has both helped tremendously but also created its own set of issues. People are fascinating creatures; we adapt quickly to change as well as reflect on our impact, whether that be locally or globally. The scale of the impact the internet and technology have had is enough to make your head spin, but also opens many opportunities. All these changes and all these opportunities require that designers continue to learn and adapt to change. We are on the back side of a global pandemic, at the crux of sentient AI, and able to move around the planet with greater ease and convenience than at any other time in history.

[Likert skill scale via FigJam activity](#contents.xhtml_sc2_15a)

Let us now reflect on and assess our starting place via FigJam:

Total time: 30 minutes to 1 hour

1. Go to FigJam Community File:

2. Make a copy:

a. Assess your skills.

3. Add a date to the top.

4. Score each of the skills included in the FigJam Board.

5. Write a short set of goals under each skill.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_16a)

UX design and HCD have a rich and vibrant origin that sits shotgun next to technology and changes to computers and the internet. By keeping aware of the changes that occurred in the past, we can attempt to anticipate the changes that will come. The role of the UX designer is to adapt to change and use the skills of human-centered design to align the interests of users, businesses, and technology.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_17a)

- UX design and HCI history are connected to the past and continue to grow. The UX design community is large and growing bigger every day. Sharing knowledge and learning from other UX design pioneers will improve your learning.

- UX design and HCI are worth learning over a long period and will require a continuous learning mindset.

# Chapter 2: How and Where UX Design is Useful

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_18a)

Moving forward with the information from the previous chapter, we will extend the foundation of human-centered design and expose the limits of what user experience (UX) can and cannot do. We cover a few key samples of where UX has been useful and will unpack and analyze our own experiences with computer software.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_19a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- UX design in our world
  - Silicon Valley, California and USA

- Business types
  - Buisness-to-government

  - Buisness-to-buisness

  - Buisness-to-customer

  - Customer-to-customer

  - Figma FigJam business model research and inspiration activity

- UX design limits

- Common goal and common language
  - UX Design Lexicon Figma or Figjam Activity

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_20a)

After completing this chapter, you should be familiar with the systems that use UX design. You will be able to recall business models as you analyse your own interest in companies and practice the language and jargon of the industries we use regularly.

[UX design in our world](#contents.xhtml_sc2_21a)

Can UX designers solve all the world’s problems? No. However, our world is designed, and UX designers have had a part in making it the way it is. On the face of it, this statement might sound pessimistic as our world still has famine, war, inequality, disease, injustice, and more. Are UX designers responsible for these factors? Unfortunately, the answer is maybe. On the other hand, UX designers have a unique opportunity to use their human-centered skills to combat the pessimistic factors that have been listed previously. UX design must be an optimistic profession; we must believe we can make the systems better. If we do not believe our world can improve, it will lead to stagnation. Are UX designers the only ones responsible for making improvements? Absolutely not!

As we have been discussing in the brief history of UX design and human-centered design (HCD), the world is complex and changes all the time. UX designers have put themselves on the path of untangling and learning from all that change. UX designers continuously learn as a coping mechanism to constant change. This is because their jobs are connected to solving problems brought on by that change.

However, it should be a comforting fact that other people besides UX designers are also committed to improving our world, but it is recommended to seek out companies, teams, and communities that share that point of view.

UX design exists in many places around the globe and hopefully, you are reading this book because wherever you are, you have seen the need for UX design and HCD thinking. UX design was once regionalized, where certain cities cultivated more UX designers than others. Now that our world is hyper-connected through the internet, the UX design community has expanded with less regionality associated with the profession. However, the history of software development and the need for UX designers keep some locations like Silicon Valley, California, USA, with a higher population of UX design and HCD practitioners than others. However, that does not mean UX design and design talent are not available in other places. Let us discuss the origins of UX design, and it might help make sense of why UX design communities thrive where they do.

[Silicon Valley, California and USA](#contents.xhtml_sc3_22a)

Most UX design and HCD thinking have their origins in the places where software companies started or have been concentrated because this is where those skills are needed most. Silicon Valley, the collection of cities from San Francisco, CA, USA and south to San Jose, CA, USA continues to be home to Apple, Xerox, Adobe, Google, Salesforce, Airbnb, Uber, Meta, LinkedIn, PayPal, Zoom, HP, and other large software companies as wells as countless other start-ups and Software as a Service (SaaS) or Application companies.

| |

|----|

| Note: UX is a significant business investment, and you should look to join companies that see the value and are dedicated to growing their UX/HCD thinking ranks. Try not to join companies that just dabble in UX or that keep designers at arm’s length. We will discuss this later. |

The reality is Silicon Valley has possibly the highest concentration of UX designers on the planet. This data is not backed by any quantitative numbers, but based on the size of the companies that have been listed above, we could say that it is a pretty good guess. Whether or not Silicon Valley continues to remain a hub for UX designers in the future, is entirely up to the success of our skills and the global need for software. Due to the scalability of remote work and changes in geo-political investment opportunities for UX design, work is popping up in areas way beyond the Silicon Valley bubble. If you are in Silicon Valley, do not fear, there is still plenty of software to work on and the large corporations who have sustained the UX design innovations are not going anywhere. Silicon Valley has a huge incentive to keep innovating and cannot rest, otherwise they risk the chance to be overtaken by leaner, better software companies and products. Moreover, as we have been discussing, the world changes too fast for any of them to sit on their hands. At one time, Silicon Valley was where the world went for talent, because of the concentration of companies that were utilizing UX design, were established there. There is still a lot of talent in Silicon Valley, however, the world has embraced technology and UX designers can be found all over the world.

[Business types](#contents.xhtml_sc2_23a)

Although the location can have an impact on the volume of opportunity in UX design, what can be useful is to understand the types of businesses in the world that need UX design and HCD thinking. We can then translate how UX design is valuable for that type of business model. An important factor in being a UX designer in the first place is understanding business systems. A goal of every UX designer should be to be able to impact business decisions and to do this, we should become well-versed in business models and know what is important for our businesses. We should be able to translate what is meaningful to our users and why user-centered design impacts business success from quarter to quarter or year to year. UX designers can work for lots of different business models and UX design can be applied in any. A business model is a representation of how an organization plans to generate revenue, so let us review the different types of business models (refer to the following figure):

![A diagram of a business model Description automatically generated](images/Figure_2.1.png)

Figure 2.1: Business model spectrum

[Business-to-government](#contents.xhtml_sc4_24a)

A business model where businesses do most of their revenue for a Government, Military, or Administration, at times called Business-to-Administration (B2A). B2G businesses compete for government contracts and assist the government in regulation, government services, government-related systems, and more. For example, the body scanning technology products used by the Transportation Security Administration (TSA) in the United States at international and domestic airports as well as other airports around the world. While the TSA defines and manages regulations and operates these technologies at airports, it does not design or manufacture the technology itself. A B2G business supplies this technology, and they are supposed to keep our traveling process safe and secure. If you have ever traveled on an airplane, you may already know how much opportunity there is to improve the travel process. The multitude of software systems involved in transporting millions of passengers globally presents numerous opportunities for enhancing UX design within government operations and their design systems.

[Business-to-business](#contents.xhtml_sc4_25a)

A business that does its primary revenue for another business. These business models are seen more in the supply chain process where one business will sell raw material to another to be used by the second business during the manufacturing process. There are 1000’s of B2B companies and depending on how complicated the manufacturing is, it can create marketplaces for B2B to specialize. Take Dell Computers for example, they work with vendors that create their printed circuit boards (PCBs). How can UX design be involved in this type of business? Where complexity exists UX designers are there to thrive. UX designers can help improve a business’s processes through human-centered design research, implementing ordering/inventory user interfaces, adding empathy to the manufacturing processes, and so much more. UX design can also improve any other business-to-business need. Sometimes business-to-business tools do not get the UX design attention they deserve however all software tools are used by people and therefore they can be improved. B2B businesses tend to focus on 3 categories: products, services, and software. At some point, software is involved in making a business work because that allows people to accomplish tasks for the business more efficiently. Hence, everywhere where people are accomplishing tasks through software, a UX designer should be used.

[Business-to-customer](#contents.xhtml_sc4_26a)

A business model that does its primary revenue selling products and/or services directly to the people (end-users) of the product or service. For example, any brick-and-mortar retail business or online e-commerce shop that sells products directly to the buyers like hair salons, banks, or clothing stores. UX designers can help these businesses as our role is to advocate for users (customers which are, people). B2C businesses are very diverse and focus on products and services. Many times, software is used to drive B2C transactions. For example, e-commerce businesses using Shopify are engaging with them via B2B business exchange, however, the websites using Shopify and offering goods or services to customers are in a B2C model. One supports the other. B2C businesses must create value for their users, capture their attention, and deliver a product or service better than their competitors. Simple, right?

Well, there are libraries full of books on how to make B2C businesses a success. One clear-cut way to improve a B2C business is by applying HCD and employing UX designers who are dedicated to customer experience. Some UX designers even specialize to the point where they define themselves as customer experience (CX) designers. CX design is typically a strategy-based position, but there is a lot in common skill-wise between a UX designer and a CX designer.

The titles we give UX designers are highly likely to change over time, but the underlying human-centered design skills will continue to underpin whatever we call ourselves. A key thing to recognize is businesses and their models are also in flux. Any business needs employees who can address change, drive impact, and improve revenue; Therefore, any business needs UX design to capture these opportunities.

[Customer-to-customer](#contents.xhtml_sc4_27a)

A business model where consumers sell goods or services to other consumers mostly via online marketplaces. C2C businesses use third-party companies to facilitate transactions between private consumers. For example, eBay, Etsy, Facebook Marketplace, and Craigslist are all facilitating C2C interactions. C2C businesses emerged with e-commerce technology and the sharing economy. For example, with businesses, UX design can help improve software systems that facilitate and manage the process. Whether it be creating a trust to sell your old couch to a stranger or scheduling an exchange of things in a well-lit parking lot. UX design and HCD can improve the systems, tools, communication expectations, and more that we require to maintain and support the success of the marketplace. When a customer is the party managing a software platform, the role UX design can play is huge, not only for improving time on the platform, but also in addressing the unique needs of customers using these C2C platforms.

| |

|----|

| Note: This is not the limit of business model types or a comprehensive review of all the types of products and services each of these business models can produce. However, having an idea of where you might want to apply your UX design skills can be helpful in honing and focusing your systems experience. |

[Figma FigJam business model research and inspiration activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_28a)

Let us now do some research on businesses and reflect on business models we might consider working for:

Total time: 20 min per business model

1. Create a new Figma FigJam board.

2. Label the board business interests:

a. Add a table (Shift + T) 2x6.

b. In the top cell of column one (1x6), add the business model that you find most interesting: G2B, B2B, B2C, C2C, or other, you have researched.

c. In the following cells, add five business names you think would be interesting to work for.

i. Criteria: Business has a good reputation, appears to have a good user experience, business is growing, business is human-centered, business is good for the planet, etc.

d. In the tabletop of column two (2x6), add the Job Ops URL for the job opening. Following add five URLs for job openings for the corresponding business in the row:

i. Job opportunities typically live in the footer of business websites under labels like Careers, Work for us, or Join our team.

3. Repeat the table for other business models:

a. Flag the business or industries you would be excited to work for.

b. List businesses you do not want to work for. Make a separate table for these businesses.

| |

|----|

| Note: You will be revisiting this list later so be diligent and make a good list. |

[UX design limits](#contents.xhtml_sc2_29a)

While UX design and HCD are incredibly versatile and beneficial, they are not universal solutions that fit every business model seamlessly. UX design and HCD are very useful, but that is not a solution. Furthermore, UX design thrives on collaboration. It is a team effort, not a hierarchical or isolated endeavor.

Can we apply our skills to a team? Absolutely, but we cannot cure cancer, engineer a bridge, architect a building, or produce a microchip, those are all skills and products that rely on entirely different professions and systems that may or may not use UX design. Can HCD be applied to every profession, yes! Is it? No. There are certain industries in which UX design is very applicable and software development is one of those. Our skills and experience can quickly be applied to software problems like understanding our users’ needs, user interfaces, or applying new technologies.

| |

|----|

| Note: Avoid the danger of an over-inflated sense of worth. UX design is fundamentally a team effort, and you should see your role as symbiotic to your team vs hierarchical or worse parasitic. |

As we have been discussing, UX design is a part of the puzzle that makes a business successful. However, UX design alone is not responsible for success. We should be glad of this fact as that is a lot of pressure both from the business and on the designers. There are many factors that are associated with running a successful business, and hopefully, UX design is one of those investments.

UX designers should not be afraid to quantify their work. We should provide proof through data to our businesses on how our solution is helping our users and impacting business objectives. UX designers can and should be able to translate their business value, but should also be involved in defining what success for the business looks like. The limits of how UX design can be involved with business come down to how much a business cares about employing human-centered design. One way to see how much a business is dedicated to design is to look at the C-suite roles at the business. The presence of roles like Chief Design Officer (CDO) or Chief Experience Officer (CXO) can indicate a business’s commitment to UX. However, the absence of such roles, as seen in companies like Apple, does not necessarily mean a lack of UX design maturity. Jonathan Ive occupied the CDO role previously and many have speculated on why Apple has not been able to fill his shoes since he stepped down in 2019. UX-related C-suite roles are not universally applied at businesses, either, which makes this UX design maturity benchmark even more complicated, even if the business utilizes UX design heavily. Of course, this might change in the future, but part of the limits of what makes UX design successful at a business is the lack of UX-based executives. UX design requires investment from a business, and if UX designers are not on the boards of businesses or in the C-suite or able to be CEOs of software companies, then UX design will continue to be underutilized and underinvested in.

Furthermore, what is limiting UX design is purely a lack of understanding by businesses looking for talent. For example, I have seen UX Engineer roles pop up in job postings, and it is a red flag for a business. A role of this type is looking to combine two different roles in the hope that they can pay one person a little less than paying two people. Not that an engineer is not able to demonstrate UX design skills, but the responsibilities of a UX designer and an engineer are very broad and very different. Roles like this make me think the business has no idea what either role is or what their specialty can bring to the team. Unfortunately, many businesses are under-investing in UX design and engineering talent to the detriment of their software products and ultimately their users. It is incumbent on designers to shift this reality, and essential for new UX designers to find and grow their UX design careers wherever they are employed. Not every business is going to wake up tomorrow and realize they need UX design, and even if they did, not every business knows how UX design, or HCD, can be used effectively. UX design is often underutilized due to a lack of understanding by businesses. Misconceptions about UX design roles, such as combining UX design and engineering into one, highlight this issue. However, this gap presents opportunities for designers to educate and advocate for proper UX design integration, which should fill you with promise but also know and be mindful of joining businesses that need a lot of UX design handholding. New UX designers should avoid being the sole UX design professional in a company. They should seek environments where they can learn from experienced designers and grow as part of a team. Remember UX design is a learning profession, so look for roles that allow you to learn. If a business expects you to know everything about UX design before you can be hired, that is a red flag. As we have been discussing, there is no way to reach the UX design mountain top, so avoid that trap.

Here is a secret, no one has it all figured out. No business is perfect, and no design team is Shangri-La, and as a UX designer, you can help teams figure out their problems so look for teams that let you help and will in turn let you grow. Choose a place that will allow you to make mistakes and learn. Before you can find your first UX design job, you will need to get up to speed and learn to talk the talk and walk the walk. Like any profession, there are limits, but also room for growth, but to achieve that growth, you must start to learn the UX design language.

[Common goals and common language](#contents.xhtml_sc2_30a)

As we just discussed UX design, and HCD thinking is a series of cycles that are practiced by UX designers and teams. Alongside the design process is a type of language we all use to keep it going. UX designers learn the design process over time, and learn to communicate about design which produces jargon:

![A cartoon of a person with a speech bubble above his head Description automatically generated](images/Figure_2.2.png)

Figure 2.2: UX/HCD Jargon

Jargon is a special set of words or expressions that are used by a specific profession or group and are often difficult to understand or place in context if you are an outsider.

[TABLE]

Jargon is highly useful inside a group but needs to be learned and unlearned as a participant who moves between groups.

Some UX jargon examples are:

- Wireframes

- Mockups

- Prototypes

- User interface (UI), or graphical user interface (GUI)

- Call to action (CTA)

- Responsive web design (RWD)

- Brand book

- A/B testing

UX design jargon is interconnected with the jargon of technology, the jargon of development, the jargon of product design, and other business jargon.

Some tech jargon examples are:

- Application programming interface (API)

- Bring your own device (BYOD)

- What you see is what you get (WYSIWYG)

- Business intelligence (BI)

- Content management system (CMS)

- Cloud computing (RWD)

- Bug

- Object-oriented programming (OOP)

Some business jargon examples are:

- Low-hanging fruit

- Core competencies

- Wheelhouse

- Synergy

- Bandwidth

- Thought leader

- Buy-in

- Bleeding-edge

- Much more

To do the job well, you will need to be able to navigate all these communities and all the jargon. At this point, you have already started to be exposed to the UX/HCD jargon by reading up to this point. By joining the UX design community, you will be expected to familiarize yourself both with the skills of the profession, but also the jargon. You will be expected to be able to join a conversation among a group of UX designers and hold your own. To do this, you need to help yourself with the jargon and practice. Practice will require time but also recollection, which is where our activities along with the remainder of this book should help.

An important thing about learning jargon is memorizing jargon is not an end goal. On the other hand, you will need to learn jargon to ultimately be able to unpack and communicate what the jargon means back to clients or teams that are not in the UX design group. The goal should be to not use jargon with non-designers. Jargon is a strange, because we need to learn it to be able to navigate and work within a group, but to be successful, we need to not use Jargon otherwise we risk isolating ourselves and our audiences. Hence, learn it and be mindful of how every organization, every business, and every profession uses jargon.

[UX Design Lexicon Figma or FigJam activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_31a)

Let us now help you with your UX deisgn language via Figma/FigJam.

Total time: 1 hour:

1. Create an Figma FigJam Board. Label “User Experience (UX) Lexicon”.

a. Create a 3x Sections (Shift + S).

i. Label them: Known Jargon, Unknown Jargon, DO NOT USE.

b. Add a Sticky note (Shift + S).

i. Make sticky notes – Green, Yellow, Red to correspond with your sections.

c. Add UX Jargon Word to a Green Sticky note.

i. Add the description as you know it.

d. As you read the book, UX design articles, or other UX design learning, add jargon words to this Figma FigJam board.

| |

|----|

| Note: Activities in this book are not meant to be one-off examples but rather are part of the process and should be practiced over time. If you put in the work on these activities now it will help in the future when you need to remember things you learned, are looking to be inspired, or just want to share how you collect knowledge. |

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_32a)

UX design has a foundation in many businesses and can help solve problems for a variety of business models. There are limits just like with any other profession or skill set but aligning your efforts and skills with businesses that value UX design and are dedicated to doing HCD is a good starting place.

In the next chapter, we will explore the companies, organizations, communities involved, and conversations happening in the UX design universe. We will build on our understanding of human-centered design and agile software development.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_33a)

- UX design might be concentrated in some locations on the planet like Silicon Valley, CA but has relevance and opportunity around the globe.

- The UX design business community is large and growing every day but make sure to find businesses that want to use human-centered design.

- UX design and HCD are jargon prone so do your part to learn the language.

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:
