# Chapter 3: User Experience as a Team Endeavor

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_34a)

To extend our conversation from a previous chapter. we will dig into the formulation of human-centered design teams and what design teams work on. Human-centered design (HCD) is a problem-solving framework that centers on the needs of users (people) when creating products and services and is foundational to design thinking. We cover a few key communities to be involved with and how UX design can be applied to agile computer software development.

Structure

The chapter covers the following topics:

- UX deisgn team structure
  - Business part

  - Management part

  - Design part

  - Engineering part

- UX design and human-centered design team in practice

- UX design professional organizations
  - FigJam UX design community activity

- UX design and agile development

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_35a)

After concluding this chapter, you should be familiar with the communities that use UX design. You will be able to recall UX design team structure and familiarize yourself with UX design in practice as well as well as get introduced to UX design inside agile development teams.

[UX deisgn team structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_36a)

As we have been discussing, UX design is a team role. Are all teams equal in size and skill? No. Every business has different needs, different sizes, and, therefore, vastly different relationships to UX design or team structures. The variety makes discussing a team structure quite difficult. We will discuss the UX design team in theory and will also share what a few of my teams have looked like over the years. My goal is to shed light on the fact that there is no perfect UX design team or any ideal software company, but there are signs to look out for and ways to identify high-performing, high-functioning software teams.

Any software team will be made up of basically four parts (refer to [Figure 3.1](#ch03.xhtml_fig3-1)):

- 01: The business part

- 02: The management (project/product) part

- 03: The design part

- 04: The engineering part:

![A drawing of a van Description automatically generated](images/Figure_3.2.png)

Figure 3.1: The A-team of software: Business/Management/Design/Engineering

[Business part](#contents.xhtml_sc3_37a)

The business part is made up of business stakeholders. These team members could be in leadership roles e.g., chief executive officer (CEO), chief technology officer (CTO), chief financial officer (CFO), chief marketing officer (CMO), chief design officer (CDO), etc. or project-based where a project manager, product designer, UX design manager, or product manager is responsible for defining and rationalizing and communicating the needs of the business. The business stakeholders should be responsible for defining business requirements, establishing budgets, holding software teams to timelines, and feature release cycles. Depending on the team development process and feature release cycles, this is typically driven by a business' quarterly goals, but not always. If you work for a team that is part of a publicly traded company, the quarterly-driven business environment will be more pronounced due to quarterly earnings and regulatory business reporting that is required for public markets and company investors. The business roles should also be able to align software teams to business goals and help a software team know how their work is contributing to objectives and key results (OKRs) or other business key performance indicators (KPIs).

| |

|----|

| Note: As a UX designer, your ability to collaborate with business roles as well as be able to articulate the value UX design brings through business language and data is essential to advancing your career as well as broadening the impact UX design can have on a business. |

How business roles are aligned with software teams is highly dependent on the size of the business as well as the corporate structure. If you work on a small start-up team the business role will likely be shared and likely overlap with other team roles. On the other hand, if your software team is part of a larger corporation, you might have layers of business roles that require attention and need to be part of the decision-making process. The business part should strive for clarity in the team's purpose and facilitate the UX design and engineering roles to achieve their team's goals as well as their business success.

[TABLE]

The business is not your user, which is an important thing to understand about working with businesses that make software. Although it can be reasonable to think that a CEO, CTO, or Product Manager who is responsible for software creation is also someone to design for is wrong.

For example, Elon Musk used Twitter/X a lot, once an hour on average according to business insider ([https://www.businessinsider.com/elon-musk-twitter-use-increase-since-buying-company-2022-11](https://www.businessinsider.com/elon-musk-twitter-use-increase-since-buying-company-2022-11)). It was likely a determining factor in his decision to spend 44 billion dollars to purchase the company in 2022, however, the moment he became the owner of Twitter/X was the moment he stopped being a user of the platform. Does Elon Musk still use Twitter/X? Absolutely and you can follow him at [https://twitter.com/elonmusk](https://twitter.com/elonmusk). However, because Elon Musk is financially compromised by ownership of Twitter/X, his point of view and therefore his decision-making about the Twitter/X platform do not match up with that of real Twitter/X users. Hopefully, the designers and software engineers at Twitter/X are not just creating Twitter/x for Elon but instead are balancing the needs of the business and its owner alongside the needs of everyday Twitter/X users. Only time will tell if that is the case for Twitter/X, but the opportunity is available.

The reality is UX designers are required to balance the needs of businesses with the needs of their users. This is a difficult position which can be made more difficult by business owners who think they are the users themselves. In many cases, a UX designer’s role is to help a business, and their leadership not confuse their own needs for the needs of their users.

In the best scenarios, business roles understand that there is a gap between what the business wants and what the user wants and rely on UX designers and human-centered methods to bridge those issues to help solve those problems.

Your business stakeholders are an essential piece of a software team, and your ability to gauge how engaged that business part is or how knowledgeable they are in the software design process can be the difference between finding teams that build great software or working at exceedingly difficult places. As a UX designer, it is incumbent on you to grow your business part skills and be able to effectively work with business stakeholders while holding the line on making software that is what your users need.

[Management part](#contents.xhtml_sc3_38a)

The management part is made up of planners and business process hawks. These team members could be in leadership roles e.g., CDO, VP of product, VP of UX design, etc. as management contributors, be it product designers, product managers, project managers, technical product specialists, or other roles that oversee the steps involved in moving software improvements through a company’s development process. Depending on the team development process and feature release cycles, the way the plan for the team works will change. Typically, you will see software teams working inside 2-week design sprints. A sprint is part of scrum and is a term that comes from the agile product development framework, which we will discuss in more detail later. Management team members help run sprints and keep all team members working, sharing, and iterating their design and development solutions. The management part should focus on four aspects of software development: planning, execution, reviews/approvals, and retrospectives.

First is planning. What does the business want to do, and what changes need to be accomplished in the software? How hard is the change, addition, or update going to be? We also need to plan the time the team will need to accomplish the change, addition, or update, and the complexity of the change, addition, or update. The kinds of decisions and in what order to get the change, addition, or update accomplished will also be decided. The planning function of the management part is to attempt to be accurate with their plans and keep the software team aware and feeding back into the plan. There is an old Yiddish proverb that says, We plan…God laughs. The management part should work towards clarity in their plans, but be flexible enough to allow a plan to change or shift with the needs of the business, and grow with your users. Nothing will stifle an agile software team faster than a planning cycle that is too rigid or a management team that takes the my way or the highway approach.

For example, I worked at a company that would do product planning once a year in late January. The product teams would get together and put in a lot of work to show the business what a year-long plan would contain. We did our best to highlight product feature plans and complexity plans for the coming year, 2019. In March, just two short months later, the COVID-19 pandemic had us all working remotely, and the plans we had put in place were thrown out of the window to help our software and content address this massive shift. The world has many forces, both internally and externally, that can impact a product team’s plan. Typically, the longer the plan, the riskier it gets. The role of a UX designer for the business can be highly strategic when it comes to planning and helping a business estimate time and investment. Learning to work with management to reduce the risk and allow your team to execute the plans is hard but worth the effort.

Great software team management understands the ebbs and flows of software development and the knowledge that great software is not always squeezed into two-week sprints or able to be a crystal ball into the future.

The management part is the cog in the wheel of a software team and your ability as a UX designer to action a plan, keep the team moving, and improve management planning accuracy will only improve the final software outcome and your promotional opportunities.

Next is execution. Management helps guide team members to collaborate to accomplish an aspect of the project plan. Execution is about moving solutions through the research, design, and development cycles that ultimately become software used by people. Execution is about making sure the right resources are in the right place at the right time. Management’s role in execution is to make sure designers and developers have the information they need to solve the problems and that they are collaborating on the complexity, level of effort, and handoff process so that the software improvements can adhere to the plan, once execution is completed.

Next comes reviews and approvals. Management needs to review work accomplished by designers and developers to apply a quality check, and in some cases, can determine if a payment needs to be rendered for a completed software feature. Management should be assuring that business requirements are met in the execution of software development, whether that be during design, prototypes, staged features, or shipped software features. Management is not the only responsible party for reviews and approvals, and in some cases, UX designers and engineers have their own review and approval cycles inside their internal teams before a solution moves on to the final approval from management. The management’s role in reviews and approvals should be about keeping the design and development process moving forward. Ideally, they should be continually reviewing and approving work and be able to keep the software design and development teams efficient. One way to accomplish an efficient approval process with management is to apply asynchronous approval tools like [https://www.loom.com/](https://www.loom.com/) or [https://www.useloops.com/](https://www.useloops.com/) or others.

| |

|----|

| Note: If management is stepping into design and engineering solutions, it could be a symptom of poor business requirements in the first place. UX Design and engineering team members should hold management accountable for supplying accurate and stable requirements. |

Finally comes retrospectives. Management has the responsibility to oversee the end-to-end of a team’s progress and this process requires a feedback loop. Retrospectives are collaboration functions that allow all team members to reflect on the process or sprints. There are many retrospective frameworks, but a popular one is Start, Stop, and Continue. Management should be interested in tuning a team’s effectiveness and continue to learn what a team should start doing (from each role point-of-view), what the team should stop doing (from each role point-of-view), as well as what they should continue to maintain (from each role point-of-view). Retrospectives should be a consistent part of a team’s design/development process and typically happen at the end of projects or are scheduled into the end of design and development sprints. We will discuss more about UX design and agile development in the coming chapters, but a key tenet of agile is to regularly reflect and adjust the way a team works to boost effectiveness. Management retrospectives are a key component in improving a team’s effectiveness and morale. Retrospectives need to be practiced by teams to become effective, and should practice different retrospective frameworks to keep the team collaborating and sharing in new ways. There are also retrospective tools built into product management software like Atlassian Jira or specific retrospective software tools like: [https://www.retrium.com/](https://www.retrium.com/) and others.

[Design part](#contents.xhtml_sc3_39a)

The design part is made up of researchers and makers. These team members could be in leadership roles, e.g., CEO, vice president (VP) of product, CDO, director of UX design, Lead User Researcher, etc., as individual contributors, be it UX designers, interaction designers, product designers, UI designers, content designers, copywriters, graphic designers, etc. The design team members are responsible for making things. They translate the business requirements, the management plans, plus the needs of users into software solutions. UX Designers are typically responsible for creativity and leverage creativity to solve problems. UX design is a broad set of methodologies, but all share common outcomes. The design part can be perceived as messy or unstructured; however, this is entirely up to the way teams express their vision and arrive at solutions. Design team members should ask a lot of questions as the design process typically moves from unknown to known. Some projects or features go from 0-1:

![A person with glasses and a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_3.1.png)

Figure 3.2: The A-team of software: Business/Management/Design/Engineering

What I love about the creative process, and this may sound naive, but it is this idea that one day there is no idea, and no solution, but the next day there is an idea. I find that incredibly exciting and conceptually actually remarkable.

– Jonathan Ive (Apple, 2007)

The design part is hard. UX designers must consider the criteria applicable to solving problems. Great software is the by-product of countless considerations, millions of design iterations, numerous failed prototypes, and many rejected ideas. Design members must have fortitude and git to keep at it. We can make our world better through design and software. The level of effort from design members to create solutions that scale around the globe is tremendous and frankly requires people who care about the product and their users. Creativity can be elusive, and businesses do not always understand or value its contributions, but it is worth it. The design part is skilled at addressing these challenges, and we lean on human-centered design methods to infuse empathy, creativity, utility, simplicity, accessibility, and so much more into every nook and cranny of our outcomes.

The design part is imperative to a software team, and your ability to gauge how knowledgeable a design team is, how well their design process works, or how creative their team functions is a huge contribution to growing as a UX designer. The quality of a design team has a huge impact on your opportunity to make meaningful things in the world.

| |

|----|

| Note: When seeking employment with the design part of a team make sure to ask lots of questions about their design process. For e.g., How does your team stay inspired? How often do your UX designers talk to end users? Ask about your design process. How does the team facilitate creative thinking? et cetera. |

Similarly, good design teams share a human-centered ethos and are willing to talk about user research, user testing, and solving the right software problems. All creative teams must deal with constraints, varying budgets, and changing timelines, but understanding how a creative team bridges these challenges to achieve great results is paramount. Over your career, you will continue to recognize creative teams that are worth joining and growing, so you too can design great software. As a UX designer, it is incumbent on you to grow your design skills and be able to effectively work with not only business stakeholders but also the broad set of specialized design skills that can be required to solve software problems.

[Engineering part](#contents.xhtml_sc3_40a)

The engineering part is made up of coders. These team members could be in leadership roles, e.g., VP of product, CTO, VP of engineering, lead front-end, lead back-end, etc., or as individual contributors be it FE developers, BE developers, full-stack developers, AI/ML engineers, computer scientists, technical writers, quality assurance (QA) engineers, data scientists, etc:

!

Figure 3.3: A software coder adding to their software system

The engineering team members are responsible for making things work and translating design solutions into working-coded software solutions. Developers translate the business requirements into technical requirements so that design and engineering can arrive at software solutions. Developers are typically responsible for code and leverage logic and technology system rules to solve and deploy software. Development is a broad set of methodologies, but all share outcomes to make working and stable software.

The engineering part is hard. Development members must consider many criteria on the path to solving problems, and developers must continuously learn about ways to be more efficient, secure, and compliant with technology regulations and changing development methods. Good developers are great collaborators and see the opportunity to work alongside the business part, the management part, and the design part. Design and development should not be at odds as they both need each other to produce the best outcome for their businesses and their users.

For example, when a UX designer produces a prototype to improve a software navigation issue that was revealed during user testing. The developer is needed to help recommend code solutions that will work with the UX designer’s ideas, as well as complete and push the production-ready code to their users. The UX designer and developer must get on the same page to be efficient and effective in making software updates.

The engineering part is a required piece of the software team, and your ability to get a sense of how knowledgeable they are in the software development process can be the difference between finding teams that build great software or working at places that are difficult or stifling to a UX designer. As a UX designer, you will only do yourself a favor by familiarizing yourself with the development part of the process. It does not mean you have to learn to code necessarily, however, your ability to collaborate and build solutions that your development team can build will help you work faster, and ultimately allow the team to push out better solutions, not only for the team, but for your users as well. As a UX designer, your ability to have empathy for the development team members as well as designing solutions that encompass the technical requirements will allow you to collaborate more closely and move faster.

Here is what a software team looks like:

- 3 business stakeholders (CEO, VP of product, department leader, etc.)

- 1 x product manager

- 1 x technical project manager/scrum master

- 2 x UX designer/researcher (Me) + 1x UI designer

- 1 x technical lead

- 5 x front-end engineers

- 4 x back-end/full stack engineers

- 2 x QA engineers

  | |

  |----|

  | Note: The design/developer ratio = 5 to 1. 5 developers to every one designer. Asking what this ratio looks like during a company interview is a good way to gauge a business’s point of view on design and can help you understand your role on a software team. If the design/dev ratio is high, say 25+/1, be wary that design in that organization is at risk of being a bottleneck, or worse, is unable to support the needs of the development team altogether. |

Is this a perfect team? No; however, the better a software team functions, the better the outcomes are for users and the businesses. A UX designer should look to improve their skills to join high-functioning teams, as well as be able to recognize where solid teams they live and breathe. Our team size seems to work for us, and we have over time learned to improve our effectiveness as well as shipped some high-value solutions to the business, which is all any business can ask for. As you learn more about UX design and the software design process, your ability to recognize and potentially drive how you and your team contribute to a business will be key in producing high-quality solutions at scale.

[UX design and human-centered design in practice](#contents.xhtml_sc2_41a)

UX design exists at countless businesses around the globe, and hopefully, you are interested in joining a team that allows you to keep learning as well as wants you to grow your experience. In practice, human-centered design and UX design are not uniform from business to business or team to team. As UX design practitioners, we might be able to conceptually agree on what the human-centered design process is, but in execution, those outcomes might produce different deliverables and results from team to team. Our ability to share practices and feed off a similar ethos is what binds UX designers together. As we have been discussing, UX design is a learn-by-doing profession and will require you to continuously learn throughout your career. The human-centered process must be practiced a lot, but do not mistake it as a recipe for success. The reality of our world is that humans are ever-changing, technology is advancing at a breakneck speed, and design and development tools impact how we deliver software at scale. There is no silver bullet, and the processes we discuss in this book or in other corners of the design world are not fail safes. UX design is difficult, and it requires teams to continually adapt. Although our teams might change as well as our tools, what will remain true is that humans matter. When designers care about people and focus on their users at the center of their solutions, it improves businesses. However, to do this in practice is difficult, but leaning on UX designers and human-centered design methods will ultimately help your teams make it a reality. There are many ways to improve teams through design, and if you and your teams find aspects of the design process that lead to success for the business and your users, then make sure to share how your team accomplished it with the design community. The design community is constantly learning from each other and is eager to hear how the design process works from team to team.

| |

|----|

| Note: Whether it be a designer’s portfolio case studies or articles on aspects of the design process, there are a lot of sharing successes but less about sharing failures. I encourage you to share your failure too. Try and avoid the rose-colored view of design in what you share. |

As a UX designer, you should join a community to grow your experience, learn from others, and build connections that improve your chosen profession.

[UX design professional organizations](#contents.xhtml_sc2_42a)

As you continue to learn about UX design and human-centered skills, know that you are not alone and by doing so you are by default joining a community. It is entirely up to you to choose how active, or passive you are with our community. It is encouraged that you be as active as possible as the reality is more opportunities will be made available to those that seek out a professional community than those that do not. Professional communities are easier than ever to join as they operate online and are equally as interested:

- User Experience Professional Association (UXPA) ([https://uxpa.org/](https://uxpa.org/)):
  - Annual fee based on experience.

  - International with local chapters.

  - Produce an annual international conference to gather designers each year.

- Interaction Design Association (IxDA) ([https://www.ixda.org/](https://www.ixda.org/)):
  - Annual fee based on experience.

  - International with local chapters.

  - Produce an annual international conference to gather designers each year.

  - Resources and connection opportunities.

- American Institute of Graphic Arts (AIGA) ([https://www.aiga.org/](https://www.aiga.org/)):
  - Annual fee based on experience.

  - International community with local chapters.

  - Produce an annual conference to gather designers each year.

  - Run competitions, produce a blog, and engage in policy.

  - Professional development, education, and design certificates.

- Join online communities and local UX meetups.
  - Here is a list of 30 Online UX communities: [https://blog.uxtweak.com/top-ux-communities-and-groups/](https://blog.uxtweak.com/top-ux-communities-and-groups/)

    | |

    |----|

    | Note: UX design is a significant business investment, and you should look to join companies that see the value and are dedicated to growing their UX/HCD thinking ranks. Try not to join companies that just dabble in UX design or that keep designers at arm’s length. We will discuss this later. |

[FigJam UX design community activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_43a)

Let us now do some research on our own about the UX design community and find organizations, publications, and communities both near and far where we can engage, learn, and contribute.

Total time: 1-2 hours:

1. Create a new FigJam board.

2. Label the board UX design community.

3. Poke around the internet using Google, Quora, Reddit, social media, etc., and find communities and resources related to UX design and HCD. This could be Blogs, Forums, Articles, Meetups, Slack/Discord channels, and more.

a. Create a 4-column table (Shift + T). Add 10+ resources from the internet:

i. Column 1: Community name

ii. Column 2: Website URLs

iii. Column 3: Community type: Professional organization, community groups, publications, resources, etc.

iv. Column 4: Personal notes

v. In the notes add whether you have joined or are willing to join each community, resource, or publication.

| |

|----|

| Note: There are many communities and many resources. I recommend starting local and seeking out connections in your local town, city, state, county, or prefecture, then expanding broader. |

[UX design and agile development](#contents.xhtml_sc2_44a)

One of the difficult things about doing UX design is that you are part of the team, and to be a good team member, you must also understand some of the things your colleagues are doing. For example, the UX design role and the development role are not the same and are occupied by very different skills but need to work together. Therefore, we should be able to adhere to a consistent set of working guidelines. In the software world, many teams apply the agile development framework. Agile is a software management framework that allows teams to break projects into phases (sprints) and encourages teams to practice continuous collaboration and iteration. Agile teams typically follow a design/dev cycle that looks like planning \| executing \| evaluating.

Many software designers and developers have adhered to this framework as it is proven to produce high-performing teams. Check out ([https://agilemanifesto.org](https://agilemanifesto.org)) to learn more. Agile teams follow four primary values:

- Individuals and interactions over processes and tools.

- Working software over comprehensive documentation.

- Customer collaboration over contract negotiations.

- Responding to change over following a plan.

These values might sound familiar or at least are rational based on your experience, and designers find the agile process particularly relatable to the design thinking methodology we discussed in a previous chapter. UX designers who value practicing human-centered methods will find value 3. Customer collaboration over contract negotiations is the closest value to user-centered or human-centered as customers are your users, and only by understanding their needs and incorporating their feedback can agile software be any good.

The agile framework also has 12 principles that I highly recommend you familiarize yourself with, which you can find here: ([https://agilemanifesto.org/principles.html](https://agilemanifesto.org/principles.html)). However, this book is not dedicated to agile training as there is a whole set of books, certificates, and educational opportunities for growing those skills. The reason a UX designer should become versed in agile is so that you can work on teams that also practice this methodology. Your ability to follow the lead and be a good agile team member will likely require you to translate your design work into the agile process.

Does the UX design process need to fit well into the agile method? It is not necessary. However, agile as a framework is meant to be quite flexible for each team’s needs, and the design process should follow suit. Agile teams should work towards creating some level of balance among the members of the team as no one member group, be it business, management, design, or development, should impose their will on another. Agile teams strive to be nimble and be able to adapt quickly to changing users, changing requirements, and even changing team members. We will dig into more about how UX design and agile development strategies play nicely together, but for now, know that agile can be so much more than just a software buzzword, but only if team members apply the framework effectively, and business and management allow for agile to thrive. Agile for UX design is not standardized among software teams, but typically, the UX designer works ahead of development by 1x 2-week sprint and then manages the support between new work and what is currently being developed to keep the team effective and efficient. When a UX designer is allowed to focus on discovery and delivery, this is called dual-track agile. A goal of dual-track agile is that team members are not blocked and have new and old things to work on, and that UX designers can work slightly ahead of development, which allows agile to function like a relay race. Look out for software teams that can clearly articulate how agile keeps their design and development teams effective. A clear sign a software team is using agile effectively is if they are allowing UX designers the time and space to do discovery, user research, and user testing, and not just supporting development sprints and their code commitments only.

Some businesses participate in agile by name only or faux-agile. This is where management and recruitment teams talk about agile but, in practice, do not allow teams to demonstrate the process. Some will say they participate in 2-week sprints but then plan out rigid feature requirements for the whole year, which, as we discussed previously, is not very agile. It can be difficult to see under the hood of any software team but understanding the values of agile and asking questions about the design and development process will help reveal who is doing it well.

| |

|----|

| Note: Look out for businesses who are participating in faux-agile where they claim they do agile but are unable to discuss how their teams apply the process or are only using agile language and not actually applying it. |

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_45a)

UX design is a growing set of communities that are increasing their ranks around the globe. Some of this is because UX designers create value for businesses and businesses exist everywhere, but a large piece is that UX design is a team role, and we work with teams to build software or other solutions. The work is hard, but if we keep learning from each other and join communities that support growth, improve software education, and maintain professional quality standards, then over time, our software tools and the teams that design and develop them will get better as well.

In the next chapter, we will start building skills for making software by practicing some human-centered methods as well as exploring some system thinking strategies. Besides getting some hands-on practice, you will also get a chance to reflect on your own understanding of design systems and improve your experience with design systems.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_46a)

- UX design teams are made of larger teams but basically from 4 parts:
  1. Business

  2. Management (project/product)

  3. Design

  4. Engineering

- The UX design business community is large and growing every day but make sure to find other people who are doing human-centered design.

- UX design and HCD apply their skills inside software teams that likely practice agile methods. Learn what agile means and look out for teams who practice it effectively.

Join our book’s Discord space

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:

# Chapter 4: UX Design is a Human-Centered System Design

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_47a)

Extending our knowledge from previous chapters, in this chapter we will start building skills for making software through practicing some human-centered methods as well as exploring some system thinking strategies. Besides getting some hands-on practice, you will also get a chance to reflect on your own understanding of design systems and improve your experience with systems.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_48a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Human-centered design for problem definition
  - Problem statement writing activity

- UX designer is not the user

- System thinking
  - People, not users

- UX designers focus on people
  - Communicate with real people who use the product
    - Humanize the issues

- Empathy UX design activity

- Systems of empathy

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_49a)

After completing this chapter, you should be familiar with the systems that use UX design. You will be able to practice human-centered problem writing as you analyze system strategies as well as reflect on your own experience with design systems that we use regularly.

[Human-centered design for problem definition](#contents.xhtml_sc2_50a)

Effective problem definition is crucial in human-centered design as it involves understanding user needs, the impact of technology, and the complexities of modern software design teams in the business of solving problems. In the UX design process, problems are often complex and not immediately apparent. Moreover, if you are working on teams that are only willing to go and fix easy problems, that could be a part of the problem as well. UX designers should embrace the challenge of solving problems by ensuring they are clearly defined and understood by everyone involved.

The role UX designers play on agile software teams can vary wildly, but you are a part of the team responsible for finding and then fixing problems in your systems. One of the challenges in our modern world is that technology moves quickly, and our customers are not always great at telling us what they need. Technology is itself a catalyst to human behavior and can produce outcomes that the UX designers of the technology could not anticipate or, worse, willfully choose to ignore during the design process. The technology itself can be a creator of problems. For example, Henry Ford’s Model T automobile solved a very real set of problems in mobility in 1908. Today, however, the combustion engine, assembly line manufacturing, and improved mobility speeds have also created other very real issues for the world including climate change, supply chain issues, traffic deaths, and more.

Problems are potentially abundant, and your ability to recognize, document, and classify the severity of your software issues and users' problems will help you be able to design effective solutions that potentially address the root cause of the problem. As UX designers, we need to improve our skills in articulating problems. There are many ways to improve the process of identifying and defining problems, and it is recommended to focus a significant amount of time and effort on making sure the problems are succinct. There is a commonly quoted saying from Abraham Lincoln, If I have 6 hours to chop down a tree. I’d spend the first 4 hours sharpening my axe. This quote is a good piece of advice and speaks to the power of planning and defining the problem:

![A drawing of a person sitting in a chair Description automatically generated](images/Figure_4.1.png)

Figure 4.1: An illustration of Abraham Lincoln at his desk and chopping down a tree

The sharpening of the axe is the equivalent of the time spent defining and articulating the problem. As we will learn, UX designers can move quickly with their making through prototyping and other design methods. However, you want to ensure that the speed and agility you have acquired are utilized effectively. UX designers can only move quickly in the design process because they understand the issue they are solving. If you do not know what you are solving when you are designing, you need to stop. Put your pencil, mouse, or digital pen down and return to the problem definition.

How do you approach defining problems in your projects? Consider the diverse perspectives of your team members. As you will learn, there are many ways to define a problem, and just as we have different team members focused on different aspects of the software system, they might all define a problem and solution a bit differently. For example, a business stakeholder might define a problem through the lens of the business while a developer might define the problem through a technical point of view, and a UX designer might define the problem through a user and visual point of view. Let us look at how different roles might define a problem and then practice unifying the problem through a problem statement activity.

A problem definition by role:

- Business stakeholder problem definition: Our website sales have declined each quarter. We need to reverse this trend.

- Product management problem definition: Our product page and shopping experience are not moving users through the process of completing a purchase.

- Engineers’ problem definition: Our product page and shopping cart are not loading quickly enough, causing confusion and abandonment of the cart experience.

- Designers’ problem definition: Our users are not compelled to add products to their cart, and few trust our checkout experience.

- Marketing problem definition: Our customers are not finding out about products effectively.

To create a unified problem statement, gather insights from each role, discuss the commonalities and differences, and collaboratively draft a comprehensive statement that addresses all perspectives.

[Problem statement writing activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_51a)

Total time: 20 minutes per problem statement.

Summary: You are a UX designer working on a small team for a game company. Your team is being asked to solve a problem on the website.

Business: A medium-sized board game manufacturer.

Problem data: Website sales are dropping.

Refer to the following steps:

1. Create a new Figma file or Google Doc file.

2. Label the file problem statements.

a. Add a new frame (F) presentation (16x9) – 1920px x 1080px.

b. Label the frame Problem Statement v1.

c. Use the type tool (T) to drag a type box.

d. Write out a problem statement using this template:

i. (User Role—Name and age) feels (painful feeling) about (reason) and needs to (user action) but is dealing with (issue/obstacle).

3. Review your v1 problem statement, then write a second one.

a. Write out a customer-focused problem statement:

i. When it comes to (user situation, customer types) need a way to (Job to do/Problem) so that (desired user outcome) and (desired business goals) are achieved.

4. Repeat writing problem statements for practice.

Sample:

- Problem statement v1: John Andrews, 25, feels confused and impatient with the concert ticket purchasing portal and needs to confirm he has secured his tickets for the on-sale concert but is dealing with a decreasing clock and a seat selection process that is slow to reload and difficult to select.

- Customer-focused problem statement v2: When it comes to purchasing concert tickets from the venue ticketing portal, John Andrews, 25, an avid concertgoer, needs to confidently select and pay for his seats so that he can share his support for live music and the venue can sell out their event.

  [TABLE]

The world has many cause-and-effect relationships that go into the scenarios that produce problems. The reality of software design is that the job of the UX designer is often more than just fixing the design-related issues, rather, it starts to overlap with understanding and combining all the different factors that go into the cause of a problem. A UX designer is not a wizard. Our work is not magic. It is the outcome of considering many factors and trying to solve the root of the problem by seeking out the root of the issues experienced by our users.

Will every problem you solve fix the root cause? No, however, if a UX designer and software team have done their hard work, they should have confidence in the solutions they are proposing. Confidence in the solution is a by-product of confidence in the problem definition. By focusing on defining unique and specific problems up front, a UX designer can devise ways to solve the specific problems with the help of the software and development team members. A UX designer should know if what they make is usable, useful, reliable, and sustainable over time for their users, and that all starts from a well-defined problem.

[UX designer is not the user](#contents.xhtml_sc2_52a)

Even if the UX designer uses human-centered skills and advocates for them, the intended audience is not us. A UX designer is solving problems for the users, and because of this there is a strong temptation to assume they can operate as proxy users; however, this is wrong. The designer is not the user:

![A person holding a megaphone Description automatically generated](images/Figure_4.2.png)

Figure 4.2: An illustration of a UX designer standing on a desk proclaiming “I am not the user!”

Designers can fall into the trap of thinking they are right because they designed things, and this is potentially a dangerous and costly mistake. A UX designer’s role in a software team is to advocate for users and to do this effectively, you must pull in the perspectives of your actual users as often as you can. Design solutions are experiments that require validation from your audience. The more your design experiments are informed by your users, the better the outcomes of the experiments. One of the many challenges of UX designers working on agile software teams is that teams can get into the need to ship features during 2-week sprints, and the designer’s role is making that possible. However, a UX designer should make sure that the features that do get shipped are useful to the users. We will cover the topic of prototyping in much greater detail in the upcoming chapters, but a UX designer should validate a feature through low-fidelity prototyping with actual users before implementing the solution and shipping it into the software with the team.

| |

|----|

| Note: During UX design interviews, ask how often and where their UX designers interface with real users. What does solution validation look like? If a software team says, we learn from our paying customers on the live product, be mindful that you might not have the opportunity to do effective UX in that environment. |

[System thinking](#contents.xhtml_sc2_53a)

We have been discussing that UX designers work on teams. Teams are systems and they perform inside larger systems. Depending on the size of your software company, there can be many layers to your teams and can result in many other systems as well, including technology systems, design systems, business systems, and communication systems. All these systems need to coordinate together to produce solutions. For example, when designing a new feature, a UX designer needs to understand how the business system prioritizes customer needs, which can help in creating solutions that are aligned with the business and impactful for the user. The goal is to align systems and find the overlap between the user and the business.

As a UX designer, your role is connected to understanding and visualizing complex systems. A UX design is about systems logic. UX designers are particularly skilled at communicating how things work, as we must design things. If you do not understand the systems that go into producing solutions, you will ultimately be at the mercy of one of those systems to produce solutions. UX designers do not need to possess expertise in all the systems. However, they should be able to understand and communicate with each system to implement change.

Much of what a UX designer achieves will not necessarily be visible in software designs, wireframes, or user interfaces but will manifest itself in teams that understand their systems better, communicate solutions faster, and ultimately have teams that perform better with fewer errors like the one described here. A key to improving any software product or software team is to raise all the systems to the same level. Not every feature or priority is equal, but all systems that produce high-quality features function as one. In concept, this seems rational, but this is very difficult to achieve. Mainly because not all software businesses staff their teams with similar skills. Therefore, UX designers should work at adding empathy at every turn, communicate with real people who use the product, and humanize the issues to the team.

[People, not users](#contents.xhtml_sc3_54a)

As we have been discussing, a UX designer’s role relates to focusing on the end user of a piece of software. An end user is a person who ultimately interacts with a product or service. A key aspect of this is that a user is a person. It is unclear why the software world has focused on the language of users, and it might be that people experience (PX) designer does not roll off the tongue, but Donald Norman likely coined UX design, and that made sense and thus stuck with us for better or worse.

| |

|----|

| Note: I have attempted to call myself a people designer in a social setting and it did not go over well. The response was a puzzled look with a follow-up question, what do you do? If you talk with any early UX designers, they likely have stories of non-designers being confused by their professional titles back in the day. |

Regardless of the job title, the ways UX designers impact a piece of software and, ultimately, a business, can be directly tied to how well they get a business to care about the people they serve. UX designers cannot be the only team members responsible for caring about people who use their software, but we can diversify our methods, scale our human-centered tools, and democratize the mindset that decisions we make on our software impact real people every day.

[UX designers focus on people](#contents.xhtml_sc2_55a)

We attempt to infuse empathy for our users into solutions through the design process from beginning to end. Empathy is the ability to understand and share the feelings of another. Empathy, when it relates to UX design, includes understanding our users’ problems. We can accomplish this by bringing in real customers to identify issues, learn from them via user interviews, do market or user research, build user personas, construct user scenarios, develop user insights, design user journeys, and analyze their feelings.

[Communicate with real people who use the product](#contents.xhtml_sc3_56a)

We understand that UX designers are not users, and therefore, we need to understand who our users are. We gain knowledge about the people who use our products. Knowledge about people can be accomplished in several ways, but the best ways allow UX designers to interact and talk with real customers when UX designers get the chance to spend time with the people who use our products, their needs, actions, and mental models. Time spent with real users builds context that seeps into a designer’s process and ultimately results in software products that not only address the user's needs but can anticipate them as well. UX designers should be sponges and recognize that, to solve the right issues, we need to care about the people who are having them. There are potentially a lot of assumptions that must be made in the design process, and it is better to minimize assumptions made about users.

Nearly every human being can have empathy because we participate in mankind, and we grow up in the world. Empathy is learned through life experience, and fascinatingly, it needs to be practiced for our entire lives. A UX designer's ability to empathize and apply it to software solutions or extend it to people we design for is a valuable skill. Designers should bring empathy practices to their businesses and their users. In many cases, UX designers are tasked with distributing empathy and communicating its value to companies and inside products. Even better is when UX designers help organizations grow their empathy methodologies and build software teams where all team members understand the value of caring about their users.

[Humanize the issues](#contents.xhtml_sc3_57a)

As we discussed, empathy is a key skill for UX designers to practice and extend to their software teams. Empathy for your users is not solely the responsibility of UX designers because software teams who hold their users at the center of their software, typically build better solutions. The importance of being able to translate and democratize empathy across a team as well as a business is deeply connected with the role UX designers play. As UX designers, we consider the systems we impact and the people who make those changes. The reality is people make software, and therefore, software is a mirror to the values of the people and businesses who design it, and this idea is both revelatory and terrifying. As UX designers, our role is to make digital products and software that users and businesses want but also to humanize all the issues that come up along the way. Our responsibility is to question the validity of every issue and investigate how we can determine its truth.

One good way to humanize issues is to personify them and attach issues to real people. The concept of adding a username to issues or problems is akin to a politician using her specific constituent for a campaign. UX designers should improve their way of telling stories about how they know the issues experienced by their users, which can be streamlined into a story. The three-act story structure is a good starting place for creating and writing stories, as shown:

![A diagram of a diagram of action Description automatically generated](images/Figure_4.3.png)

Figure 4.3: 3-Act structure diagram

The three acts of a three-act structure are:

- Setup (who is your story about, set up the characters).

- Confrontation (what challenges, issues, or obstacles do the characters have, and how are they failing or ending in crisis).

- Resolution (where your character can succeed).

A person using a piece of software is your setup to tell a better story. The better you understand people and can communicate who your users are, their motivations, hopes, dreams, fears, successes, and failures, the more context a software team must work from to solve problems.

| |

|----|

| Note: Avoid false empathy. Empathy that is performative and gives the impression you understand and care without truly connecting and engaging with other people. |

UX designers should begin by defining their target audience and then interacting with them to cultivate empathy. A way to identify your target audience is by building proto personas. Proto-personas are an ad-hoc form for guessing who your users are. They are methods for software teams and UX designers to illicit the assumptions our stakeholders hold about their users:

![A close-up of a paper Description automatically generated](images/Figure_4.4.png)

Figure 4.4: Proto persona sample

Empathy UX activity

Let us now help you practice your empathy skills by creating a proto persona. Proto personas should be a quick and early process to help identify possible user targets to reveal glaring stereotypes or dangerous assumptions. They can be quickly practiced with paper and pen, whiteboards, or digital whiteboard tools like Miro or Figma.

Total time: 1hr (20 min each):

1. Take out a 5x 8.5 x 11in sheet of paper:

a. Fold the paper into quarters.

i. Fold in ½ longways and fold in ½ again.

2. Create a proto-persona:

a. Your user: Upper left quarter:

i. Add a name, age, location, and drawing of your user.

b. User behavioral demographics: Upper right quarter:

i. Focus on preferences, buying patterns, attitudes, political stance, technology usage, etc.

c. Values, goals, and motivations: Lower left quarter:

i. What do they like?

ii. What matters to them?

iii. What do they care about?

iv. What are they trying to accomplish?

d. Needs and pain points: Lower right quarter:

i. What does your user need?

ii. What challenges do they face?

iii. What is frustrating?

iv. What are the known issues that affect their interaction?

3. Repeat four more times.

| |

|----|

| Note: Activities in this book are not meant to be one-off examples but rather are part of the process and should be practiced over time. If you put in the work on these activities now, it will help in the future when you need to remember things you learned, are looking to be inspired, or just want to share how you collect knowledge. |

[Systems of empathy](#contents.xhtml_sc2_58a)

Now that you have practiced articulating your user through a proto-persona, you might notice something is missing. A proto-persona is not a reflection of your actual users, it is made up by you. If you have tried hard, then it might be quite good, and the goal should be to create proto-personas that are authentic and sincere to the best of your ability. If they look more like the characters from The Wolf of Wall Street film or a Marvel Studio film, you need more practice. Luckily, they are fast to produce, and you can practice them regularly.

A good place to practice making proto-personas is in airports when you are traveling or in other public places like busy coffee shops. You can try and characterize the people you see in transit or out in the world. Besides being a useful killer of time, quickly making proto-personas can be a great way to practice your empathy for humans. A UX designer should constantly be practicing their empathy and articulating their understanding of people.

| |

|----|

| Note: When traveling, wait until you are at your departure gate and use a notebook like a Moleskine to capture your proto-personas. Also, try and avoid getting caught staring unless you want to explain what you are doing and why to the person you are capturing. |

A proto-persona should be rapidly improved upon by moving the initial sketches and notes into digitized formats, and many digital tools from Adobe to Miro to Figma can help speed up your sharing of your empathy skills through software. The following sample was created with Figma:

![A black and white image of a cartoon character Description automatically generated](images/Figure_4.5.png)

Figure 4.5: Digitized proto-persona sample

A proto-persona is just a stepping stone and can be easily discarded if they are not good. Furthermore, if proto-personas are shared and agreed upon, they should be deeply investigated and modified through actual user research. Luckily, they are quick and useful methods for narrowing down a team's focus. As a UX designer, you must get accustomed to the idea that much of your work is temporary. Software is always changing, and very few things we make have true permanence. However, things that do have lasting power are processes and empathy practices.

A UX designer’s ability to show they care about users will never go out of style. We will not only use proto-personas but a suite of empathy tools and methods for driving understanding and user insights into our solutions and our teams. Empathy for your users can act more as a systems ethos, and when stood up and distributed by UX designers effectively, it can have lasting impacts on the software systems we share with the world.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_59a)

UX design can impact businesses and software products in numerous ways but much of it is rooted in driving empathy deep into the design and development process. From writing user-centered problems to defining authentic users as a team, the ability of a product team to have a suite of methods for caring and driving empathy into their process is a key task for UX design.

In the next chapter, you will build on your empathy and user research skills as we explore human-centered processes for understanding design systems through qualitative and quantitative methods.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_60a)

- Problem writing is difficult and should go through multiple rounds and consider multiple factors, from users to businesses.

- The UX designer is not the user, but they should be responsible for learning as much as they can. A UX designer should practice their empathy and find ways to uncover their assumptions.

- UX design and HCD are full of methods like proto-personas but should be practiced over time as empathy for your users is not a switch that can be turned on and off. It should be a foundation.

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:
