# Chapter 5: User Research Strategies

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_61a)

Now, that you have a grasp on aspects of how UX and human-centered skills are systematic, let us dive into some strategies for improving our understanding of the people we design systems for.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_62a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Empathy building
  - Addressing real users with real problems

  - Qualitative interviewing

  - Quantitative surveys

- Qualitative analysis and user insights
  - Making user data consumable and sharable

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_63a)

After completing this chapter, you should be familiar with user research methods that UX designers and user researchers alike use. You will be able to apply some qualitative strategies as you build understanding through quantitative methods and practice writing meaningful insights as we use these skills regularly.

[Empathy building](#contents.xhtml_sc2_64a)

Empathy as a starting place for UX designers is an interesting perspective because it feels obvious. Designers make things for people; therefore, they should care about the people we design for. This concept is simple, but in practice, this is where software teams or businesses can struggle. Empathy is not evenly distributed, which can result in different priorities creeping into each team member. As we have been discussing, empathy is about caring about people, and caring about your people is a value. If a company or software team does not value its customers, it will be very hard for designers to apply more empathy strategies to the software design process. What makes this more difficult is that user empathy is not binary. A company will not say, We do not care about our customers. However, companies who do not put their customers first will do other things like refuse to do user research, restrict UX design and development through restrictive tight deadlines, or value shipping any solution vs. the solution the users want. There is a myriad of reasons any company can place as blockers to designers, or that hamper their ability to create great human-centered software. As a UX designer, you will need to recognize these signs and work on ways to prevent the creation of bad software. Creating great software is hard and can be accomplished in many ways, and most of it starts with improving user empathy. Empathy building starts with understanding the needs and feelings of another person, as shown in the following figure:

![A drawing of two people Description automatically generated](images/Figure_5.1.png)

Figure 5.1: Empathy-building illustration of a man and woman understanding each other’s point of view

One way is through a company's hiring practices. If a company hires a designer motivated to implement human-centered design methods, then that work can get done. New team members will want to focus on building and growing empathy practices that sustain their work and ultimately improve the software business. The new designers will bring with them fresh empathy skills and a new passion for caring about their users, and software solutions will ultimately benefit the company. Empowering HR departments to bring on the right kind of designers will also improve the product faster. At a previous company, we had an HR team member come and sit in on collaborative workshops our design/dev team was doing so they could see firsthand the way the team worked as well as understand the types of skills and communication requirements we were looking to find in potential candidates. Cultivating human-centered designers who can all work together is hard but is next to impossible if the team bringing in talent is disconnected from what is needed.

Another way to build human-centered empathy is through education, training, and practice. Human-centered skills are not standardized or evenly distributed among industries or software design professionals. This leads to companies that have cobbled together their software systems and managed with or without the use of human-centered design or UX designers. The opportunity to train existing software teams in human-centered methods allows teams to share a common understanding and ultimately become more efficient. With training and practice, a software team can learn human-centered methods and work towards extracting value from the training of the team. Training human-centered design also allows a broader range of team members to be brought up to speed than just designers. For example, IBM has committed to training design thinking in all their team members and even the public. You can also get certified by IBM in Enterprise Design Thinking and other design thinking courses.

[https://www.ibm.com/design/thinking/](https://www.ibm.com/design/thinking/)

| |

|----|

| Note: UX design is constantly evolving, and learning new skills is key. Completing certifications is a good way to show employers that you know how to learn, and that you have experience with the hands-on skills needed to do UX design proficiently. |

A significant challenge with businesses trying to educate, train, or comply with human-centered design is whether the business is willing to invest in their teams and their people to apply human-centered methods. Some organizations are highly committed to growing their people and their teams as they know it potentially is a competitive advantage. However, this training cannot just be a one-off workshop or opt-in learning experience, as that kind of training will not be widely adopted by your teams. Design thinking and UX design are applied processes and should be practiced and repeated for them to be effective.

A third way an organization or business can implement a more human-centered design is through competitive analysis and attempting to keep up with their competition. Doing design well is a proven competitive advantage, and businesses have a vested interest in making sure they can compete. In many cases, companies have tried to compete by hiring UX design and human-centered designers in haphazard and inconsistent ways. If Business A has 5 UX designers who are supported in human-centered design methods and building a great product, Business B might be inclined to create a similar team structure to be competitive. Business B can hire 5 UX designers for their business, but they are not automatically able to reap the benefits of effective design processes, especially if they do not understand what Business A is doing to make their UX design and software team effective in the first place. Competition between companies and the proliferation of the Internet has led to an increase in design-based roles. However, just because you have a software team or UX designers does not mean the company is committed to allowing those roles to do human-centered practices or even able to move the needle for the business. If you are familiar with economics and the theory of capitalism, then you might be familiar with Adam Smith’s free market economy and the value of the invisible hand:

![A drawing of a person Description automatically generated](images/Figure_5.2.png)

Figure 5.2: Adam Smith and the invisible hand of free market economies

This idea leads to the motivational concept that companies and designers can be driven by profits. Profits and losses are something UX designers should understand how they directly impact because profits are ultimately driven by users. When you improve empathy for your users and invest in human-centered design, profits for the business will follow. In the design world, competition is a good thing, as it drives software teams to work harder and continue to improve their software. A designer learns through practice that empathy is not a baseline but rather needs to be stood up and distributed among their software teams.

[Addressing real users with real problems](#contents.xhtml_sc3_65a)

If you are a designer growing your empathy skills, the fastest way to improve these skills will be doing the hard work of speaking to real people for whom you are designing. An interesting thing about human-centered design is that, as a practice, it is additive. Designers learn from their users and then take those learnings into different software scenarios. Learning from your users is continuous, and over time, a designer can improve their instincts and their speed of understanding the problem. Design is fundamentally easier when we start by understanding our users’ needs and work with our users by gathering data to help solve them. Design is never easy, but it can be more fun when designers spend time with users. A designer can make decisions more confidently and validate their solutions throughout the process when they work with users. The world moves very fast and the difference between software that is useful, usable, accessible, and delightful and that which is objectively bad is many times determined by how user-centric a business and a design/development process is, as shown in the following figure:

!

Figure 5.3: Software factors, functional/utility, usable/reliable, accessible/credible/valuable diagram

As a UX designer, your role is to bring a human-centered point of view to your work. Do not accept no for an answer when it comes to learning from your users, and make sure you are building in time as well as budgets to allow you and your team to engage with real users. Let us discuss a few ways designers and user researchers can bring empathy gathering to our businesses and our software teams.

[Qualitative interviewing](#contents.xhtml_sc3_66a)

A UX designer is limited in their access to users and must build opportunities to learn as much as they can when they can. To do this, a UX designer or a design team should build real user participant pools. A database of real customers that a team can interact with and elicit feedback from regularly. Your user pool does not have to be complicated, as it can start by asking your potential customers or your real customers if they are willing to give feedback. You would be surprised how willing people are to share their opinion, but you do have to ask for it. A design team must also make the opportunity to give feedback easy and convenient for your participant pools. User input into the design and development process is essential, and users who are willing to participate should be thanked accordingly. When you set up a participant pool, UX designers can tap into this list of users as needed. The larger your participant pool, the better the odds that your UX designers will find connections and interact with your real users. A strong participant pool can shorten the time it takes for UX designers to get users to talk to them. A strong participant pool should feel involved and engage with the software product team, constantly stirring the pot and keeping real users connected with UX designers. Your participant pool should also be praised for helping the team improve the systems. Participant pools can be incentivized with small denomination gift cards or product discounts, but be mindful of unbalanced feedback or biased feedback values. Real users should not have to be coerced into helping.

If you are working on B2C software, your users will likely be recruited through email or in-app forms or during the onboarding process. If you work on B2B software tools, your users will likely not be your customers. Customers are those who purchase software or manage teams who use the software. A goal for working in those software environments is to have both types in your user feedback pools. Customer feedback is about feature requests or process opportunities, and user feedback sessions should focus on user needs and usability opportunities. Whether you are talking to customers or to users, the opportunity to interact with people who use your software or purchase your software is to understand their needs. Observing and interviewing people will help to uncover the opportunities whether that be new software features or improvements in the existing workflows, user interface usability, or accessibility of your systems, et cetera.

Interviewing is a difficult skill and requires a lot of practice. UX designers should be interviewing users regularly, and if they are not, they will not be good at it when given the opportunity. If you are new to talking with users, make the interviews short and sweet. Start by engaging in micro-interviews, where you ask a user 1 question and then stop. Interviews should be recorded for your records, and using teleconference technology like Zoom or Microsoft Teams can help with the recording process. With practice, you will improve your ability to elicit needs, feedback, and user insights from your users. Over time, you will find you can quickly fill 30 or 45-minute conversations with users without a problem. However, if you are new, do not try and interview users for long amounts of time. A UX designer or design researcher can work their way up to that but should start small. The following are some other tips on interviewing:

- Start with an open question:
  - Open questions encourage full answers and typically start with Why, How, or What. Why are you using x…? How did you feel when x… ?, or What do you think about x…?

- The interview is a vessel for listening:
  - Ask your question and then be silent for 20 seconds. If the interviewee does not respond in the given time. Ask the same question in another way.

  - Why are you using our software? …. (wait) …. (no answer)… Tell me a little about your day and the software tools you use in your process.

- Avoid binary questions or leading questions:
  - Binary questions result in yes/no answers. Leading questions suggest or encourage a desired answer.

  - Binary: Do you like feature X? (Yes/No prompt)

  - Leading: Why did you have difficulty with x? (Assumes the users had difficulty)

- Allow the interview to be like a bowling lane with the bumper lanes:
  - Allow the conversation to go where the user wants but guide the conversation ball down the lane and away from the gutter.

  - Wow, that is interesting… I just heard you mention x, can we return to learn more about that?

- Ask the same open question to every user:
  - Consistency is key in interviewing as you are looking for parody in your inquiry.

Interviewing users is not something you can fake or easily replace in the design process. Therefore, building your skills and participating in user interviews will improve your solutions.

| |

|----|

| Note: Interviewing users should attempt to be casual. Setting expectations with your users and putting them at ease to share and be honest takes practice. Prepare your questions ahead of time and vet them with your team or other UX design researchers before using them in your interviews. |

[Google Docs interview open questions activity](#contents.xhtml_sc4_67a)

Let us practice writing some solid questions to ask our real users.

Total time: 20 min:

1. Create a Google Docs file or other writing file.

a. Word 365, Apple Notes, Notion, Evernote, FigJam file, etc.

2. Label document: Open Interview Questions—Name—Date.

a. Get in the habit of naming and dating sharable files.

3. Practice writing 15–20 open interview questions.

a. Criteria 1: Write 5-7 open questions about user habits.

b. Criteria 2: Write 5-7 open questions about technology.

c. Criteria 3: Write 5-7 open questions about.

4. Review and rank your open question.

a. Bold your favorite open questions from each criterion or number and reorder your questions in a hierarchical list.

Interviewing your users is a sure-shot way to improve your design process, but it is hard and can be time-consuming. Be mindful that UX designers need to prioritize user research, but they also need time and support from the business to plan and execute this work. A goal of any software team is to learn from their users and not just when it is convenient or easy to do so.

[Quantitative surveys](#contents.xhtml_sc3_68a)

Interviewing users is the most direct way to understand your users, but it is not the only method for learning about your real users. Gathering data can be accomplished in many ways, whether it be through observations, surveys, user polls, diary studies, or other research methods. Your user participant pool should be given options to participate. Number 1 is participating in user interviews, and number 2 should be answering questions via qualitative surveys. Not all your user interviews will have the luxury of being collected synchronously, as your users’ schedules might not align with your interview needs. In this case, attempt to capture qualitative answers through a survey or email correspondence. Tools like Google Forms or Typeform are useful for putting together surveys for capturing short or long-form qualitative answers from users. These tools also can collect and produce quantitative results, but as an alternative to direct user interviews, a survey that allows a user to write out their point of view is useful.

Something that can be helpful in the scorch the earth user research method is that not all the users in your participant pool will be comfortable with user interviews and might be more willing to write about their experiences vs. talking it through. The importance is that information from your users is being captured and shared. Qualitative surveys should be short and sweet and be targeted to users. In some cases, qualitative surveys can be used as a follow-up to a user interview.

Let us say, as a UX designer, you have interviewed a real user. During your interview, you learned that your user is interested in growing their healthy software habits and equates software usage to eating, You are what you eat as they say, and I need to improve my software intake. The conversation continued and veered in other directions, but this idea stuck with you. As a follow-up to the user interview, a UX designer/researcher could send a qualitative survey with extended questions about other habits they participate in, for example:

We spoke about software habits, which was quite interesting, but our time got cut short. However, I would love to know more. What are the other habits that you participate in? (300–500 words) Are there any habits you want to stop? and why? (150–300 words) If you are what you eat, what are some examples of habits you are looking to start? (150–300 words) Would you be willing to be interviewed again?

Qualitative surveys need to be written in a way that encourages users to write answers and should be focused on specific topics or ideas. A qualitative survey should feel easy to consume from your real users’ perspective and hopefully get some date returns.

| |

|----|

| Note: The average person speaks at 110-150 words per minute. A qualitative survey will result in written answers that result in 600-800 words. The point here is your qualitative survey will have a significantly lower word count than a captured user interview. However, any data is better than none, so run qualitative surveys alongside user interviews to improve your empathy gathering. |

Qualitative surveys are not a replacement for user interviews but rather a way to capture data where they can. The goal should be to involve your real user participant pool as much as you can, and qualitative surveys alongside user interviews will improve a UX design team’s user data.

[Google Forms open questions qualitative survey activity](#contents.xhtml_sc4_69a)

Let us now practice building a solid survey to ask our real users:

Total time: 30 min

1. Create a new Google Form.

a. Typeform, Qualtrics, or other online survey tools, etc.

2. Label survey: Qualitative Survey—Name—Date.

a. Get in the habit of naming and dating sharable files.

3. Add 3 to 5-paragraph answer questions and/or short answer questions to your survey.

a. Criteria 1: Paragraph answer questions must be open questions.

b. Criteria 2: Short answer questions must be probing questions.

4. Review and reorder your survey questions.

5. Share your Google Form with 5 to 20 people to see if anyone responds.

[Qualitative analysis and user insights](#contents.xhtml_sc2_70a)

Now that you are getting some practice with gathering some qualitative data from your real users, the question is what it leads to. UX designers can learn many things from their users, but the role the human-centered process brings is that UX designers can coalesce this information and produce meaningful user insights. User insights are discoveries about people who can support your business in creating and sustaining better experiences. A user insight is not a solution but rather a deep truth about your users that can drive multiple solutions or become a starting point for uncovering why and how.

User insights are not easy to come by but rather are the result of analyzing patterns in qualitative data, quantitative data, changes in culture, shifts in technology, and other factors that can drive human behavior. The goal of learning from your users is to gain insight and funnel solutions through your users’ needs. A challenging thing about users is that they typically will not be able to articulate their needs with that much accuracy. Asking a user, What do you need? will not likely lead to insights about their needs for software or business. A subset of your users might be able to tell you, I need ‘x’ feature, or I need ‘y’ content, but that line of questioning will not expose the why behind those asks. However, if UX designers seek to understand their users deeply through open questions and qualitative methods, they will listen to what the user needs and learn to read through what users tell them. A UX designer or researcher will combine interview results and look for patterns in behavior, overlapping experiences, and other connective tissue. Only after analyzing your user’s data is a UX designer able to draw connections that can be articulated as user insights.

With some practice, you will learn to improve your interview analysis and user insights, but it starts with collaboration and making your results sharable.

| |

|----|

| Note: User insights are not one-and-done. They are living, breathing reflections of an organization’s understanding of its users. User insights can stay the same over time but should be validated and maintained as true through continuous qualitative research. |

[Making user data consumable and sharable](#contents.xhtml_sc3_71a)

Qualitative and quantitative data can be highly useful when trying to understand your users; however, it can also be difficult to consume. As we discussed, the average person talks at 125 words per minute. If you conducted 15 user interviews, all 30 min a piece, you have 125wpm x 30min sessions x 15 interviews = 56,250 words in terms of transcripts from your qualitative user interviews. The average reader can read 240 words per minute (WPM). Therefore, if you were just reading these interviews, it would take 4 hours (56,250 words/240 wpm/60 min) to consume this research. The reality is very few software team members will read 4 hours of user interviews. Some might, but those 15 interviews can also be shared with your team in much more consumable ways. Some ways to make this data more consumable are:

- Write and share user insights.

- Produce executive summaries of user research analysis. Quick, consumable data and top-level learnings.

- Include images of your users with their names to humanize the data.

- Data visuals of your interviews, that is., pie chart by age range (0-20 (3), 21-30 (7), 31-40 (2), 41-50 (3), 51-60 (0), 61-70, 71+ (0) or Bar charts of demographics or maps of user participants.

- Extract user needs by gender, category, or other factors.

- Cluster similar interview quotes.

- Tag your interview and create a tag cloud with a tag size representing how often that issue tag repeats in the 15 interviews.

- Quantify your interview tags as a bar chart.

- Visualize your 15 users in a spectrum showing similar users and outliers.

- Include user insights on how we might ask questions to drive ideation.

User insights are what all user research should merge into. It is difficult to share what you have learned from your users, but that is the goal of a designer or user researcher to make their research as sharable and data consumable as possible. A design team can do so much with user research, but if the business or other software team members do not also reap the benefits, that value can be lost. Luckily, UX designers are skilled in communicating and we should apply our craft to making sure human-centered thinking is not ignored. There are many tools for doing analysis, a good one is: [https://dovetailapp.com/](https://dovetailapp.com/). The following dovetail screenshot shows how quickly a qualitative interview can be given transcripts and tagged:

!

Figure 5.4: Dovetail research sample

Dovetail is a user research-focused cloud software that allows a user research team to import their user interview recordings, which get auto-transcribed, teams can then tag the interviews by time stamps and combine their experiences to create user insights. Dovetail user research software is efficient and effective for large research teams.

The goal of the tool is to speed up the analysis process and ultimately make sharing user research and user insight easier. There are a lot of meaningful advances in analysis technology, including AI-driven transcription, language sentiment analysis, and keyword tagging, that are speeding up UX designers and user researchers. An exciting thing about these tools is that they allow UX designers more time to find and discover user insights that were either harder to uncover or missed altogether. Ultimately, the goal is to do more user-centered research and to free up UX designers to spend more time with their users. When qualitative and quantitative data is made consumable through storytelling, user insights, and improved human-centered practices, it leads to improved software and happier users. Let us look at a user insight to understand how this works.

[User insight Mad Libs](#contents.xhtml_sc4_72a)

User insights are difficult and very few start insightful at all, but every designer needs to iterate their work to improve their outcomes and deliver useful insights:

- Start with this Mad Libs:

  \[User/persona\] needs to \[user/persona need\] because of \[surprising insight\].
  - Version 1: A new mother needs more nutritious food because vitamins are vital to good health for her and her baby.

    Iterate your first user insight. Improve your user needs and add more emotional factors to the user insight.

  - Version 2: A new mother with limited sleep needs to feel more equipped to manage her nutrition because stress and lack of healthy eating can lead to health risks for her and her baby.

    Keep iterating your user insight. Improve your user needs and make the insight more surprising.

  - Version 3: A new mother with a 4-month-old baby has limited sleep and needs to feel more efficient with her food preparation because lack of sleep and lack of healthy eating can produce bad nutrition habits for her and her baby.

Make sure your user insight feels surprising and does not contain an explicit solution. Is it surprising that a new mother has limited sleep? Probably not, but, surprisingly, habits started this early can have a lasting impact on both mother and child. Another nice thing is that there are many ways to improve food preparation and nutrition habits. Now it is your turn.

[User insight writing activity](#contents.xhtml_sc4_73a)

Let us now help you with writing better user insights.

Total time: 1 hour:

1. Create a FigJam board. Label user insights.

a. Create 3x sections (Shift + S):

i. Label them: User Insight Version 1, User Insight Version 2, User Insight Version 3.

b. Add a sticky note (S) to section 1: User Insight Version 1:

i. Using the Mad Lib:

ii. \[User/persona\] needs to \[user/persona need\] because of \[surprising insight\].

iii. Write your first user insight, filling in each bolded section.

c. Version 2: Copy and iterate your first version to section 2.

i. Improve your user needs.

ii. Add more emotional factors to the user insight.

iii. Reread your version 2 user insight.

d. Version 3: Copy and iterate your 2nd version to section 3.

i. Improve your user details.

ii. Make sure your user insight is surprising.

iii. Reread your version 3 user insight.

e. Share your user insight:

i. Share your FigJam file with 5 to 10 other people.

| |

|----|

| Note: Activities in this book are not meant to be one-off examples but rather are part of the process and should be practiced over time. If you put in the work on these activities now it will help in the future when you need to remember things you learned, are looking to be inspired, or just want to share how you collect knowledge. |

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_74a)

UX design has value in many businesses, but we require the ability and the mandate to work with real users along the way. Design and development can be done without it, but that is highly unrecommended. As a UX designer, you should improve your skills in qualitative user interviewing, qualitative survey methods, and user research analysis. All of this will lead to better user insights, improved learning from your users, and better research storytelling. With practice, you will find ways to drive human-centered values and user insights deep into your software products.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_75a)

- UX designers should value working with and solving problems for real users.

- Qualitative interviews are a great way to hear from your users, but they must be cultivated, supported, and nourished by a vibrant participant pool.

- UX design and user research are essential to driving user insights and should find ways to share and distribute the value of those insights to the entire organization.

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:

# Chapter 6: System Strategies

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_76a)

Now that you have some exposure to how implementing human-centered design can impact a UX designer’s process, this chapter will expose you to ways design can be relevant to increasingly complex systems. We have all been in situations where a flight was canceled due to weather or on a highway that has turned into a parking lot due to an accident. We participate in complex stems, and when they do not work, we feel it. Solving problems for users and businesses and technology demands that you be able to organize, structure, and visualize the ways our systems and people function. We will dig into information architecture and systems logic and navigate the ways your user can complete a task via a user journey.

Structure

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Information architecture values
  - Sitemap best practices

  - User flow diagramming

- UX design user scenario building
  - Write a simple user scenario activity

- User goals versus system user goals

- User journey maps
  - Communicate the system

  - Articulate the system requirements

- System requirements for solution-making

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_77a)

After completing this chapter, you should be familiar with the systems strategies that are effective in UX design. You will be able to improve your information architecture skills as you create sitemaps with best practices, improve system storytelling, and get better at articulating system requirements, which all work towards you designing better products for your users.

[Information architecture values](#contents.xhtml_sc2_78a)

As we have been discussing, UX design as a profession is supported by continuous learning. The world moves fast, and designers need to keep up to address that speed. Besides the jargon the technology world propagates, a designer must also be good at communicating the systems we ultimately impact. In many cases, because UX designers are skilled in design tools, we also help visualize our systems. Whether it be a website, applications, or software programs, they all are a series of screens, pages, or features, and all require ways to understand what is going on, how the screens are connected, where a user interacts, and why information is displayed, organized, and prioritized. Information architecture is the skill of organizing, labeling, categorizing, searching, mapping, and presenting information. It is a UX designer’s job to make information rational and understandable by users, and this starts with applying an organizational principle. A good organizational principal acronym is LATCH, which stands for Location (e.g., Paris), Alphabet (e.g., A-Z), Time (e.g., hr./min/sec), Category (e.g., Foods), and Hierarchy (e.g., Biggest in size). Refer to the following figure:

![A diagram of a diagram Description automatically generated with medium confidence](images/Figure_6.1.png)

Figure 6.1: Information architecture LATCH acronym

Information architecture is essential to designing user interfaces that are easy to navigate, consistent for your users, and provide findable and understandable content. In some organizations, there might even be the role of information architect but in many others, this responsibility has been owned by UX designers. Not all information is created equal or is given equal attention by your user and this is by design. The way information is communicated and displayed is a by-product of considering our user's mental model our user's needs, and our user's technology capabilities. Information, sometimes referred to as content, is the lifeblood of software products. How that information is managed, displayed, interacted with, or maintained is part of the responsibility of a UX designer and augmented by improved information architecture skills.

For example, a website navigation (UI Nav) is used to move a user from place to place within an interface. Once a user has completed navigating to their desired location, the navigation is less important, but has been highly informative in helping a user accomplish the goal of getting from point A to point B in the interface. If the information in navigation does not apply sound user logic and clear organization both in language and in layout, then a user will find the navigation less useable or worse, they will abandon the task altogether. Information architecture along with other user experience, user interface, interaction design, and other improvements, can hopefully keep your users from abandoning your applications.

Furthermore, software navigation within a user interface is learnable. Interfaces that are poorly designed are hard to use, become easier to use with practice, and over time, your users will get accustomed to their workarounds, workflow, or interface hacks they have learned to overcome any usability issues. The learnable nature of an interface can make modifying that same user interface more difficult. The reason is changing user navigation, even for better usability, can cause increased pain for users who have learned the old interface. Since users can learn how to use bad UI, it places a high bar on applying solid information architecture and information best practices from the beginning into your software solutions. If you do not have that luxury, which honestly few of us do, then improving your information and organization values will be your best bet for helping fix issues that stem from poor information architecture. A product is a living, breathing thing. It is not etched in stone. Luckily, any digital product can be changed, and sometimes those changes are big, macro changes like new features, new navigation schemes, new categories, or new page layouts, but most of the time, they are micro changes like updates of page titles, adding to existing categories, reordering items, or folding content together. Any change, whether it be big or small, requires applying human-centered values and improvements in information architecture. For UX design and information architecture, the values we infuse over time are what result in better solutions. If a designer does not have a clear understanding of their user's needs, then they can make a mistake in thinking information volume is equitable to improved value. However, the more options a user has to navigate, the more difficult the usability of a given interface is, a great quote dealing with this concept is:

Any intelligent fool can make things bigger and more complex… it takes a touch of genius and a log of courage to move in the opposite direction.

– Albert Einstein

![A person with a pipe Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.2.png)

Figure 6.2: Illustration of Albert Einstein shaking his finger to lecture

The job of UX design and information architecture is to have the courage to move against making things bigger, more complex, and ultimately harder to use. There is a reason it is so hard to make things simple. UX designers should look to bend the process towards clarity and mold information so that it is as simple as it can be. Let us discuss a few ways information architecture skills can assist in this clarity.

[Sitemap best practices](#contents.xhtml_sc3_79a)

As we discussed, information architecture is a collection of a number of information organizational concepts, and when applied, those concepts can be visualized. A common UX/IA strategy for visualizing information can be building sitemaps. A sitemap is a hierarchical list of pages intended to help users and website crawlers like Google navigate a website. Sitemaps are structural aids that can help give a single view of the entire network of pages. Sitemaps can be used by your users and can sometimes be found in the footer of a website, but they are more commonly used for internal design and development team purposes. They are used to help define and visualize the breadth and depth of a website experience. Unfortunately, at many businesses, sitemaps are created at one point and then allowed to decay or get lost in the junk drawer of Google Drive or any other file/knowledge management system. In many cases, sitemaps are not created very well or are hard to update and, therefore, are relegated to being ignored or, worse, discarded.

A huge strategic value a sitemap can bring to a product team is the oversight value. A sitemap is a way to see what exists, and if you are lucky, it can even help clarify what is changing in an experience.

Sitemaps can be visualized in many ways depending on how a website or product is architected, but typically, a website sitemap is laid out as a grid of columns and nested rows to represent page level. A key value of sitemaps is they should be legible and use clear logic. Whether your product is flat or multi-layered, the sitemap should be able to structure that information hierarchy. If you Google sitemaps, you will see a wide variety of executions. Unfortunately, many of the sitemaps you will see are not very good. They are oversimplified boxes with lines and add very little structural value. Here are ten recommendations all sitemaps should contain:

![A diagram of a website Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.3.png)

Figure 6.3: Good versus bad sitemap samples

Ten sitemap best practice recommendations:

- Pages should include a numbering/decimal system. Numbering should reflect page nesting or other. i.e., 0.0 (primary and secondary), 0.0.0 (primary/secondary /tertiary), etc.

- Sitemaps should define the content and page hierarchy. Big (primary), medium (secondary), small (tertiary). This should include typography and the use of color for page structure and connection purposes. i.e., the menu is green, and all food detail pages are in light green.

- Sitemaps should be read like a book. Left to right, top to bottom.

- Sitemap should only represent pages as nodes in the map. No modals, content flows, interaction states, or other non-page states.

- Sitemaps should have clear connections and easily be able to be mapped to their parent or child.

- Sitemaps should indicate templated or multi-page instances. i.e., an article could have hundreds of pages but live inside a given category, representing multiple or templated pages as a stack.

- Sitemaps should be designed via collaborative tools like FigJam/Miro/or Lucid Charts and be maintained by the entire team.

- Sitemaps should be as sharable as possible and be used for onboarding new team members, as well as during product planning meetings, feature requests, and product maintenance work.

- Sitemaps should have multiple views:
  - A simple sitemap only shows primary pages.

  - A view showing primary and secondary pages.

  - A full sitemap with all pages and all connections visible.

    Sitemaps that can be more flexible to your team’s needs will make them more useful and more sharable.

- Sitemaps should not be too time-consuming or arduous to maintain. If they are too difficult or too messy, they will not be used.

  | |

  |----|

  | Note: How do you currently organize information in your designs or for your company? If you are not using a sitemap build one and start applying these best practices. |

A website is maintained and updated over time, and therefore, so should a sitemap. Since a sitemap is not owned by one member of a team, it should not be maintained by one either. One of the fastest ways to make a sitemap irrelevant is to make it harder to update. When a sitemap is owned by one team member, that team member is a designed bottleneck. Generally, UX designers should be wary of any bottlenecks in the design process, and if you are the bottleneck then make that position aware to your leadership and work at fixing it. When a UX designer becomes a bottleneck, they will be consumed by requests from other team members to update the sitemap source of truth. Every organization should have a sitemap that is up to date. To achieve an accurate sitemap that can be done the best through a team maintenance process. If your work modifies a sitemap, then the designer/product person who executed that work should update the sitemap to reflect the solution. Every design team functions differently in this regard and maintains your sitemaps in a cloud-based collaborative software like Figma/FigJam/Miro/or Lucid Charts. Getting your team to use common tools will improve the accuracy and willingness of teams to update system components like sitemaps in the first place. Now that you have a grasp on the importance of information architecture let us practice building a sitemap using some best practices.

[Build a sitemap activity](#contents.xhtml_sc4_80a)

Let us practice some information architecture strategies:

For this activity, you will be rebuilding [https://www.thisamericanlife.org/](https://www.thisamericanlife.org/) sitemap. You could use another website or your own company but try not to start with something too complex for practice.

Total time: 30-45 min. Refer to the following steps:

1. Open Figma.

2. Create a new FigJam file.

3. Label the FigJam file sitemap This American Life v1.

a. Use the square shape (R) to create your site map page nodes (see the following figure).

i. Create primary page: number + large page title size.

ii. Create a secondary page: number + medium page title size.

iii. Create a tertiary page: number + small page title size.

Uses FigJam to create sitemap page types with page numbering, as shown in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.4.png)

Figure 6.4: FigJam screenshot of primary, secondary, and tertiary pages of a sitemap

b. Copy all the primary pages from your sample website.

i. Each primary page should get a new number. i.e., 0.0.0 Home, 1.0.0 Archive, 2.0.0 Recommended, etc.

c. Organize the primary pages from the homepage in a top-down, left-to-right orientation.

i. Using the shape arrow connector (zoom into a shape, and the arrow will appear on top/bottom, left and right), drag the arrow to connect to the next page (see the following figure).

Use FigJam to connect your sitemap page structure, as shown in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.5.png)

Figure 6.5: Screenshot of FigJam connecting a sitemap hierarchy

4. Repeat steps for each primary section, capturing every secondary and tertiary page (see the following figure).

a. Secondary pages should use the medium page size and be nested under each primary page in a column.

b. Tertiary pages should use a small page size and be nested under each secondary page as needed.

c. If a page in your sitemap is repeated, a sitemap does not need to capture every instance of pages but just represents multiple pages that exist. i.e., Articles can be represented as a stack of pages.

For sitemap content that repeats or is multiple of the same page template, use a stack of pages:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_6.6_(1).png>)

Figure 6.6: Screenshot of FigJam illustration of multiple pages stacked in a sitemap

5. TAdd all your pages to your sitemap and clean up the hierarchy and page connections:

a. Clean up any connection lines and make sure pages parent/child/ grandchild relationships are clear to follow.

b. Eliminate any repeating pages or non-page content.

c. Add color or any other system elements to help articulate the underlying information on the page.

i. Video player/download/file format could be tagged on pages.

Once you effectively number, label, and organize all your pages, you can present a logical and clear version of your sitemap, as shown in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_6.7_(1).png>)

Figure 6.7: Screenshot of FigJam sitemap with all pages connected

6. Share your sitemap with another designer or friend to review:

a. Sitemaps are built to be shared and need the eyes of other people to help them improve and to make sure they are communicating correctly.

b. Ask for reviewers to leave comments on your sitemap and request they call out areas that need clarity, are missing organization, and need more structure.

Upon completing your sitemap, you will know you have been successful if:

- Your sitemap is legible from top to bottom left to right.

- Your sitemap has a clear and logical numbering and labeling system that defines primary/secondary/tertiary pages and more.

- Your sitemap is comprehensive of all the web pages of your website or application.

- Your sitemap is organized and has a clear key.

- Your sitemap is connected both as a set of pages and to your team when shared.

  | |

  |----|

  | Note: Here is a website that has a sitemap in the footer of their experience: |

[https://www.agiledigitalagency.com/sitemap/](https://www.agiledigitalagency.com/sitemap/)

Your first sitemaps will likely not be the best version. Like any other design artifact, they require iteration and feedback from your audience and team. Sitemaps should be scrutinized and evaluated for clarity and logic. When done well, you will know it because it will be easily sharable, and your team will start using the sitemaps for broader product design decision-making. Sitemaps will be a foundation to build on and we will discuss how information architecture is consistent throughline to the UX design process.

[User flow diagramming](#contents.xhtml_sc3_81a)

Sitemaps are part of several visuals UX design can produce to help both the UX designer and broader teams understand the systems for which they solve problems. Information architecture is at the heart of defining and clarifying the information of our systems, but UX designers do so much more than just map out the systems. We use these sitemaps to diagram our solutions. A sitemap is a visual of the structure of a website, and a website is a series of web pages. String pages together, and you have a prototype and a way to demonstrate a user experience. User flow diagramming is a charting of the path a user will take as they travel through a website or application to complete a task. UX designers will build user flow diagrams to rationalize the logic and number of steps it will take a user to complete a task. To understand the correct information, time, and desired interactions a user should take with technology, UX designers build diagrams. User flow diagrams lean on sitemaps and are indispensable skills in producing user experiences that are meaningful and useful to users. Use flow diagrams are meant to illustrate the fewest number of steps and help clarify the decisions a user is required to make along a task completion.

Infuriatingly, just like sitemap, if you Google user flow diagrams, you will find a wide range of executions. Many of them will be subpar, and others will not use established system diagramming conventions. User flow diagrams that are bad typically have a few identifiable errors, like:

- The flow connection lines are hard to follow.

- The connection lines overlap or are tangled.

- Decision diamonds are unclear and yes/no exits do not use the points of the diamonds.

- Decisions no, do not loop back.

- The start and end are hard to determine or resolve. See the following sample and try Googling user flow diagrams for yourself:

![A diagram of a user flow Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.8.png)

Figure 6.8: A sample of a bad user flow

To avoid your own experiences being bad, let us discuss the conventions you should apply at the foundation, just like your sitemaps. User flow diagrams should apply the diagram key effectively. The key and shape distinction are essential for building user flow diagrams that are logical and clear to share with multiple team members:

![A diagram of a process Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.9.png)

Figure 6.9: User flow diagram key and sample

User flow diagramming best practices:

- User flow diagrams should be read from left to right, top to bottom.

- User flow diagrams should start and end with a pill, all user processes/steps/tasks should be in rectangles, all user decisions in diamonds, and all system tasks in other shapes.

- Every decision diamond should have a yes/no logic and should result in a loop. Yes and No should be connected to the directional point of the diamond corners.

- User flow should have no dead ends.

- User flow diagrams should apply sitemap information architecture labels and call out sitemap pages associated with process/task steps.

- User flow diagrams should articulate every step and decision a user takes to accomplish a task and should not hide or abstract away any user interactions with a system.

- User flow diagrams should be clear and easy to flow with clear labels and quick ways to understand where the user is and what decision they make at what point in any given process.

- Use flow diagrams should be included alongside sitemaps and other UX design or product design deliverables.

User flow diagrams are associated with logic skills and are used to determine a reasonable way your users can and should accomplish a task with any given system. As a UX designer, it is your job to care about your users and align your solution to their needs. A user flow diagram is one of the pieces you will need to understand if you have considered the best set of steps or not. They are also a great process for understanding the types of decisions your user might make when accomplishing and interacting with an interface.

Just like sitemaps, your first executions will likely not be very good. You will need iteration and perhaps multiple rounds. You will likely skip oversteps or overlook decisions your user takes along the way. This is why we design user flow diagrams in the first place. They are feedback loops into the thinking and, ultimately, the information that will go into solving a user problem. User flow diagrams should be highly collaborative and worked on as a team. As a UX designer, you might think about a solution for a user in one way without considering any other user mental model or any other alternative user path, but a team member might. If they see it differently, they can diagram an alternative or leave comments and questions on your initial diagram visual. The user flow diagram is the logic of your system visualized, and that logic will require refinement over time. When user flow diagrams are doing well, they can quickly be actioned on and converted into user scenarios, wireframes, prototypes, etc:

![A diagram of a user flow Description automatically generated](images/Figure_6.10.png)

Figure 6.10: Good user flow diagram sample

User flow diagrams pull together several system pieces and allow a design team to focus on the bird-eye view of a solution while organizing and optimizing any individual user flow. Your user flow diagrams should stick to simple shapes, easy-to-flow paths, and connections to sitemaps which are critical to involving your collaborators as well as exchanging deep knowledge about your user’s experience and creative thinking. User flow diagrams and sitemaps are essential in communicating an optimal and easy-to-use solution. If you are working with software that is difficult to use or poorly designed, user flow diagramming can also be a way to evaluate and articulate what is not working well. In my experience, businesses need to be shown alternative views into problems they might know already exist. User flow diagrams can be useful data to visualize the complexity of a process that needs to be fixed or redesigned altogether. Remember, your CEO or VP of product is not an expert at every aspect of their product and will likely need an avenue into the root of the problem. User flow diagramming is a solid strategy for getting team members up to speed on any given task as well as a great way to articulate the number of steps and the amount of time a user is required to complete it. User flow diagrams are highly useful visuals, and they should be coupled alongside a broader human-centered storytelling ethos. As a reminder, user flow diagrams are highly collaborative artifacts as no system is created perfect and your user flow diagrams can be used to articulate where improvements should be made. Your user flow diagram might be able to visualize the steps of a task, but it does not communicate why the task is being completed by the user in the first place. To tell a more complete user-centered journey, we can start by articulating a user scenario.

[UX design user scenario building](#contents.xhtml_sc2_82a)

User scenarios are part of a larger design strategy that includes infusing human-centered storytelling into every aspect of product design. As we have been discussing, sitemap and user flow diagrams help articulate a system and its logic, and a user scenario helps describe realistic situations in which your user accomplishes a designed task. It is not enough to just understand that a user goes to page X and clicks on button Y to get the desired result Z, a UX designer must also understand why a user goes to page X in the first place as well as what they might experience along their journey. UX user scenarios help a design team paint a complete and rich picture of the set of events, the context, and the practical needs and behaviors of users. At the same time, they help articulate specific context and content requirements associated with a given solution. A UX user scenario looks to apply not only the system logic supplied by sitemaps and user flow diagrams but also includes the user’s goals, motivations, and relevant behavioral demographics that might impact a given solution. The following example is a UX design user scenario from a workshop done where our team was looking to understand the needs of travelers. Our user persona, Craig Jones, was a stand-in for users from our customer research:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_6.11_(2).png>)

Figure 6.11: UX User Scenario Sample

User scenarios are an opportunity to combine all you have learned in your user research and apply your human-centered empathy more deeply. Designers apply empathy to learn, and building user scenarios allows UX designers to place themselves in the shoes of their users. Plus, they are great storytelling devices that can be used as explanations in presentations or as brief context-setting opportunities with stakeholders or unfamiliar team members. When executed effectively, they can pack a punch and help get a lot of user-centered information across quickly. UX user scenarios are typically focused on detailed aspects of a product, as they help articulate a snapshot of the total user experience and include who the user is and what their goals are in using a solution. User scenarios have a wide range of applications, and keeping them on the shorter side, allowing them to focus on quickly communicating user and product value, is recommended.

| |

|----|

| Note: Building effective user scenarios takes practice and a lot of iteration. Start by creating an initial version and share it with your team or another designer. User scenarios can be greatly improved through feedback. |

[Write a simple user scenario activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_83a)

Let us practice some design strategies and create a UX design scenario of our own. The following figure is a template that will help you think through and articulate a user scenario which is essential to solving any user interface or user experience problem:

![A screenshot of a screen Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_6.12_(1).png>)

Figure 6.12: Figma screenshot of a UX scenario template

Total time: 15 min per UX user scenario:

1. Create a new Figma file.

2. Label the file User Scenarios:

a. Add a frame (1440x900).

c. Label the frame—01-Scenario-X.

d. Divide the frame into 3 columns. Using the text tool (T) add labels:

i. Column 1: User details/Persona

ii. Column 2: UX scenario details

iii. Column 3: User goals

3. Start by articulating column 1, User details/Persona.

a. Start with the first and last name.

i. Add behavior (Age, relationship, job, education, technology usage, income, ethnicity)/Psychographic (beliefs, mental models, drivers, strives) Demographics.

4. Jump to column 3, User goals:

a. Add the goals your user is hoping to achieve in completing the scenario. Goals should start as transactional with the product or website and then dive deeper.

i. Transactional goal: Sara wants to receive a discount on the dress she has purchased.

ii. Emotional goal: Sara wants to feel part of the in-crowd and that she is getting exclusive access.

5. Return to column 2, UX Scenario details:

a. Write a scenario using your user’s name from column 1, the context in their life that has led to the experience, and what steps they take to accomplish the goals defined in column 3.

b. A user scenario should quickly establish who, what, where, when, why, and how from the context of the scenario.

c. A user scenario should lean on the sitemap and user flow diagram to establish and clarify aspects of the task a user accomplishes, and the scenario should build a broader narrative for how a user acts and thinks.

6. In the bottom section, define what the user is feeling before, during, and after they have achieved the goals set out in the scenario.

a. Define possible emotional outcomes of the defined scenario.

| |

|----|

| Note: You will be revisiting this user scenario later and will likely go back to your scenarios when solving your problems in prototypes so make sure they feel accurate and as authentic as they can. The better the scenario the more thoughtful the solution. |

[User goals versus system goals](#contents.xhtml_sc2_84a)

Now that you have some practice in creating a user scenario, there are a few competing factors in what makes a scenario good or not. If a scenario focuses too much on the product or goals of a business, it might feel forced like a user might not achieve their goals that way. User goal and system (business) goals are not always equal but can be connected, as shown in the following figure:

!.png)

Figure 6.13: Comparative table of user needs vs system (business) needs

This type of scenario is also not very realistic. As a UX designer, the goal of creating user scenarios is to infuse empathy and authenticity into the environment where the design teams solve problems. With practice, you will know when a user scenario is accurate and authentic. User scenarios that are written this way have legs, they are easily sharable, and team members, both other designers and your stakeholders, can follow along more meaningfully. User scenarios are an empathy tool, but the number of scenarios your user might encounter in any one day is nearly endless. How do you know if your user scenario is the right one? One way to tell is if your fellow designers are tracking with your scenario and feel the situation you have articulated for your user is true. A hard thing at first is your scenario might not be the right one, but that is inconsequential because, as a UX designer, you have to start somewhere. If you write out your scenario and, after thinking about it and sharing it with other UX designers, a different scenario is suggested as more relevant than creating another one. UX design thrives on iteration. Only with practice writing a significant number of scenarios, will you strike the balance between scenarios that effectively articulate the goals of the systems and technology while effectively aligning the goals of your user.

| |

|----|

| Note: Avoid the danger of an over-inflated sense of worth. UX design is fundamentally a team effort, and you should see your role as symbiotic to your team vs. hierarchical or, worse, parasitic. |

The reason you need a balance between the goals of your system and the goals of your users is that in crafting digital solutions, that is a ballpark you need to play. Designers need to understand their users and their business to such a fine degree that the scenarios they design are effective for both. The ability of designers to consider the meaningful ways a product can impact their lives as well as improve businesses is very difficult. However, a UX designer's job is to be relentless in their pursuit of doing what is right for their users. User scenarios are one of the strategies for executing and distributing better software products. Once you get a handle on writing user scenarios, then you can start moving up the experience ladder and creating user experiences that are interconnected. One good user scenario should be strung together with other great user scenarios, which ultimately leads to an improved overall user journey. User scenarios are the building blocks for thinking about and designing a total user experience.

[User journey maps](#contents.xhtml_sc2_85a)

A user journey map is a broader view of a user scenario. It is a visual representation of the steps a user takes to accomplish a task or move through an application or website. User journey maps are typically built from user scenarios and visualized as a linear map of a timeline to visualize the narrative that occurs between a user and a product or business. The following user journey map is a swim lane version:

- Process stages

- User emotions

- Stage information essential data points worth capturing

![A black and white diagram of a journey Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_6.14_(1).png>)

Figure 6.14: User journey map

User journeys are meant to articulate context within the map and typically have timeline-based categories known as swim lanes to capture user and business requirements along the user's journey. Since user journey maps are built from scenarios, they contain the following similar elements:

1. Your user/persona

2. A series of scenarios

3. High-level phases or business/user considerations

4. User actions, user feelings/emotions, user and business mindsets

5. Business and product opportunities

User journey maps come in many different shapes and sizes as well as different timelines, but they all serve a similar purpose to help articulate the needs of a user and a product together. A user journey map is a storytelling visual attempting to capture what is going on. When user journey maps are good, they become a team onboarding multiplier, allowing new team members to get up to speed with the way a user experience has been thought about and ultimately designed. They are also fun to create or validate. Like other information architecture and UX design contributions, a user journey should be built by a team and maintained in tools that allow a robust conversation as well as be highly iterated.

In its most basic form, journey mapping starts by compiling a series of user actions into a timeline. The timeline is then fleshed out with user thoughts and emotions to create a narrative. This narrative is condensed and polished, ultimately leading to a visualization that should help any party involved in creating a website understand what a user is going through and should help teams make decisions on what parts of that experience are going well and which parts are fraught with challenges.

[Communicate the system](#contents.xhtml_sc3_86a)

An important thing about design artifacts like user scenarios or user journey maps is they are reflections of the systems you, as a UX designer, are attempting to impact. Like many of the artifacts a designer can produce, they are built along a spectrum from macro (big) to micro (small). A UX designer creates design artifacts to understand and make decisions about micro, i.e., A button design, or macro, i.e., A sitemap. The ability of a UX designer to pivot between the macro and micro of a system will allow them to make decisions faster and guarantee they are applying human-centered values throughout the process. User journey maps are on the macro side of the spectrum and should be created to understand the broader environment in which your user and business participate. If a UX designer can understand and communicate a user journey in an authentic and meaningful way, then a user journey map can quickly embody the system for which a designer needs to understand how to solve problems.

[Articulate system requirements](#contents.xhtml_sc3_87a)

As a UX designer, it is important to be able to articulate both visually and in written form the ways a system works. Furthermore, being able to articulate the requirements, a product is designed to accomplish is a significant task. Requirements are a difficult foundation every designer must grapple with. The challenge is understanding what is required from a website, application, or software feature. This is our job, and by articulating the user's journey, a UX designer can start to understand what is required and why. Through mapping a user experience and communicating that to the team, a UX designer can articulate why a product should supply x content at y moment for z reason. If you are working on a team and the requirements for what you are designing are ambiguous or worse, non-existent, then this is where user scenarios and user journey maps can come in and assist. Following is a user journey map sample for customer support flow showing two users, the customer and the service agent:

![A diagram of a diagram Description automatically generated with medium confidence](images/Figure_6.15.png)

Figure 6.15: Use journey map sample: Chris R. Becker for consulting client 2022

This user journey allowed our team to identify areas in the customer journey that were not being addressed by the current process and where customer service agents had to fill in content to customers that could be communicated much earlier in the user’s journey.

[System requirements for solution-making](#contents.xhtml_sc2_88a)

As you will find with practice, the ability of a UX designer to talk about, visualize, and articulate a product system is key to doing the job. Many UX designers jump at the opportunity to understand systems as it gives them a chance to learn and ultimately deliver value. UX design is a difficult profession to nail down, and as practitioners, our greatest value is being able to attribute our caring about systems to tangible outcomes for a business. Whether that be in the form of creating design artifacts that improve products from the inside out or shifting operational practices for better requirements, UX designers can improve systems. As you move up in UX design ranks at any organization, you will find there is more and more strategic thinking involved in making sure teams produce quality solutions, and in many cases, it can circle back to how accurate are the requirements to start.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_89a)

UX design is fundamentally about understanding complex systems. Solving problems for users and businesses, and technology demands that you be able to organize, structure, and visualize the ways our systems and people function. If you continue to improve and practice your IA skills this will go a long way in improving your UX design skills and your ability to improve human-centered solutions.

In the next chapter, we will build on the values of the work designers and developers must accomplish when creating software. You explore ways of making and knowing as an ethos, and you will explore the notion of failing fast through brainstorming, ideation, and other divergent thinking strategies.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_90a)

- UX can be boiled down to business/product storytelling. Therefore, it improves your skills in designing and telling a better story about your solution through IA, sitemaps, UX user scenarios, and user journey maps.

- Information architecture is foundational to all aspects of UX design.

- UX design and IA together can improve a designer's ability to communicate a system from user and business to technology requirements.

- Information architecture and UX design can improve a designer's storytelling skills, which every designer needs to improve throughout their life.

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:
