# Chapter 7: UX Design and Failing Fast

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_91a)

Now that you have some system strategy under your belt, we can move into executing our creative strategies. In this chapter, we will build on human-centered values and do some practice work designers and developers must do when making software. Throughout this chapter, you will be exposed to various ways of designing. We will explore how design is a way of knowing, and be introduced to the idea that the design process is about being wrong, fast so that we can learn fast and iterate.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_92a)

This chapter covers the following topics:

- Failing fast ethos
  - Making as a way of knowing

  - Confront failure ideation activity

  - Figma or FigJam brainstorming activity

  - Figma brainstorming analysis and voting

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_93a)

After completing this chapter, you will get practice making which will allow you to take action and start to confront any fear of failure. You will be exposed to ideation strategies starting with brainstorming, generating ideas through low fidelity, and practicing divergent thinking from writing to drawing to speaking. Finally, you will take action by analyzing your ideas and voting for them, which all add up to you designing better products for your users.

[Failing fast ethos](#contents.xhtml_sc2_94a)

Up to this point, we have been discussing design through several vantage points: the human-centered, the user research, the information, the software best practices, the systems point of view, and more. All these points of view about the design process should help round out your skills as a UX designer. Furthermore, the fail-fast ethos will apply to every point of view of the UX design process and hopefully extend beyond. “Fail fast” is an idea that has been championed by designers, entrepreneurs, start-up communities, business circles, and more. The idea of fail fast is that a UX designer (and by proxy their software teams) can “fail” with a small set of users as they test their ideas so that they do not move those same “failed” ideas through to the broader set of users. The goal is to move only the best ideas through to your users. Fail fast is learning and making a mindset that will ultimately speed up a design and development team, as shown in the following figure:

!.png)

Figure 7.1: Fail fast

For example, I was once working on an application for a K-12 learning application. We were working on 8th-grade interactive math games and needed to test if our card-sorting exercise concept made sense. We assumed that 13-14-year-olds would be familiar with playing cards, so we quickly designed a UI based on a deck of cards, but before just moving forward with the direction, we quickly tested the idea with a few 8th graders. However, the few students we tested our direction with were left a bit confused by the card’s look and feel. This was a fail, but we were quickly able to adjust the card to have fewer playing cards and more 3x5 cards with content. See a sample of what resulted from the playing card failure:

!

Figure 7.2: Interactive Gamelet Card interaction – Chris R. Becker for consulting client 2019

Making through failure is best achieved through making prototypes. We will discuss prototyping in more depth, but at the heart of prototyping is this opportunity to be wrong and learn something. When making software solutions, a goal as a UX designer should be to validate and learn if your solutions are working. UX designers should be failing early and often through the prototypes they make. Whether you are new or old to UX design, the fail-fast ethos is worthy of maintaining throughout your career.

As a new UX designer, you might understand the fail-fast notion because your initial solutions are not that good. You are practicing, and the expectation that you design high-quality, polished solutions out of the gate is unreasonable. Do not fret about your failures as they are an opportunity to fail fast which in turn will help you learn a lot. Revel in this space and learn as much as you can. When you practice a fail-fast methodology, you will deploy fast and cheap methods to your design making. Quick-making skills, including sketching, paper prototyping, and rapid code prototypes, are used to quickly identify your failures and ultimately help guide your design and development iterations. Failing in design is not about starting and stopping, but rather about using failure to guide towards a solution and success.

Failing can be such a negative idea. A negative sentiment that has been perpetuated by our education systems around the world. For example, getting an F grade in K-12 or collegiate-level courses is seen as a signal of severe underperformance. In the context of a class, failure is assessed this way and can help a student understand their learning. A UX designer’s failure, when they are performing work, is a signal of progress. If a UX designer makes a solution and it fails to solve the issues a user is having the original solution is to modify and improve through user testing. The failure, especially if it happens early, allows a designer to quickly correct the course of the original solution. Over time, this correction is not a failure but a point of success for a business. Failure early can help avoid failure later.

For a designer, failing fast serves multiple values:

It forces designers to make a lot. When they make quickly, they fail quickly, but a designer also learns from all their failures and is a better maker because of what they have learned along the way. No one gets it right on the first try.

Failure early forces a business to understand what success looks like because businesses do not want to fail but any entrepreneur or seasoned business owner can rattle off where failures happen. In the long run, if you fail fast it is ultimately cheap and keeps a business from larger more expensive failures.

Failing fast frees a designer and development team to experiment more and spend time early on finding solutions that work. Failing fast produces a lot of options and is more about elimination. A software design team only truly fails when they stop.

Fail fast is no easy feat. A design team must make a lot of things fast and get their businesses and other team members comfortable with making them quickly as well. In software, design prototyping is essential to any agile or iterative development process. If you learn your idea will not work with a few users through building prototypes, your software team has saved a bad solution from moving into the world. The reason UX designers talk about failing fast is that fundamentally we have to make things to know if it works or not, and in the process of making a UX designer, we will make lots of mistakes. Hopefully, you can make mistakes early and, often, your ideas will quickly evolve past your failures. A UX designer should hone their skills in making mistakes via the fastest methods possible. It is recommended that you practice making via paper, whiteboards, or other low-fidelity methods as it is where a lot of early considerations fail, but a failure on a piece of paper or a whiteboard is worth

[Making as a way of knowing](#contents.xhtml_sc3_95a)

As we discussed, a UX designer should practice making and making fast. UX designers use making as a way to generate knowledge, and that knowledge is a feedback loop to the success of our thinking. Up to this point, we have been discussing how a UX designer should visualize their thinking, and the act of making is deeply connected to the success or failure of the design process. Since we make the design process visual, we can evaluate it and iterate it. Iteration is part of making but also about learning from failure and being able to modify the solutions, so they are reflections of the knowledge a design gained along the way. As a UX designer, you will need to make a lot, and only by going through the design process will you improve and get better, not only with your ideas but also with your making speed. Your knowledge will grow, but only if you are willing to make mistakes, learn from them, and improve your solutions, will you be on your way to knowing:

!

Figure 7.3: Making as way of knowing

As humans, we do not like to be wrong and as UX designers, we are leaned on to use the design process so we can reduce the chance of being wrong. Only through the process of failing can we learn, and if we do this fast, then we can avoid failing in the long run. Let us practice failing fast.

[Confront failure ideation activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_96a)

Let us practice a failing fast strategy.

For this activity, the total time is 25 minutes and setup takes five minutes:

1. Get 5 x 8.5x11 sheets of paper.

2. Fold each sheet of paper in ¼ and with pen/pencil add numbers to each ¼ section (total 4x5 = 20) plus three labels:

a. In the first two sheets, add a label at the top: Conventional.

b. In the second and fourth sheets, add a label at the top: Unconventional.

c. In the fifth sheet, add a label at the top: Unique/Impractical Ideas.

3. Set a timer for 15 mins (three minutes per sheet).

4. With the remaining time, think deeply about your experience with pizza:

5. On a notepad. Jot down all the things you know to be true about pizza delivery.

Ideation prompt and timed ideation session (15 minutes):

1. Generate ideas on… “Different ways to deliver a hot slice of pizza.”

2. Start the timer and start generating ideas.

a. Using a pen/pencil (doodle, sketch, draw, and write) your ideas as fast as you can.

i. Start with the conventional ideas (five minutes), then unconventional ideas (five minutes), and then unique/impractical ideas (five minutes).

Be mindful of the time, as the goal is to complete every page within the time limit. Do not overthink it just capture your ideas quickly.

ii. Review Ideas (Five minutes).

3. When your timer goes off, stop generating ideas.

a. Lay out your idea sheets and take a photo.

4. Review how many ideas you have:

a. If any repeat. Use your pen to make an X through the repeated idea.

b. If you completed 20 ideas, nice job; if not, review your distribution.

i. For example, eight conventional, five unconventional, and one wild idea. It might show where to spend more time.

5. Review if there are any patterns.

a. Can be helpful to cut each page into ¼ and reorganize ideas.

i. For example, five ideas all deal with delivery containers.

ii. Once you reorder your 20 ideas into groups take another picture.

6. Share your pizza delivery ideas with another designer or friend to review.

a. Ideation requires feedback and should be shared as 15 minutes worth of thinking about and generating ideas will not be very good at first.

If this is your first ideation session, you will likely not have been able to generate that many ideas in the 15-minute time limit. This is okay. You have failed, but you did it fast. 15-minute is a short timeframe; perhaps you are not experienced with pizza delivery. Whenever you are expected to generate ideas about any topic, it is recommended that you do not just jump into ideation but rather you spend some time deeply thinking about the topic. This seems obvious, but if you are ever expected to generate ideas, make sure you give yourself time before to get your ideation juices flowing.

For example, let us take an ideation session with a group of creatives at an Advertising Agency in Los Angeles. At the beginning of the 60-minute ideation session, it was asked, Has everyone had time to think about our client and the problems we are here to generate ideas about? Everyone sat silent, which is typically a bad sign. The ideation session was promptly rescheduled for the next day, and everyone was called to do their homework. The existing time was then spent thinking deeply about problems and writing out some problems that the client had not given us. The next day, we held our ideation session, and the group was more prepared and eager to deliver their ideas.

As a UX designer, you will be leaned on to be able to generate ideas and will need to practice to understand the amount of time you need to prepare. The more you prepare, the better the outcome. If you want to get good at coming up with ideas, you need to have a lot of them. To have a lot of ideas, you will need to practice, and you need to fail fast. Much of what UX designers do can be both in the realm of generating ideas as well as facilitating others whom we work with to participate and contribute meaningfully to similar ideation sessions. It is said that a good idea can come from anywhere and anyone, but that good ideas must be facilitated and pulled out of participants. Hopefully, you have willing participants but likely as a UX designer, you will be in rooms of unprepared, unpractised, and likely unskilled people who will look to you as an example of what to do. Therefore, to lead the ideation charge and be able to pull ideas out of people, you will need to practice both facilitating people and demonstrating ideas yourself. We will continue to discuss strategies for being able to consider your problems as deeply as you can.

[Ideation value](#contents.xhtml_sc4_97a)

As a Designer, much of your value will be drawn from your ability to generate ideas. Anyone can have ideas; however, as a UX designer, you should be better at sketching, visualizing, and completing your ideas from the whiteboard to the finished product. The better you get at creating ideas, communicating their value to a business, and following those ideas through to being in the hands of your users, the more value you will continue to demonstrate. A UX designer, at minimum, should be versed in generating ideas that are feasible for a business to stay competitive. At maximum, you help generate ideas that are unique, innovative and potentially open up new ways for a business to drive success.

![A drawing of a tower Description automatically generated](images/Figure_7.4.png)

Figure 7.4: Ideation Value

There are many ideation methodologies, but at the heart of each is the ability to generate ideas quickly, and then being able to evaluate the ideas once they have been generated. We will start by discussing how to generate ideas and fast. Part of the fail-fast ethos is the concept that ideas are cheap at first. Which is why we need to have a lot of them. If you can generate a lot of ideas, you can then quickly evaluate which idea will work and which will have the most impact on your users, before moving those ideas into the hands of your total marketplace. Generating ideas is part of designers participating in divergent thinking, which can be non-restricted, free-flowing thoughts and actions associated with creative thinking. Divergent thinking should be fun and playful and requires a UX designer to cultivate and set up environments that allow creative behavior to thrive. Being creative is not a magic trick, it is the by-product of practice, intentionality, and culture. Let us start with a common divergent method.

[Brainstorming](#contents.xhtml_sc4_98a)

UX designers work in teams, especially in software, and because of this, we are called upon to generate ideas. Whether those ideas are related to improving existing software features or creating new ways users can solve their problems, the role a Designer plays on a team can be tied directly to how they help generate ideas. Brainstorming is a series of methods that can help teams think about, define problems, and produce ideas that might help solve specific problems. The American advertising executive Alex F. Osborn (1888-1966), from the advertising agency BBDO (he is the O), is credited as the father of brainstorming. In his book Applied Imagination, he said, Brainstorm means using the brain to storm a creative problem and to do so in commando fashion, each stormer audaciously attacking the same objective. Creativity is encouraged by not allowing ideas to be evaluated or discussed until everyone has run dry. Sticky notes and writing as a group are effective ways to get ideas down quickly through tools like Miro, or FigJam. Online whiteboarding tools like this can capture brainstorming sessions and are quite useful for distributed teams as well. The following sample is an example of a brainstorming ideation session capturing sticky notes through the We Like…, We Wish…, We Wonder… methods where a team of 6 creatives comes together to produce ideas:

![A screenshot of a computer screen AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Image52279.png)

Figure 7.5: A Miro board with Brainstorming “What if…, We like..,, We wish… session – \

Chris R. Becker for consulting client 2022.

Brainstorming is a combination of individual idea generation and group idea synthesis. A key to brainstorming is that teams should be given a free-thinking environment. These environments can be physical, like a team conference room, or virtual, like a Figma or Miro board space. Alongside a free-thinking environment, teams should also be given time to generate ideas in a judgment-free time frame. If you are brainstorming together for one hour, then allow the first 20 minutes for the team to just add ideas. If you are doing a brainstorming session asynchronously, make sure each brainstormer is given a parking lot/area where they can add their ideas. A team that brainstorms should be given time to generate as many ideas as they can and should maximize their output the best they can.

Some brainstorming tips are:

- Brainstorm sessions should be facilitated and guided by an experienced Designer. The facilitator should keep time, model ideation behaviour, encourage ideas, and support team members who are self-editing or waning in their idea output.

- Never brainstorm on an empty stomach. Either have snacks in the sessions or plan for brainstorming to happen right after lunch.

- When ideating, create a judgment-free space through the following ways:
  - Avoid the HiPPO effect known as the highest-paid person in the room who has more authority over ideas for a product or business. They can be requested to speak last or only allowed to +1 other ideas.

  - Establish no judgment during ideation. This is both internal and external. Many new to ideation will self-edit due to being afraid their idea will be ridiculed or judged later.

- Brainstorm via multiple methods:
  - Try sketching ideas. (Encourage stick figures and simple shapes).

  - Try speaking ideas out loud (have a recorder or a facilitator capturing these ideas from each member).

  - Try writing with underlining keywords.

  - Try using analogies.

  - Try using metaphors.

  - Try using allegories.

  - Split ideation generation and ideation analysis into two separate sessions:
    1.  Ideas need time to congeal and should not be judged too quickly after they have been produced.

- Ideation should be like a purge:
  - You will likely not come up with the best ideas right off the bat and, therefore need to get some ideas out of the way. Get every idea out of your head first.

- Do not judge the idea as good or bad as it gets produced just document it and move on. Remember: No bad ideas. Just write it down.

- Encourage out-of-the-box, unconventional, and unique ideas, like:
  - Ideas that are off-the-wall or far-fetched just might be the most relevant or spark other ideas from your team. Remind brainstormers that no idea is a bad idea and just generate it.

- A facilitator should call out and acknowledge unique ideas and give kudos to team members who are stretching out and producing them.

- Encourage lateral thinking. A manner of solving problems using an indirect and creative approach via reasoning that is not immediately obvious. A facilitator should call out examples of non-obvious ideas.

- Encourage free association. A psychoanalysis method for expressing whatever is on the brainstormer's mind including memories, words, images, and daydreams, however random. The key is producing ideas and not being blocked by the purely rational mind.

- Encourage tapping into the subconscious. This might include turning off all music or distracting noises during ideation generation. You can also have brainstormers participate in mindfulness exercises before they jump in and generate ideas or have brainstormers stretch and get some blood moving before creating ideas.

- Allow brainstorming to be individual ideation and group ideation:
  - Each brainstormer should empty their ideas into a group space either via Post-it notes or digital notes. Once a brainstormer has added their ideas, have them read silently through a team member’s ideas with the same no-judgment point of view. Once they are done, have them generate more ideas for a closed amount of time (that is, 10 mins more), and on any new idea document which idea they are branching from or which idea they have taken inspiration from.

  - Ideas can be sparked by conversations, listening, watching, and being inspired by others. Allow teams to amplify each other.

- Make sure ideas are credited:
  - If doing brainstorming digitally, turn on the author tool for notes. If brainstorming, physically assign Post-it colors to participants or have brainstormers place their initials in the corner of all their ideas.

  - Place all wild ideas in a unique place in the group brainstorm space or give all wild ideas a sticker or unique Post-it color. All ideas will get regrouped during the analysis/idea judgment phase, so being able to identify them later will help see any overlap between teams and reveal connective tissue between wild ideas and others that are related or connected, for example:

  - Ideas will get repackaged, regrouped, and potentially combined with other brainstormers' ideas, so make sure your ideas are clear and legible.

- Keeping your user scenarios on the shorter side a focusing them on quickly communicating user and product value.

It is easier to tone down a wild idea than to think up a new one." Alex Osborn

| |

|----|

| Note: Building an effective brainstorming culture is on Designers as much as it is on the business who what their people to solve problems. Be wary of design teams who do not practice brainstorming regularly. |

[Figma or FigJam brainstorming activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_99a)

Let us now practice some brainstorming

Total time: 60 min session.

Set up five mins:

Many teams use whiteboarding to brainstorm so we will use a digital whiteboard in Figma via FigJam:

1. Create a new Figma FigJam file.

2. Label the file “Brainstorming Practice”:

a. Add a Section (shift + s) to make a big rectangle.

b. Label the section – 01-Brainstorming.

Thinking time: 25 mins

3. Using the text (t) tool, write out some thinking.

4. Prompt: What are common problems people have when buying products online?

5. Before jumping into brainstorming, consider the last time you bought a product online:

a. Start by thinking about the problem of online purchasing:

i. Using the Text (t) tool, add bullets for all the problems you can think of.

ii. Add all the big and small issues a user might have while buying something.

Brainstorming/Ideation time: 30min

6. Using a sticky note (S) choose a color and turn on the author feature. Drag out 10 to 15 sticky notes.

a. Brainstorming thrives under time constraints, so place time-boxes around your sessions.

b. Set a 25-minute timer.

c. Write down all your ideas quickly:

i. This should be quick. Do not spend too much time on any one idea. Write it down and move on to the next.

ii. Write / Sketch / Use keywords on your sticky notes.

iii. Ideas should focus on quantity (we will focus on quality next):

7. In the last 5 minutes add all your non-conventional, or off-the-wall ideas.

a. Use a different sticky note (S) color to indicate these ideas.

i. Remember that brainstorming is a judgment-free zone, so do not self-edit. Write it down any crazy idea.

ii. If you had some crazy ideas in the first 25 min change those sticky note colors.

iii. If you are finding it harder to come up with crazy ideas. Use this Madlib… What if… (user) … could do (x)… with (crazy idea).

8. After you have placed all your ideas in the Figma, organize them into a grid:

a. Adding ideas can be a little chaotic, so reordering all added ideas will be helpful, especially if brainstorming with a team.

[TABLE]

[Idea analysis and prioritization](#contents.xhtml_sc4_100a)

Ideation is an important skill any UX designer can bring to a team and generating many ideas is a function of being willing to fail fast. Ideas on sticky notes are cheap and fast, and many of them will not be very good. This is the point. If you are willing to put forth several ideas, a lot of them will fail, but they will fail at the earliest stage. Another important thing about generating ideas is that you should not judge your ideas too pre-emptively, although it is tempting to jot down some ideas and then switch to analyzing and judging the idea. Do not do this. The goal should be to give idea generation its own time and space. No idea is a bad idea during brainstorming but judging ideas too quickly is a bad idea. Allow the divergent thinking to occur completely and try to not interrupt it. Once idea generation has been completed and given some time to rest, then a Design team can go about analysing and prioritizing the ideas. When several ideas have been generated through brainstorming, the outcomes can feel quite jumbled. Just a lot of sticky notes peppering a conference room or Figma/Figjam boards. This is good but a Designer should now use convergent thinking tactics to add logic and order to the ideation process.

Convergent thinking is a process for identifying the best solution for a problem; it is about defining rules and logic that narrow down a set of ideas and focus a design team on fertile opportunities. For brainstorming, this starts with grouping and categorization. If you are brainstorming with a team there will likely be ideas that overlap or are somewhat similar. In this case, these similar ideas should be physically grouped by stacking the sticky notes or removing repeating sticky notes and adding a multiplier to the original idea. Grouping is then proceeded by categorizing. If a group of ideas is similar but not the same it should be given a category name.

![A diagram of a diagram of paper Description automatically generated](images/Figure_7.6.png)

Figure 7.6: Ideation prioritization

Idea analysis and prioritization are best done as a group activity and should be done together live. A UX designer can act as a facilitator for how a team should regroup and categorize ideas as well as participate in combining and connecting ideas together. If ideas are getting combined into groups that are too large, look to break those into subgroups and create parent/child relationships either through connecting lines or by drawing circles around parent and child groups. A goal of analyzing ideas is to reveal where multiple team members are thinking about the same things. If a team generates a lot of ideas around one topic, it can indicate a strong need in that area. A lot of ideas in one topic area does not mean that those are good ideas necessarily, it just indicates that multiple team members had ideas in that space. Grouping and clearly labeling your ideas can be extremely helpful in illuminating to the design team what has been considered and potentially what has been ignored.

Once a brainstorming team has reordered, combined, categorized, labeled, and added structure to their ideas, the team can participate in prioritization. Team idea voting is where a team should start. Voting can be done in many ways, but there should be rules.

The voting prioritization rules are:

- Establishes rules of voting, that is, vote only once or only 3 times, etc.

- Votes should be +1 or vote stickers dots.

- Each member has limited votes, that is, three or five. This should change depending on the total number of people participating in brainstorming.

- Members cannot vote for one idea multiple times.

- Member should not vote for their own ideas.

- Voting should be timed, that is, a 15-minute voting session.

- Every member must use one vote on one crazy idea.

  | |

  |----|

  | Note: Try to avoid voting groupthink. This looks like the VP of product voting for an idea and everyone else following that vote. You can stager team voting or voting in seniority rank so design team members do not feel intimidated to vote of certain ideas. Do this to help the culture of brainstorming and improve your team’s autonomy. |

Once a design team has voted on ideas, review the votes. Take a screenshot or photo of the initial vote tally. Voting establishes a priority list, and the next step is to reorganize your ideas into the top votes, to the least voted ideas. Following is a sample of group voting through comments in Miro:

![A screenshot of a computer screen AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Figure_7.7.png)

Figure 7.7: Miro Screenshot of idea voting using comments - Chris R. Becker for consulting client 2023.

If your team has a lot of tied voting on ideas, participate in a second round of voting but only for the ideas that have been voted for. Adding more voting will increase the range of prioritization. Make sure your second round of voting is distinguished meaningfully.

[Figma brainstorming analysis and voting](#contents.xhtml_sc3_101a)

Let us practice prioritizing ideas. (Since you have not practiced brainstorming as a team, use this voting session as practice even if it breaks one of the brainstorming voting rules)

Total time: 40 min session.

Idea Analysis up to 15 mins.

You can prioritize ideas in the following ways:

- Review your ideas from your Figma/FigJam “Brainstorming Practice.”
  - Make a copy of your section for a record as well to show progress.

In the copied section reorganize your ideas into groups, add categories, and connect ideas to the connector (x) tool.

Voting time: 20 mins:

1. Using the ellipse (O) shape tool create a voting circle. Change the color to green and use it for your votes.

a. Copy and paste (Command C + Command V) 15 votes:

2. Start a timer of 15 mins.

3. Vote on your ideas:

a. Drag your votes onto your sticky notes and group (command + g) the votes and the sticky note.

4. Vote up to five times for any one idea:

a. Use up all your votes.

b. Vote for a minimum of 1 crazy idea.

5. Take a few screenshots of your voting process.

6. Ideation Vote Priority: 5 mins.

a. Make another copy of your voted for section for a record as well to show progress.

7. In the second copied section, reorganize your ideas into a list based on your votes:

a. If there are multiple ideas with similar vote counts, add five to ten more votes to your tally to break up the ties.

b. Leave any comments (C) on your ideas for future reference as well as why the vote tally was established.

c. Take a screenshot of your final ideation:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_7.8.png)

Figure 7.8: Brainstorming sample: High Priority / High Feasibility quadrant – Chris R. Becker for consulting client 2022

[Improving ideation requires practice](#contents.xhtml_sc4_102a)

If you are new to brainstorming and ideation, you will find that your ideas may not be good, unique, or novel. This is okay. You are new and you have not practiced coming up with ideas very often. However, you have gotten some practice producing thinking, making it visual, reorganizing it, and adding priority to those ideas through voting. All these skills are unique to the design process and important to improving your brainstorming capacity. Brainstorming and being creative is not like a light switch that you can just turn on and off when you want. It is more like a dimmer switch; at first, your ideas will be dim, and you will not have enough time to turn up the intensity of your thinking or your idea-making. Luckily, practice is here, and as a UX designer, practicing is essential to your job. Learning anything new requires practice. In UX design, practice is the process, and you have to make ideas to know if they are good or not. A UX designer generates knowledge through making, and if you fail early and often in your ideation, you can learn to produce more ideas that are not failures but rather worthwhile solutions for your users and your business. You can only learn to do this with practice. Over time, you will get better at generating ideas quickly as well as moving those ideas through the process and into the hands of your users and your business, which we will continue to practice in the next chapters.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_103a)

In this chapter, we learned that UX design is fundamentally about learning from the process, and UX designers should be adept at making fast and failing fast to learn and iterate. There are many ways to fail fast; however, brainstorming ideation is a good method to practice. As UX designers, we should be practicing divergent and convergent methods when solving problems, and only with practice can we generate unique, novel, and high-value solutions for our users and our businesses. You will be called upon as a UX designer to generate ideas and be adept at brainstorming, but this is a learned skill and requires practice. Failing fast is a way to learn quickly, which is why you are here. By improving your ideas through practicing brainstorming and failing fast, you will improve and only through practice will you be comfortable with executing and succeeding under pressure.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_104a)

- UX Brainstorming is one of many divergent thinking strategies and should be practiced regularly individually and as a team.

- UX design can facilitate idea generation and should be experienced in producing ideas in abundance. If you have lots of ideas, then you have more options to fail fast.

- UX designers should practice ideation but also show how they can organize and add priority to ideas through organization and voting.

[References](#contents.xhtml_sc2_105a)

1.  Applied Imagination: Principles and Procedures of Creative Thinking by Alex Osborn

# Chapter 8: From 1 to 1.1, Prototyping First

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_106a)

Now that you have some practice coming up with some ideas through brainstorming, let us jump into moving those ideas forward. Throughout this chapter, you will be exposed to ways to rapidly design through sketching, be introduced to paper prototyping, and rapidly test your quick idea through Figma. These skills are particularly needed in our fast-paced, ever-changing world, and companies from Apple to Google to artificial intelligence (AI) startups are all looking to hire UX designers who can produce solutions quickly through sketching and prototyping.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_107a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Paper prototyping
  - Paper prototype activity

- Rapid paper prototyping into a testable format
  - Building a clickable paper prototype activity

  - Testing Figma clickable paper prototype activity

- Getting your ideas to your users

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_108a)

After completing this chapter, you will get practice prototyping, which will allow you to take action and test your ideas. You will be exposed to brainstormed ideas into paper prototyping, generating ideas through low fidelity, and practicing evaluating your solutions through testing, which all add up to designing better products for your users.

[Paper prototyping](#contents.xhtml_sc2_109a)

As we discussed in the previous chapter, UX designers should learn to fail fast. To take ideas and learn if they work or not, we must make something to test. If a designer can move their ideas quickly into the hands of their users, then they can quickly validate if what they are designing will work or not. An efficient way to design quickly is to use materials that are equally fast to manipulate. Paper prototyping relies on the use of paper and pen/pencil., as shown in the following figure:

![A group of paper with a graphic design AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Figure_8.1.png)

Figure 8.1: Paper prototyping sketching example using a paper template from UI stencils

A UX designer can quickly render a solution with paper and quickly approximate the technology size through sketching. The key to sketching is giving yourself the luxury to think through the options. Producing multiple sketches and iterating your sketches from low fidelity to high fidelity into a working product prototype is the essence of UX design. All of the design process is a type of sketching. The better you get at sketching, the faster you can render ideas into user interfaces and better software. A key to paper prototyping is that the process is about speed. If you are not very skilled at sketching, then start practicing. Anyone can learn to sketch, and a key piece to understand is that sketching is thinking, but it also needs to be practiced. A UX designer must practice sketching and not rely only on words alone. As designers of software interfaces, we ultimately need to draw. Somewhere along the way, there is the idea that using creative software, Adobe XD, Illustrator, Photoshop, Sketch, Figma, etc., is not drawing. It is drawing, and if you sketch first using a piece of paper, you will ultimately execute the drawing through software more effectively and efficiently. Many software tools are inadequate sketching tools as they lack speed and fluid mark-making at the core of their experience. Luckily, we have paper and pencils, and they are affordable and readily available. Practice with your tools is important as a UX designer, and the more practice you do with sketching, the better and faster you will generate and execute your ideas. With practice, you can get faster at communicating how a system works and start sketching through interactions:

![A diagram of a prototype Description automatically generated](images/Figure_8.2.png)

Figure 8.2: Paper prototyping tools

Executing ideas is hard, and the ability to make the process more participatory, fast, and validatable through prototyping and testing is why you should sketch more. Let us practice executing a paper prototype.

[Paper prototype activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_110a)

Let us practice a paper strategy. For this activity, the total time required is 1.5 hours, with the setup time being five mins. Perform the following steps:

1. Get 3 x 8.5x11 sheets of paper, a pencil/pen, and a straight edge or ruler.

2. Using your ruler, draw 2x rectangle dimensions of an iPhone 15. (6.1 inch / 2.78 inch) per sheet.

a. Option B: Trace your smartphone 2x per sheet of paper.

3. Add numbers and title spaces above each screen.

Let us now see the steps for the paper prototyping prompt (25 minutes):

1. Prompt:

a. Option one: Buying coffee beans from Kava Coffee… (We will use this for the demo)

b. Option two: Use one of your ideas from your brainstorming session.

2. Start with an outline:

a. Using a pen/pencil, write out a user goal to the left of your screen:

i. Our user needs to select a bag of medium-roast coffee and add it to their cart.

b. Write out the potential steps involved in selecting and buying coffee beans:

i. The user reviews the coffee types\| 02. The user selects medium roast \| 03. The user reviews 4 options for medium roast \| 04. The user selects the Ethiopian variety of coffee \| 05. The user adds coffee beans to their cart.

3. On your first screen, during step 01. User reviews coffee types, create an online for your screen. Be as specific about information as you can. The more details in your outline, the better.

a. For example, each option has an image of the coffee bag, a title, a short description of the coffee, a map of the coffee flavor profile, Tags for flavor types, Price + tax, and an Add to your cart button

b. The Homepage has the following:

i. Logo + Navigation Menu (Hamburger + Shopping Cart)

ii. About Kava Coffee: Image of roasting practices & coffee.

iii. Eight types of coffee: Caffeinated and decaf for 1. Dark-roast, 2. Medium-Roast, 3. Light-Roast, 4. Espresso Beans.

iv. Footer: Logo, address, phone number, email, and copyright.

4. Repeat the following for the remaining Screens 02 – 05:

a. Never sketch a screen without a clear outline of what information makes up that screen.

b. Establish a clear happy path through your paper prototype steps and then sketch your screens.

Paper prototyping sketching onto your screens (50 minutes):

1. With your outline to the left/right of the screens. Start sketching out with simple boxes, lines, and limited text what your mobile screen might look like.

a. Use a pencil for speed and to reduce mistakes.

b. Use boxes with an x in them to indicate images, horizontal lines to represent copy sections, and clearly draw out and add call-to-action labels to interaction buttons, navigation, etc.

c. Sketch out your screen top down just as you have outlined it.

d. Add content where needed to help communicate context.

2. Repeat and sketch the other screens in your flow:

a. Make sure to copy the basic layout from screen to screen and include a consistent button design. For example, if your CTA’s are pills make them pills everywhere. If your logo is in the upper left and navigation the upper right all screens get that treatment.

3. Do not be scared to trace your home screen to reduce errors. Make sure you are keeping consistent screen elements in the same place.

4. Review your screen flow:

a. Make sure all screens have been sketched out and add labels to keep them organized.

b. Add any last touch-ups or copy additions to add clarity to your sketches.

Paper prototyping digital capture (10 minutes):

1. Now that your screens are sketched out. Use your smartphone to capture photos of each screen:

a. Take photos in the order you sketched them.

b. Crop each photo so that it is just the screen space of the box you have drawn for the screen.

2. Copy your images and place them in a folder on your hard drive in a folder called Paper-prototyping-practice:

a. Relabel each image to match your screen titles from your paper sketch, i.e., 01-Coffee-Types, 02-Select-medium-roast, 03-medium-roast-summary, 04-Ethiopian-Details, 05-Add-coffee-to-cart.

b. We will be using these images later, so keeping them organized and labelled to match your sketches is essential.

Paper prototyping should not be an overly time-consuming process. If you are spending hours sketching out your screens, it could be a sign you need to practice sketching more, or your screen outlining and understanding of the problem is not sufficient. If this happens, it is recommended to stop and work on defining your experience so that you can sketch your prototype faster. Paper prototyping is a UX designer's opportunity to complete an idea and make a software user interface tangible. At first, your paper prototype sketches might not be great. The sketches might feel rushed, sloppy, or missing content. This is fine because you are new and have not participated in the process often. However, like any craft skill, it requires practice. Paper prototyping should be fun and allow a UX designer to stretch their understanding and hopefully engage in experimentation.

[Rapid paper prototyping into a testable format](#contents.xhtml_sc2_111a)

Prototyping is an iterative process and should help a designer move from low-fidelity to high-fidelity and lead to product releases, as shown in the following figure:

![A sketch of a smart watch Description automatically generated](images/Figure_8.3.png)

Figure 8.3: Paper into digital prototypes

As a UX designer, much of your value will be drawn from your ability to generate ideas. Anyone can have ideas; however, as a UX designer, you should be better at sketching, visualizing, and completing your ideas from the whiteboard to the finished product. The better you get at creating ideas, communicating their value to a business, and following those ideas through to being in the hands of your users, the more value you will continue to demonstrate. A UX designer, at minimum, should be versed in generating ideas that are feasible for a business to stay competitive, and at a maximum, you should help generate ideas that are unique, innovative, and potentially open new ways for a business to drive success. A paper prototype is the first step in many iterations, and a UX designer should not treat any sketch with too much reverence. If the sketch sucks, then sketch it over again or go in another direction. Your sketches can and should fail, but if they are failing early, then a designer is fundamentally saving themselves and their software team time, and time is money to a business. Paper prototypes can also be tested in their paper format but with the advancement of digital prototyping software, they should quickly be added to a digital format that can be tested and turned into clickable paper prototypes. There are many digital prototyping tools, but we will continue using Figma to stay consistent. However, as a UX designer, it is highly recommended to experiment with multiple tools. Let us practice.

[Building a clickable paper prototype activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_112a)

Let us practice converting our paper sketches into a clickable prototype using Figma.

The total time required is one hour

The time required for Figma setup: (Five minutes)

Perform the following steps:

1. Create a new Figma file:

a. Label the file Kava Paper Prototype.

2. Add a Frame (F) and select a phone. Choose iPhone 14 and 15 pro (383x852):

a. Select the new frame. Hold down the option key to copy and drag to the right.

b. Repeat making copies for the five frames of the paper prototype.

3. Organize your frames into a row in the following way:

a. Select all screens and, using the align tool in the design right panel, align your frames to the top. Then, distribute them evenly.

4. Double-select each frame title and update them to match your paper prototype screen titles.

5. Start with the first frame in the row and match the same numbering 01-Coffee-Types. (Use – in your titles, as frame titles will get used during file exportation out of Figma)

6. Select your first frame:

a. Select the fill swatch to pull up the Fill Modal.

b. In the fill option, select the Image option.

c. Select the Choose image.

d. A local file finder will be launched. Navigate to the folder on your hard drive where you saved your paper prototype images.

e. The image preview will get loaded. In the fill dropdown, select “fit” and close the Fill Modal.

7. Select the next frame and repeat step 5:

a. Add all your paper-prototype images to your frames.

b. Now, you are ready to prototype your frames.

| |

|----|

| Note: In Figma, you can also drop our images onto the frames. However, this method will copy the image into the file rather than link the image content back to the source. For paper prototyping, this is key because if you sketch new screens, you can merely replace the image on your hard drive image folder, and Figma will automatically upload it as long as it has the same image name. |

Wire up your paper prototype (30 mins):

1. All your frames should have the loaded paper sketches.

2. Select the prototyping mode in the upper right (next to the design).

3. In the Prototype mode Device section, change the dropdown from none to iPhone 15.

4. Select your first frame and add a Flow Starting Point:

a. Double-click Flow 1 and update to 01-Kava Medium Flow.

b. Flow starting points can be added, deleted, and renamed and are helpful for working with more complex prototypes.

5. Next, connect your screen together with the node connectors:

a. Select the + node on the right middle of the frame, drag the line out, and connect it to the second frame through the following ways:

i. In the interaction dialog, make sure the left dropdown says on click and the action states navigate to.

ii. The screen navigation should also match the frame you connected your node.

b. Repeat on frame 02 and connect to frame 03:

i. Repeat for the remaining frames.

ii. On the last frame, make the connector loop back to the first frame.

Select all your screens to reveal your paper prototype flow. If all connections are correct, go and test your prototype.

6. In the flow indicator, select the play icon or select the present icon in the upper right:

a. An iPhone prototype emulator will load. Click through the flow of your paper prototype screens.

b. Go back and clear up any frames.

c. Make sure your screen flow progresses the way you intended.

7. Next, we will share this clickable paper prototype.

At first, getting your paper prototypes into a clickable flow might be slower since you are likely new to prototyping features in Figma. However, just like paper prototyping and other aspects of UX design with some practice, you will be able to quickly set up, oversee, and maintain your prototypes digitally. A key aspect of putting paper prototypes into digital tools is that these prototypes can then be tested more readily. As we have been discussing, a UX designer should look to fail fast and be able to place their ideas into the hands of their users faster as well as facilitate this. Clickable paper prototypes can be shared more easily, and design teams can collaborate and iterate the solutions faster as well. By making an early idea tangible, the team and your users can use it, which is fundamentally what a UX designer is looking to do. Now that you have a clickable paper prototype, let us share it with a few people.

[Testing Figma clickable paper prototype activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_113a)

The total time required for this activity is 40 minutes.

The setup requires 10 minutes. You can follow the following steps to perform the activity:

1. Open your Kava Paper Prototype Figma file.

2. Click on the prototype feature, and do the following:

a. Review your prototype flow from the previous activity.

b. Add some polish to each of your interactions. Not every screen transition should be instant in the dropdown of some of your connections update to move in or push.

3. Select the Play button and click through your prototype to run through your paper prototype. If everything works as intended, then you are ready to share it with a user.

4. Open the Figma app on your phone. If you have not downloaded the Figma app, go to your smartphone app store, install the Figma app and Login.

a. In the Figma app, choose your active file. Kava Paper Prototype.

b. In the upper right of the app select the … icon and hit the view prototypes play button.

c. Click through your paper prototype on your phone.

Mobile user testing (30 minutes):

Now that your prototype works on your phone, let us see how people use it. Ask a friend, significant other, or colleague if they have 10 minutes free and tell them you just need to get their opinion on something, many people are happy to help, especially if the ask is short.

1. With your clickable paper prototype loaded on your phone, hand your phone to your user tester:

a. Tell them- I am working on x, I have built this paper prototype and I am looking for your feedback. Can you please try and do x. Ask your user to do the task your paper prototype was designed for.

b. Hand your phone over to your user and watch them.

c. Position yourself so you can see your user’s face as well as what they are doing on your phone.

d. Be quiet while your user tester clicks through your short paper prototype.

a. Take out a notepad and take some notes. Record any moments your user pauses, looks confused, or looks for assistance as they click through your experience.

When your user has completed the steps, ask for the phone back. If you have an audio recording app on your phone, open it and start recording.

2. Have a post-testing conversation. Ask your user the following:

a. What do you remember about my paper prototype?

b. Were you able to accomplish my task? if not why not?

c. Is there anything missing or something you wish you would have had in the experience?

d. Would you be interested in doing this process again? If not why not?

e. How did this prototype make you feel?

f. Stop the recording.

3. Thank your user and take down any more notes from your conversation. You can document the following:

a. Document how user testing your paper prototype made you, the designer feel.

b. Document what you wish you had or what you think you missed during the user testing session.

c. Document any other insights you gained along the way, including the technology usage, feedback quality, and testing success or failure.

| |

|----|

| Note: User testing will be discussed more deeply but it starts with wanting to validate your ideas. It can feel hard, but with practice, you can quickly get your ideas in the hands of real users. A UX designer should look to practice user testing as much as they can as well as improve their user testing environments which can make facilitating user testing easier and faster. |

[Getting your ideas to your users](#contents.xhtml_sc2_114a)

Sketching and paper prototyping are about making something that is built for speed. It is about making your ideas tangible and validating your solutions early and often. It is deeply connected to the fail-fast ethos, and the value you will find it created for the design process is immense. So much of UX design is about making some level of assumption. If a UX designer can get their ideas in the hands of their users early and often, then the assumptions they are making can be validated or invalidated over time. The faster a UX designer can invalidate ideas, that is, fail, the better a user experience will be because those ideas will never make their way into a product but rather be part of the maturing both of a UX designer as well as of a product. A paper prototype is cheap and should be created quickly, and equally, as well as it should be tested quickly. User testing can help you quickly validate an idea, and hearing directly from your users means spending time with them:

![A person and person sitting at a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_8.4.png)

Figure 8.4: Validation user testing – Get your idea into the hands of your users

All this making speed will feedback into a UX designer's process with much more efficiency. UX designers who can ideate, design, and test their solutions through prototyping are the ones who ultimately get the best ideas into the hands of their users. Paper prototyping is just one of the prototyping steps as well, and when a UX designer can navigate ideas quickly through sketching and prototyping, then they are aligning themselves with the fail-fast ethos and can learn more from their users because they are fundamentally testing all their ideas through them. A UX designer must be user-centered, and to action this idea we must test our ideas at every point along the process with our users. Paper prototyping is a fast way to start, and it is encouraged that you keep practicing this step throughout your career.

UX design is about making ideas tangible, and through practicing paper prototyping, you will experience failure as it is highly unlikely that your paper prototypes were successful. If they were unsuccessful, it would allow you to learn from your users and seek out feedback on what needs to improve. Luckily, your paper prototypes are not precious and can be modified, iterated, or even burned in the fire or thrown away. Either way, a paper prototype creates a dialog between the UX designer and the user, and this is the beating heart of UX design. Go forth and test your prototypes early and often; the earlier, the better, which will also help you in practicing the fail-fast ethos we have discussed previously.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_115a)

UX design can impact businesses and software products in numerous ways, but much of it is rooted in driving empathy deep into the design and development process through learning from prototyping. From validating ideas and testing them in paper prototypes, through to the countless iterations as your software solutions make their way into the hands of your users.

In the next chapter, you will build on prototyping skills as you continue to evolve and iterate your paper prototypes through user interface design principles. We will continue our user testing ethos through testing out mid-fidelity executions as well. Keep making rapid progress on your ideas through sketching and rapid user testing.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_116a)

- Paper prototypes are a starting place and not a final solution.

- UX design should practice making that can get ideas into the hands of their users and fast.

- UX designers should lean on tools when needed but should continue to make things quickly so that they can be tested quickly. Paper, pens/pencils are very fast so do not abandon these tools for digital ones altogether.

Join the book's Discord Workspace for Latest updates, Offers, Tech happenings around the world, New Release and Sessions with the Authors:
