# Chapter 9: From 1.1 to 2.0 UI Prototyping

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_117a)

Hopefully, the paper prototype from the last chapter revealed some things to fix when you shared it with a friend, colleague, or user. If it fails altogether, good because you can quickly sketch and test a new one, and hopefully not make similar mistakes. Every software solution is the outcome of many failures and iterations along the way and UX designers who practice making quickly can make sure their ideas are moving forward, improving over time, and ensuring their businesses are truly getting what they need to be successful. The early stages of a paper prototype can reveal the path, and a UX or UI designer can take a software solution through a refinement process, which we will start to practice. The refinement of a user experience is accomplished through the development of a user interface.

Throughout this chapter, you will be exposed to ways to take your ideas, either as paper sketches or low-fidelity (lo-fi) prototypes, and rapidly digitize them into high-fidelity (hi-fi) versions. You will be introduced to using existing UI design libraries and rapidly testing refined mid/high fidelity prototypes via Figma. Let us continue to practice.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_118a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- UI design prototyping

- Design systems
  - UI design prototype activity

- Explore design system components
  - Review design system documentation activity

  - Becoming an expert in documentation

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_119a)

After completing this chapter, you will get practice developing a UI design and prototyping in mid/high fidelity, which will allow you to act and start to test your ideas. You will be exposed to user interface principles, gain experience with existing design systems, and practice digging into software documentation for usage and better solutions. The iterations and refinements made in this chapter will continue to be built upon to improve your software skills. Although UI design is a highly specialized set of skills that requires a lot of practice and the infusion of many other skills, including graphic design, user psychology, technical knowledge, visual design, branding expertise, and more.

[UI design prototyping](#contents.xhtml_sc2_120a)

As discussed in the previous chapter, UX designers should be prototyping first and using their solutions with users to validate and help a software team move solutions in the right direction. The speed at which a UX designer can create and solve problems in software is an outcome of practice. Since some of you are new to UX design, your paper prototypes may have a lot of room for growth. Believe it or not, this is a good position to be in because how ideas iterate and refine themselves over time is a testament to a designer’s determination and grit. Trust me, you will not solve your users' problems as the first time it will require a lot of feedback. It is advisable to never get into the mindset as a UX designer that you are right, since only your users can confirm if you are on the right track. When designing software, validating ideas through prototyping will allow you and your team to keep your ideas human-centered. The more early feedback you receive, the better, as the iterations and refinements you can make can be applied through higher and higher levels of fidelity. Fidelity in UX and UI design is a quality indicator for the level of accuracy and design detail revealed in a prototype or design solution. Lo-fidelity can be anything from a sketch to a paper prototype (like we have created) through to a digital wireframe. Mid-fidelity has a wide range of executions depending on the team, the content definition, and a business's or client's ability to make software decisions. Mid-fidelity starts to apply typography hierarchy, applies iconography, limits color decisions, and starts applying user interface standards. In the later stages, we move low-fidelity and mid-fidelity into high-fidelity, which is seen as a completed product with the final copy, final imagery, and final user interface design decisions. High-fidelity prototypes should be about polishing and not about making big changes in solutions. As you get more practice with user interface design, you will improve your speed in creating solutions at higher and higher fidelity, however, anyone who has done UI design for a while will tell you it takes some time. Moving from low-fidelity to high-fidelity is iterative prototyping and it is the only way to improve your speed and accuracy over time, as shown in the following figure:

!

Figure 9.1: Fidelity in UX and UI

Generally, it is not advisable to start solving problems through high-fidelity. Since high-fidelity is more time-consuming, it is advisable not to jump right into making your solutions through this method. As we have been practicing, the UX design process is fundamentally iterative. Like evolution, designers can take ideas from base elements into globally scaled solutions. Furthermore, a design that follows this evolution will be far more successful and move through the decision process faster. Evolving ideas through iteration is hard, and the ability to make the process more additive through user feedback and improved solution fidelity is why UX and UI design brings these skills to our software teams. As you will experience, a user interface (UI) is the tangible outcome of foundational thinking and human-centered focused work. The goal of any UX designer should be to make sure that a user interface is solving the identified problem. To do this effectively, a UI designer must adhere to many software standards and apply their craft to all elements of a software product. In our global software environment, this means delivering solutions that can scale and are useful from Toronto to Timbuktu, and to do this effectively, it will require working with design systems.

[Design systems](#contents.xhtml_sc2_121a)

Design systems come in varying sizes and qualities and are a library of reusable assets, UI design elements, UI design components, software page templates, design standards, branding elements, and more. Design system elements are the pieces of a puzzle that can be assembled into a near-infinite number of software solutions. A design system is an assembly of design patterns and rules for making a user interface consistent, useful, and critical to creating user experiences that meet your user’s needs. As UX designers, there are billions of ways to assemble any user interface where decisions about layout, color, shape, pattern, typography, iconography, gestalt principles, interaction principles, UI design affordance, accessibility, usability, UI design consistency, and more all come into play. A design system is a process for codifying the rules and distributing the tools among global teams so that software solutions all look and feel similar even though their user and their designers are different. There is a reason a user in Germany can use Google Software and find Google Software in America similar. The base language might be different, but the UI design system is all the same. Along with many other global software leaders, they have been applying design systems thinking to their software and development teams since before the proliferation of the internet. Google uses Google’s Material Design, and a majority of software companies use their design system versions. Check out [https://designsystemsrepo.com/design-systems/,](https://designsystemsrepo.com/design-systems/) where you can explore the design system documentation from Airbnb to Oracle to Apple, and more. Design systems are a collection of building blocks like Lego pieces. We create user interface components that can be assembled in various ways to help solve defined user problems within software or web applications, as shown in the following figure:

!

Figure 9.2: Design systems a kit of parts

As a growing UX or UI designer, it is important to recognize that software runs our world, and the better the design systems, the more efficient and scalable those solutions can be. Some of you might even go on to work directly on design system teams and be a part of helping improve the systems of your software tools, or some of you might go on to work with a design system team. If you are designing a software solution, you will be designing a user interface, and therefore you will be designing a design system. A design system can be created by a small team or by large organizations. We will explore Google’s Material Design in more depth and understanding that Google has 1000s of designers and 10,000s of engineers will help in your understanding of the application of these resources. A lot of work by a lot of people has gone into many of these design systems, and we can capitalize on and grow with the application of their design systems and hardware platforms.

[UI design prototype activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_122a)

Let us practice creating a digital prototype using the Material Design System library. For this activity, we will be relying on existing UI design documentation and Figma design system libraries.

The Figma Material Design 3 Resource can be found at the following link:

![A screenshot of a web page Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_9.3_(1).png>)

Figure 9.3: Figma Material Design Community file screenshot

[https://www.figma.com/community/file/1035203688168086460/material-3-design-kit](https://www.figma.com/community/file/1035203688168086460/material-3-design-kit)

The UI documentation is as follows: [https://m3.material.io/](https://m3.material.io/)

The total time taken for the activity is 2-5 hours.

Refer to the following steps to add the Material Design Library, which will take 10-15 minutes:

Open your Kava Paper Prototype in Figma.:

1. Go to Material 3 Design System Resource Kit in Figma:

[https://www.figma.com/community/file/1035203688168086460/material-3-design-kit](https://www.figma.com/community/file/1035203688168086460/material-3-design-kit)

2. Make a copy of the Material Design 3 Kit:

In the file name, move your copy out of drafts and into the same project as your Kava Paper Prototype Figma project. Your design system library files should be in the same project folders, especially if you are working with an individual vs organizational license in Figma.

3. Open the Material Design 3 Kit file:

a. Under the (v) carrot, next to the file title, select Publish Library.

i. Allow the library progress spinner to finish publishing.

4. Next, open your Kava Paper Prototype file:

a. Select the Figma logo/main menu, and under the dropdown, choose Libraries from the list.

b. In the libraries modal, find Material 3 Design Kit and select Add to file.

c. The Material Design library will be added to your Figma assets in your Kava Paper Prototype File.

5. Set up a digital prototype page:

a. Control-click your Kava paper prototype page and choose Duplicate.

b. Relabel your new page as Kava Material Prototype.

6. Relabel your paper prototype page labels:

a. Add M3 to the end of each page title.

UI design prototype using Material Design (1-3 hours)

Next, we will be using the Material Design library to rebuild our paper prototypes through digital UI and execute a digital prototype.

1. Start by adding a layout grid or your frames:

a. Select Assets \| Materials 3 Design Kit \| Styles/Layout.

b. Find the extra small component and select the 4-column grid.

c. Copy and paste the grid onto all of your frames.

d. Lock the grid layer on all your frames.

2. Start with Frame 01:

a. Using the Assets \| Material 3 Design Kit \| Components folder.

i. Start rebuilding your paper sketch. Working top down.

ii. Add components from the Material Design library that are closest to your sketches. Include navigation, buttons, tabs, etc.

iii. It can be helpful to drag out all the components you will need onto your Figma canvas and then assemble them onto your frames.

iv. Resize your elements to approximate and match your underlying paper prototype sketch.

b. Add-Type/labels, as it will all be generic:

i. Go through and update all labels, buttons, and other places of context on your digital screen.

c. Add imagery where needed:

i. Replace all generic image placeholders with contextual images.

3. Repeat the UI design for the remaining Screens 02 – 05:

a. Complete 01 screen from top to bottom before jumping to the next screen.

b. Copy and paste any relevant UI design element that transfers between screens. i.e., navigation, footer, UI design buttons, etc.

Wire up your Digital Material Design Prototype (30 min) through the following steps:

1. Select the prototyping mode in the upper right (next to Design).

2. In the Prototype mode Device section, change the dropdown from none to iPhone 15.

3. Select your first frame and add a Flow Starting Point:

a. Double-click Flow 1 – and update to 01-Kava-M3-Start.

b. Flow starting points can be added, deleted, and renamed and help work with more complex prototypes, which we will do in the future.

4. Next, connect your screens and interactions with the node connectors:

a. Select the UI design element that will be interacted with, i.e., a button. Use the + node on the right middle UI design element, drag the line out, and connect it to the second frame.

i. In the interaction dialog, make sure the left dropdown says click, and the action states navigate to.

ii. The screen navigation should also match the frame you connected your node.

b. Repeat the same on all UI design elements that can be interacted with that move to frame 02 and connect to frame 03:

i. Repeat for the remaining digital frames.

ii. On the last frame make the connector loop back to the first frame.

5. Select all your screens to reveal your material 3 prototype flow. If all UI design connections are correct, go and test your prototype.

6. In the flow indicator, select the play icon or select the present icon in the upper right:

a. An iPhone prototype emulator will load. Click through the flow of your digital prototype screens.

b. Go back and clean up any frames.

c. Make sure your screen flow progresses the way you intended.

7. With your digital prototype clickable, share it with a colleague, friend, or loved one for some feedback. Just like our paper prototypes, digital versions should also be created to be tested.

| |

|----|

| Note: Prototyping in all its forms from paper through to high-fidelity prototypes and functional prototypes (we will discuss these later) should be used for user testing. As a UX designer or developer, there is a lot to learn from making and putting solutions in the hands of our users, and remember your clients or executives are not your users. (although allowing them to use prototypes as well can be useful for demonstrating value). |

Digital prototyping should be about making progress and working towards a final solution. The faster you can get at working through a UI design process and assembling your solutions into prototypes, the more opportunities you will have to test the ideas. Just like your paper prototypes, a digital prototype is a step closer to the idea and will garner you and your team more effective feedback during user testing. If you are spending hours upon hours assembling your screens, it could be a sign you need to practice more or that your design system is not robust enough to address the issues. In this case, pause and clearly define your experience so that you can use the design system components more efficiently and ultimately prototype faster. Digital prototyping is a UX or UI designer's opportunity to complete an idea and make a software user interface tangible. UI design and using any design system effectively requires a lot of practice. Digital prototyping should allow a UX designer to stretch their understanding and, hopefully, engage in some experimentation as well as be fun. There is a lot of nuance in the ways a digital prototype can be executed, and the better you get at designing the UX design flows, the more time you will get to polish the UI design and be able to use a design to its fullest. Your first material design digital prototype might feel a little cobbled together, which is fine. You might find the Google Material 3 Design system is highly useful and in other instances, constricting. Only with practice building digital prototypes using design libraries will you get a sense of what works and what does not. Either way, you have a solution that you can test with a user and that is worth your time and effort. Now that you have some experience with the Material 3 Design system let us explore a bit deeper.

[Explore design system components](#contents.xhtml_sc2_123a)

As a UX designer, a software product is not just a collection of screens designed in a tool like Figma; the reality is that a software product is not real until it is coded and used by your users. There are many ways to accomplish and develop software, which there are libraries of books written about, so we will not go too deep, but at its core, an interface design must be coded and deployed. Building digital prototypes is one way to ensure your user's experience is aligned and working the way it is intended. Prototypes can be effective not only for testing with your users but also as communication tools with your development partners. To achieve consistency, many high-quality design systems like Material Design have produced style and usage documentation. It is highly recommended that you check out the following documentation and read through many of the components: [https://m3.material.io/](https://m3.material.io/). Moving from overview \| specs \| guidelines \| accessibility. Google is a global software company and one way they achieve success across multiple products is directly tied to its ability to maintain and distribute its design system and its UI design ethos through design and code documentation. Some of you might go on to work on design systems and will likely be required to work on and set up UI design documentation. Therefore, you should familiarize yourself with what makes a UI design system successful, especially if you want to work on Android-based systems. If you want to work with Apple-based software, you should go to Apple Human Interface Guidelines:

[https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines](https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines)

As a UX designer, you will be expected to be an expert in what makes software successful. Consuming design and development documentation will help you achieve that success or, at minimum, make sure you are following established standards. We have to learn a lot about our design system and adhere to many UX and UI design patterns. The more practice you can get with these standards, the more effective your prototypes and, ultimately the more usable your software solutions will be. Let us review some documentation.

[Review design system documentation activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_124a)

Let us practice reviewing some design system documentation to improve our exposure to these systems as well as explore the nuance between the major players in the software space.

The total time is 1.5 hours.

Figma Google Android and Apple iOS documentation setup is 15 minutes:

1. Create a new page in your Figma file:

a. Label the page UI design documentation.

2. Add a Frame (F), and select desktop (1440 x 1024):

a. Select the new frame. Hold down the option key to copy and drag to the right.

b. Repeat making copies for the eight frames for reviewing eight different components.

3. Organize your frames into two columns (4 x 4). Select all the screens in a row and, using the align tool in the design right panel, align your frames to the left. Then, distribute them evenly. Repeat for the next column

4. Double-select each frame title and update each column title left for Google Material Design and right for Apple Human-Centered Guidelines:

a. Start with the first frame in the left column of Frames and match the same numbering:

i. 01-Buttons-M3

ii. 02-Cards-M3

iii. 03-Tabs-M3

iv. 04-TextFields-M3

b. Next, the right column and match the same numbering:

i. 01.1-Buttons-iOS

ii. 02.1-ContextMenus-iOS

iii. 03.1-Menus-iOS

iv. 04-Seach-Fields-iOS

Figma Android and Apple documentation review and analysis takes 1.25 hours.

Refer to the following steps:

1. Select your first frame (01-Buttons-M3) for Material Design Documentation frames.

2. Open: [https://m3.material.io/components/buttons/overview](https://m3.material.io/components/buttons/overview)

3. Review the documentation and read through the overview \| the specs \| guidelines and accessibility.

4. Review button code documentation in GitHub:

[https://github.com/material-components/material-web/blob/main/docs/components/button.md](https://github.com/material-components/material-web/blob/main/docs/components/button.md)

5. Take a screenshot of the 5 states of the button and any other important info you need to remember (hint: guidelines have do’s and don’ts) code has samples plus code snippets.

6. Repeat steps for the three other components in the Material Design System.

7. Select the second column (01.1-Buttons-ios) for Apple Design System documentation frames:

a. Open: [https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/buttons](https://developer.apple.com/design/human-interface-guidelines/buttons)

i. Review the documentation and read through overview \| best practices \| style \| content \| role \| platform considerations.

ii. Review Apple button code documentation:

[https://developer.apple.com/documentation/appkit/nsbezelstyle/nsbezelstylepush](https://developer.apple.com/documentation/appkit/nsbezelstyle/nsbezelstylepush)

iii. Download Apple Figma:

[https://www.figma.com/community/file/1248375255495415511](https://www.figma.com/community/file/1248375255495415511)

b. Screenshot the 5-button configuration, code usage, and other important details for how to use Apple elements.

8. Review and contrast the two documentation styles together. Both companies manage and maintain global software products:

a. Add a new frame for each column (Android & Apple). Using the type tool, add your thoughts as to what you think is successful and why you think the design system documentation works or does not.

9. Reflect on the design documentation you just reviewed:

a. Which design system UI documentation seemed the more useful?

b. What is difficult about using each UI design system documentation?

[TABLE]

At first, getting familiarized with design systems might feel daunting, but do not despair. The reason design system documentation exists in the first place is, that designers or developers do not have to memorize the vast number of quality standards in a software product. Can you imagine if they did not exist? Our software would be a total nightmare. Every UX designer and/or developer would interpret the standards differently, making software function very differently from place to place or even from screen to screen in each user interface. Luckily, through the hard work of countless, mostly nameless people, we do not have this dystopia, but rather, we have some level of UI order and semblance of control through design libraries that we can all learn from. They are not perfect since there is no such thing as perfect UI. However, it is a quite large private corporation that shares its work and distributes its design systems. Of course, they benefit, too, as the more designers and developers they get using their documentation, the more software products and services will get designed to their standards. Design systems can be a virtuous cycle. Hopefully.

[Becoming an expert in documentation](#contents.xhtml_sc3_125a)

Prototyping with a design system library is about making that is built for quality, speed, and usability. It is about making your ideas work inside the standards of the software systems or brand. UX design is about applying what we know about our users through the lens of design systems, which will ultimately deploy our solutions into the hands of our users. The faster a UX designer can consume documentation and execute a software design through best practices and design system standards, the faster a design system can demonstrate its value. The reality is that UX designers can solve problems in many ways, but if they are given a set of tools that align with development, it allows ideas to move from a piece of paper into a developed product with ease.. However, as a growing UX designer, you should get extremely comfortable with using available design system documentation so that when you must work with other design systems, you might know how good they are or how much work you need to apply over time. Design system documentation, as shown in the following figure, includes all the detailed rules of your UI design components and elements, from size specs to usage rules to code samples:

!

Figure 9.4: Learn for design system documentation

All this design system learning will feedback into a UX designer's process with much more efficiency. UX designers who can ideate, design, prototype, and test their solutions through the use of design systems are the ones who ultimately get software shipped and into the hands of their users. This is the goal. Get good at design systems and documentation and shipped products will follow.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_126a)

In this chapter, we have learned that UI design is about making ideas that adhere to standards through design systems. By prototyping with design systems, you can move solutions through the design and development process faster. Digital prototypes are not shipped software but something a UX designer can learn from. By using design systems and consuming design system documentation you can apply your learning and iterate your solutions to meet your user’s needs. A digital prototype creates a dialog between the UX designer and the design system, and through feedback from the user, all of them can be improved.

What you have learned so far is not enough to make you a UI design master, but it will get you started in practicing. Just like the other parts of this book, it is about helping you get along a path toward improving your user experiences through improved UI.

In the next chapter, we will explore taking our prototypes and applying the rigor of user testing to improve usability and accessibility.

| |

|----|

| Note: There are entire books, software tutorials, and learning curriculums dedicated to improving UI design skills, and if you really love this aspect of the software design process, I highly recommend you pick up more resources in the UI design space. As stated before, this book will not be comprehensive, but a catalyst in your continued learning, so know there is always more. |

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_127a)

- Digital prototypes are a starting place and not a final solution.

- UX design should practice consuming UI design documentation.

- UX designers should learn many design system standards as well as consume UI design documentation so that they too can improve their software from the inside out.

# Chapter 10: Applying Usability and Accessibility

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_128a)

In this chapter, we will be practicing evaluating digital products for usability and accessibility. Work from the previous chapter revealed gaps in your user experience when it was tested with some users. Prototypes are built for testing. If you are not testing your prototypes with users, you are just participating in performative design, therefore, continue to test your prototypes. Using an established design system library like Material Design 3 can be a useful way to get your UI design prototypes up to speed quickly, and even better prototyped with established best practices. An inconvenient part is that the strategy is your solutions will look like Material Design 3, that is, Google UI design. If your solution is meant to be designed for Android or other Google products, this will not be a problem, but Kava Coffee is likely not a Google service, so applying Google’s UI design could be problematic. You could modify the underlying Material Design 3 library to change its design to address the needs of Kava, or you could design your UI design library adhering to the quality factors established in the Material Design 3 assets. One of the good things about design libraries like Material Design 3 is that they have applied rigorous usability and accessibility factors as well. As a UX designer, a big piece of your job will be to care deeply about your users. Applying effective usability and accessibility are ways to show that you do care about all of your users. Ultimately, your user experiences need to be used by a lot of different people, and iterating your prototypes so they are as usable and accessible as possible will show a massive amount of value to a business and your users. Let us continue to practice adding usability and accessibility to our solutions.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_129a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- User testing ethos

- UX design usability and accessibility

- Usability and accessibility standards and tools

- Usability best practices
  - Nielsen’s heuristics

  - Nielson usability heuristics evaluation activity

- Explore user testing options
  - Usability test your Figma prototype with Maze.co Activity

- Iterate your solution for usability and accessibility

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_130a)

After completing this chapter, you will apply accessibility and usability best practices before user-testing your UI design prototype. Practice allows you to evolve your solution and evaluate the success or failure of your usability. You will be exposed to accessibility principles, gain experience with usability best practices, and practice evaluating a solution through a heuristic usability evaluation. The iterations and refinements made in this chapter will continue to be built upon to help you improve your software skills.

[TABLE]

[User testing ethos](#contents.xhtml_sc2_131a)

As we discussed throughout this book, UX design is human-centered. One way to guarantee you are applying human-centered design is to make sure your users are testing your solutions at every phase. User testing has a wide variety of values, from creating a feedback loop for designers to refining user interface usability and accessibility. Fundamentally, user testing is an ethos. An ethos is a set of guiding principles or beliefs held by a community. Product design teams need to create credible solutions that are meaningful for their users and help drive success for their businesses, and this can be attained through prototyping first and user testing. A product team must hold user testing to be important. User testing is hard. It requires effort, time, and participation, but also it needs the team to value the feedback.

| |

|----|

| Note: For example, I once worked on a small business-to-business (B2B) team working on a software tool for customer service agents. To get up to speed, I got access to the customer service call logs and recordings. After watching and listening to a few calls, it led me to wanting to talk with our customer service agents and set up a few user tests. The business was quite reluctant to allow us to use customer service agents in some user tests or even see their employees as users, but fortunately, the business agreed to a small user test. Because of this, I was quickly able to identify a few critical software tool issues, including some performance problems and a few key workarounds the customer service agents were using to cope with our software. User research and user testing were essential in showing the business why making improvements to the software through user testing mattered, but ultimately, we improved our customer service agents’ tool and performance, which improved their efficacy and efficiency. |

With user testing comes change, and a design team that can user test their solutions will build better software.

![A person using a tablet Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_10.1_(1).png>)

Figure 10.1: User testing ethos

The reason user testing should be an ethos for designers is that, in our current software design and development environments, it is relatively easy to skip and not test your prototypes with users, but just go and ship software to users. Generally, it is not advisable to start solving problems without user testing, but many businesses and start-ups ignore this advice. Can a product team ship their solution to their users without user testing? Sure, however, the risk of shipping an idea without user testing can result in deploying features that have usability problems. All of your users will then experience these flaws, and some of them might even leave your product because of them. On the other hand, a product team that produces a prototype and tests that prototype with users might catch a usability issue experienced by a fraction of a product's total user base. A product team can then iterate their solution to be more usable and help their users more meaningfully before shipping the solution to their broader audience. User testing prototypes are about creating an environment where products are continuously improved, and UX designers and developers can infuse usability and accessibility into every crevasse of a shipped product. When done with care and attention, software products can be used by the broadest audience possible. Designing and scaling solutions is the whole point. Let us dig deeper by applying accessibility and usability best practices.

[UX design usability and accessibility](#contents.xhtml_sc2_132a)

In the end, a software product can be used by a lot of different people, hopefully, your solutions are used by millions. Whether there is one user or 1 Billion users, the goal of any software product is that it can be used and accessible by anyone. Usability and accessibility are intertwined like a Möbius strip. A Möbius strip is a one-sided non non-orientable surface constructed by affixing the ends of a rectangular strip after first having given one of the ends a one-half twist. The shape is infinite, if you trace your finger along the surface of the Möbius strip, you would never overlap, your finger would go inside and outside of the loop’s path forever. Similarly, usability and accessibility do not exist without the other, as shown in the following figure:

![A line drawing of a tie Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_10.2_(1).png>)

Figure 10.2: Usability and accessibility is like a UX Möbius strip

Both usability and accessibility are quality metrics that look to make products and services successful at scale. The goal is to design for all users. Making systems usable is the minimum requirement for user experience, and usability by itself will not equal great user experience.

The Möbius strip is one of the most famous surfaces in mathematics and is constructed by cutting a long strip of paper, putting a half twist in it, and gluing the ends of the strip together. What makes this seemingly ordinary construct so fascinating is that while the original strip of paper had two sides, the Möbius strip seems to have only one. The shape is continuous, just like usability and accessibility. A product that is usable but not accessible is ignoring the totality of the users that can be affected. A product, service, or interface that is not accessible is not usable. For example, a product cannot be usable if only 87% of people can access it. According to the Pew Research and 2021 US Census Bureau, 13% of the US population identifies as disabled. That is equal to ~42.5 million people in the USA. Scaled to the globe’s 8 billion plus people, that means 1 billion humans on the planet are disabled.

Accessibility is the practice of making products and services usable by as many people as possible. Usable and accessible products start with designers and developers who care about their users and, thus, break down barriers to access throughout the design and development process. Moreover, usability is only truly understood through user testing, and if you test your product and services with a wide range of users with a wide range of accessibility challenges, your team can make sure your products are serving everyone. There are many accessibility and usability factors, and we will continue to practice more throughout this chapter, but know that usability and accessibility are in constant flux, just like our users and technologies. To consider the many factors that go into accessibility and usability, we can start with some of the larger accessibility categories shown in the following figure:

![A diagram of different types of disability Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_10.3_(1).png>)

Figure 10.3: Accessibility Categories – cognitive/neurodiversity, communication/speech, mobility/dexterity, vision/sight, mental health, hearing/auditory

Now, let us learn more about the categories:

- Vision/sight issues:
  - A range of access challenges for users who are blind through to users who are color blind and even to users using their technology on bright sunny days or at night. If a user cannot read a UI button because they see the type the UI button is not accessible or usable.

- Hearing/auditory issues:
  - A range of access challenges for users who are deaf through to users who are hard of hearing or have limited hearing range. If a user cannot hear a video or get feedback from a UI button, other methods can be used to improve an experience with limited sound.

- Mobility/dexterity issues:
  - A range of access challenges for users might be victims of medical challenges, like arthritis, through to spinal cord injuries, or MLS to users who do not have access to both hands like a parent holding a baby or missing limb. If a user cannot interact with a product, then it is not accessible or usable.

- Communication/speech issues:
  - A range of access challenges from learning disabilities, including focus, concentration, and understanding. If a user cannot understand a UI because it uses too much jargon, then it is not accessible or usable.

- Cognitive/neurodiversity issues:
  - A range of access challenges for users with dyslexia, autism, seizures or other cognitive challenges. If a user cannot understand a UI because it is hard to read, then it is not accessible or usable.

- Mental health issues:
  - A range of access challenges for users who are living with issues from PTSD to depression or bipolar disorder. If a user is triggered by a product’s UI, then it is not accessible or usable.

There are many resources about accessibility and it is highly recommended that you check these out, including Microsoft, along with other software and web-based services:

- [https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/accessibility](https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/accessibility)

- [https://www.apple.com/accessibility/](https://www.apple.com/accessibility/)

- [https://about.google/belonging/disability-inclusion/](https://about.google/belonging/disability-inclusion/)

- [https://www.w3.org/WAI/fundamentals/accessibility-intro/](https://www.w3.org/WAI/fundamentals/accessibility-intro/)

- [https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Learn/Accessibility/What_is_accessibility](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Learn/Accessibility/What_is_accessibility)

- [https://www.adobe.com/trust/accessibility.html](https://www.adobe.com/trust/accessibility.html)

and many more.

Usability and accessibility are incumbent on the UX designer to care and put in the hard work that makes their solutions successful for as many users as possible. Since the software runs our world, the importance of making your solutions useable and accessible is directly connected to our work.

[Usability and accessibility standards and tools](#contents.xhtml_sc2_133a)

In 1998, just as the internet was rapidly scaling around the globe, Section 508 was added to the Rehabilitation Act of 1973. The Rehabilitation Act prohibits employment discrimination against any individuals with disabilities, and Section 504 sets up that disabled people are a class and not a minority, which put into motion the conditions to pass the ADA – Americans with Disabilities Act in 1990. The ADA establishes and sets regulations and standards and is regulated within the Department of Justice. The ADA helps establish and oversee laws for the built environment, employment regulations, communication technology, and more. Similar government regulations exist around the globe because the same protections for disabled individuals extend across borders as well as into our digital and screen-based experiences. Section 508 – Electronic and Information Technology sets up regulations and standards for software and web-based technology systems with the goal of applying universal design and making a goal that all people have access to our digital tools.

The European Union through the European Accessibility Act (EAA), the United Kingdom, and Asia (China, Japan, Korea, etc.) also have governments that have enacted similar regulations to the USA. Many of these protections exist around the globe and they are there for the benefit of humankind but also as a way to make our global systems work and be usable and accessible regardless of where they are designed/developed and deployed. Like McDonald’s or Starbucks exists around the world and can deliver similar experiences, software and website systems can be produced for a global audience with relative ease through the internet and software compatibility. It is nice to know that many similarities and common government oversight for disabled people exist in varying forms around the globe and that usability and accessibility standards make our systems more scalable. Whether you are building native software or creating global websites, the goal of making systems accessible and usable for everyone is deeply connected to our human-centered design process, and you should want to make your solution accessible and usable by all your users. Due to this, there are many regulations and standards to follow, as well as tools for improving the effectiveness of your usability and accessibility. Many countries, trade blocks, and international law systems have drafted and adopted laws and regulations about accessibility which can be a difficult thing to navigate as a designer. However, the W3C.org leads most web accessibility systems and you should start there as a learning place for improving your usability and accessibility opportunities.

Let us review some regulations and standards first:

1. USA: W3C—WAI (Web Accessibility Initiative): [https://www.w3.org/WAI/](https://www.w3.org/WAI/)

a. WCAG 2.2: Web Content Accessibility Guidelines 2.2:

2. USA: ADA Web Accessibility Guidance

a. [https://www.ada.gov/resources/web-guidance/](https://www.ada.gov/resources/web-guidance/)

3. USA: Section 508.gov:

a. https://www.section508.gov/develop/software-websites/

4. EU: EAA: [https://ec.europa.eu/social/main.jsp?catId=1202](https://ec.europa.eu/social/main.jsp?catId=1202)

a. [https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/](https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/?uri=CELEX%3A32019L0882)TXT/?uri=CELEX%3A32019L0882

5. UK accessibility:

a. [https://www.gov.uk/guidance/accessibility-requirements-for-public-sector-websites-and-apps](https://www.gov.uk/guidance/accessibility-requirements-for-public-sector-websites-and-apps)

6. South Korea:

a. [https://dredf.org/legal-advocacy/international-disability-rights/international-laws/south-korea-welfare-law-for-persons-with-disabilities/](https://dredf.org/legal-advocacy/international-disability-rights/international-laws/south-korea-welfare-law-for-persons-with-disabilities/)

7. Japan:

a.

Fortunately, the WC3 has been adopted as a common guideline for most countries but like most things in software design, the standard is constantly being updated over time. If you get familiarized with the standards and regulations, you will ultimately design more accessible software:

![A screenshot of a computer AI-generated content may be incorrect.](<images/Figure_10.4_(1).png>)

Figure 10.4: Screenshot of color contrast checker evaluating background color \#eeeee (light grey) with foreground color \#000000 (black) showing passing WCAG AA and AAA contrast ratio scores

There are also many accessibility tools that you can apply to your systems to help guide your software toward compliance as well as keep your system more accessible and usable. Here are a few that should be on your accessibility list:

- Colour Contrast Checker: [https://colourcontrast.cc/](https://colourcontrast.cc/)

- Accessible Colors: [https://accessible-colors.com/](https://accessible-colors.com/)

- Adobe Color: [https://color.adobe.com/create/color-contrast-analyzer](https://color.adobe.com/create/color-contrast-analyzer)

- Web Accessibility Score: [https://www.webaccessibility.com/](https://www.webaccessibility.com/)

- WAVE accessibility automated evaluations: [https://wave.webaim.org/](https://wave.webaim.org/)

- Google Lighthouse: [https://developer.chrome.com/docs/lighthouse/overview/](https://developer.chrome.com/docs/lighthouse/overview/)

- Axe accessibility Chrome extension: [https://www.deque.com/](https://www.deque.com/)

- Google Chrome screen reader extension:

  [https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/screen-reader/kgejglhpjiefppelpmljglcjbhoiplfn](https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/screen-reader/kgejglhpjiefppelpmljglcjbhoiplfn)

Many other services, accessibility audit companies, accessibility tests, and usability evaluations should be explored or improved upon. There are also a large number of designers, usability experts, and accessibility consultants that exist due to a few parts. One, making your website and/or software accessible and usable will impact a business’s bottom line. A business wants to make its products as usable and accessible as possible, as this can lead to more customers, more revenue, and more brand value. The second part is reducing litigation risk. A business does not want to leave itself open to legal action. Section 508 is a set of laws, that if wilfully ignored, could leave a business subject to accessibility lawsuits. In 2022, 2,387 web accessibility lawsuits were filed in either federal court under the ADA or California State Unruh Act. The reality is accessibility matters, and if not addressed, it can leave a business liable. The most important part is that designers and developers want to help people. When design is done well, it includes making sure solutions are usable, accessible, and valuable to all users.

[Usability best practices](#contents.xhtml_sc2_134a)

In 1994, Jakob Nielsen of the NNG group developed a set of usability heuristics that are a standard for evaluating and improving software from a usability perspective. If you are not familiar with heuristics, they are a simple set of strategies that humans, animals, organizations, and even machines can use to quickly form judgments, make decisions, and find solutions. Fundamentally, improving software is rooted in evaluating what works and what does not. A software solution should be evaluated both through users via user testing, as well as through software experts who apply usability best practices and evaluate your solution through usability heuristics. Software is not a foregone conclusion; it is essentially a hypothesis, and if designers apply best practices and care about building usable systems, then those systems just might be usable. Of course, we can only guarantee this through user testing, but if UX design applies usability heuristics to their solutions, they will likely be better than those that have not applied this rigor.

[Nielsen’s heuristics](#contents.xhtml_sc3_135a)

Jakob Nielsen refined the list originally developed in 1990 by himself and Rolf Molich improving a human-computer dialogue resource. It is recommended to follow him and read his expertise in making systems accessible and useable:

![A black and white image of symbols AI-generated content may be incorrect.](<images/Figure_10.5_(1).png>)

Figure 10.5: 10 Usability heuristics

Let us look at them in detail:

- Visibility of system status: The system should always keep users informed about what is going on, through appropriate feedback within a reasonable time.

- Match between the system and the real world: The system should speak the users' language, with words, phrases, and concepts familiar to the user rather than system-oriented terms. Follow real-world conventions, making information appear in a natural and logical order.

- User control and freedom: Users often choose system functions by mistake and will need a clearly marked emergency exit to leave the unwanted state without having to go through an extended dialogue. Support undo and redo.

- Consistency and standards: Users should not have to wonder whether different words, situations, or actions mean the same thing. Follow platform conventions.

- Error prevention: Even better than good error messages is a careful design that prevents a problem from occurring in the first place. Either eliminate error-prone conditions or check for them and present users with a confirmation option before they commit to the action.

- Recognition rather than recall: Minimize the user's memory load by making objects, actions, and options visible. The user should not have to remember information from one part of the dialogue to another. Instructions for use of the system should be visible or easily retrievable whenever appropriate.

- Flexibility and efficiency of use: Accelerators—unseen by the novice user—may often speed up the interaction for the expert user such that the system can cater to both inexperienced and experienced users. Allow users to tailor frequent actions.

- Aesthetic and minimalist design: Dialogues should not contain information that is irrelevant or rarely needed. Every extra unit of information in a dialogue competes with the relevant units of information and diminishes their relative visibility.

- Help users recognize, diagnose, and recover from errors: Error messages should be expressed in plain language (no codes), precisely indicate the problem, and constructively suggest a solution.

- Help and documentation: Even though it is better if the system can be used without documentation, it may be necessary to provide help and documentation. Any such information should be easy to search, focused on the user's task, list concrete steps to be carried out, and not be too large.

These 10 factors are a great starting place for evaluating the success of a website, product, or service.

[Nielson usability heuristics evaluation activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_136a)

Let us practice evaluating a website and evaluating its usability through Jakob Nielson’s 10 usability heuristics:

Total time: One to two hours

- Setup: Heuristics tool + Google Chrome Extension: (five min)

To get started, we will be using a Chrome extension software to speed up our heuristic evaluations:

1. Go to: [https://heurio.app/](https://heurio.app/)

a. Create an account, as shown in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](<images/Figure_10.6_(1).png>)

Figure 10.6: The Heurio project page

Once your account has been created start by clicking the + Create project button.

2. Create a project for: [https://www.rockauto.com/](https://www.rockauto.com/)

The project can be seen in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.7.png)

Figure 10.7: View of Heurio project instance for rockauto.com

Install the Heurio Chrome extension from the following link:

[https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/heurio-ux-check-visual-fe/pjdbofhiijhapnmpgilkeammkanglfdj](https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/heurio-ux-check-visual-fe/pjdbofhiijhapnmpgilkeammkanglfdj)

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.8.png)

Figure 10.8: Screenshot of the Chrome web store for the Heurio extension

3. Open your RockAuto Huerio project, as shown in the following figure:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.9.png)

Figure 10.9: Screenshot of RockAuto homepage with Heurio UI design activated via the Chrome extension

Now let us add Usability Heuristic Evaluation Annotations for all 10 Nielsen, which will take one-to-two hours:

1. Using the Comment View (Alt + v) in Huerio. Add annotation where you see usability issues.

a. Update the corresponding Nielson usability factor, add notes, add comments, and submit the annotation:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.10.png)

Figure 10.10: Screenshot of adding a Heurio annotation note

2. Select the annotation and update the severity from neutral (blue) to high (orange):

a. Save high/critical issues for bugs, broken or unusable UI design, or other egregious errors in usability.

b. In the following sample, the annotation has been set to High (orange):

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.11.png)

Figure 10.11: A Screenshot of Heurio Changing severity of an annotation to High

3. Go through all the UI on the RockAuto page and make annotations of varying severity for all 10 usability factors.

a. Run through all 10 usability heuristics in order and then circle back if there are others that repeat:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.12.png)

Figure 10.12: Screenshot of Huerio 10 usability heuristics categories

4. Interact with the homepage to expose more usability issues:

a. Add annotation for as many things that feel hard to use, or difficult to see, or confusing. There are many issues, so start with the big ones that are the most severe and work toward those that are less severe.

b. Huerio has a quick reference guide to Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics that slide out on a right rail to help during your heuristic evaluations.

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.13.png)

Figure 10.13: Screenshot of Nielsen’s 10 Usability Heuristics guidelines right rail

5. Add 10 or more annotations on the homepage of RockAuto.com:

a. There are a lot more than 10, but cover at minimum the 10 Usability Heuristics.

b. See the following sample that has a number of heuristic evaluation annotations classified by the number and the color of severity:

!

Figure 10.14: Screenshot of Heurio heuristic evaluation annotations

6. Go to your Heurio Project:

a. Review your usability annotations, evaluating how many critical, high, medium, low, and neutral issues you have.

b. Reassign severity if necessary.

c. All your heuristic evaluation annotations are placed as cards on your Huerio project page.

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.15.png)

Figure 10.15: Screenshot of Heurio project page with evaluation cards placed in severity columns

7. Bonus Evaluate the tablet and mobile phone sizes:

a. Using screen resizing features, update to tablet and mobile and add more annotations:

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.16.png)

Figure 10.16: Screenshot of Heurio screen resizing features for evaluating responsive web experiences

8. Share your Heurio heuristic evaluation with 3 to 5 other people.

a. Request reviews, leave comments, update severity, and point out suggestions for what could be fixed.

9. Define comment priorities by identifying heuristic issues that should be fixed and in which order.

a. Not all issues marked critical are the highest priority.

10. Re-prioritize issues based on feedback from people you have shared this with.

| |

|----|

| Note: Usability heuristic audits are an essential tool for any UX designer looking to improve their usability and accessibility skills. The expert review tools can also be part of your UX design services and part of the vocabulary you as a professional need to be able to articulate to your clients and fellow designers. Only by practicing good usability and accessibility and learning to have a keen usability eye through usability heuristic evaluations will you build products and services that hopefully. |

Now that you have some practice looking at a website through the usability lens and hopefully catching many usability issues or parts of the user experience that make you go. You have gained some experience through your usability assessment. Hopefully, you are seeing ways to improve a piece of software and why a UX designer needs to be an expert in all aspects of usability and accessibility. In the real world, many people can be responsible for creating software, but a UX designer should take the lead in making their software solutions as usable and accessible as possible. Making software usable by all your users is hard and many businesses are content with it—it works well enough. A good UX designer should not settle for this mediocrity but rather put their foot down and do the hard work to improve software for everyone from the inside out. Get good at evaluating usable systems and ultimately you, too will build them yourself.

[Explore user testing options](#contents.xhtml_sc2_137a)

Usability and accessibility are lofty goals of all software, and we can do our best efforts by applying usability standards and usability best practices from the get-go into our user experiences. Applying these practices, however, can only get us so far. A UX designer can only ever know they have built a usable and accessible system by testing it with users. Therefore, never assume because you have applied best practices that your solution is usable; that is a dangerous trap. Every software solution has unique content, context, and users and, therefore, is subject to be used differently. Due to this variation, a UX designer should apply usability and accessibility to their solutions and then test that solution and validate if it works or does not. As we have been discussing, prototypes are an effective way to quickly test how effective a software solution is or is not. Usability testing can applied in user testing as a way to polish and refine a piece of software. There are many tools for executing user testing in software development. Some tools that you should be familiar with are shown in the following figure:

![A diagram of a software development Description automatically generated](images/Figure_10.17.png)

Figure 10.17: User testing feedback software tools

Let us look some user testing tools:

- Maze: [https://maze.co/](https://maze.co/) (we will be using this later)

- UserTesting.com: [https://www.usertesting.com/](https://www.usertesting.com/)

- Hotjar: [https://www.hotjar.com/](https://www.hotjar.com/)

- Loops: [https://www.useloops.com/](https://www.useloops.com/)

- Userlytics: [https://www.userlytics.com/](https://www.userlytics.com/)

- Lookback: [https://www.lookback.com/](https://www.lookback.com/)

- Optimal Workshop: [https://www.optimalworkshop.com/](https://www.optimalworkshop.com/)

- Loop11: [https://www.loop11.com/](https://www.loop11.com/)

Although there are many tools for user testing, it will never hurt you as a UX designer to try out these tools. User testing can be time-consuming and tools like these are built to help streamline the process and shorten the level of effort as well as the barrier to testing in the first place. As we have been discussing, testing is not optional so choose a tool that fits with your team and helps you improve your usability and accessibility over time. We will be experimenting with Maze to gain some usability feedback on our Figma UI design prototypes.

[Usability test your Figma prototype with Maze.co Activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_138a)

Let us practice usability testing our UI design Figma prototype:

Total time: 2 hours

Maze Setup 15 mins

Open [https://www.maze.co](https://www.maze.co) and create a free account:

1. Create a new Maze Test:

a. From the templates choose Usability Testing \| Usability testing a new product.

b. Update the title to be: Kava Coffee Usability Test.

2. Click the Prototype Test Flag Question:

a. Connect Maze and Figma.

b. In your Figma prototype, copy your prototype link. (anyone can view the link.)

c. Add to link to maze \| Import prototype.

d. Allow prototype to load into Maze.co on the right.

3. Update the prototype task title and description for your user tester:

a. Go through your other questions and update as needed to what aspects of usability you were hoping to achieve.

4. Select start testing (blue button upper right:

a. Select Go Live. Now your UI design prototype is ready to be user tested for usability.

5. Copy the Maze link and share it with 5 to 10 people:

a. Share it with your friends, and family, share the link to social media, share the link via email, text message your friends the link, etc. start gathering some usability feedback data.

6. Give the user a test a few days and come back to review the results.

a. If you need more testers, share your link again. The goal should be more than 5 users to complete your user test.

7. Download your report and repeat using [www.Maze.co](http://www.maze.co/) for other user testing needs.

| |

|----|

| Note: Rely on the user testing software documentation and any code that proceeds it as many of these user testing tools function similarly but differently. When we learn user testing tools well it allows a UX designer to implement similar systems into our teams over time. |

[Iterate your solution for usability and accessibility](#contents.xhtml_sc2_139a)

Now that you have gotten some user testing data back from your UI design prototype, what did you learn? Likely, there will be some level of issue or problem that your users experience. This is good as it is what usability user testing is for, and the data you collect can be a meaningful guide to identify when and where your user experiences issues. Gathering user testing data into a trackable list or iterative changes table can be useful when working with a team as well as allowing your team to quickly prioritize and rank the iterations that resulted from your user tests. Hopefully, the user testing data is helpful and directional in feedback and possible action. With the user testing data that you gathered quickly, you can start iterating your UI design prototype. The user test and subsequent feedback data have streamlined the improvements you can make in your usability and accessibility. Applying iteration through user testing can make your software a champion for usability and accessibility. In the early 2000s, the steps of prototyping and user testing took 5x the time it does today because of software tools like Figma and Maze, a UX designer should be able to prototype and user test at increasing speed. UX designers should apply this complete process to guarantee they are solving their user’s problems. Do not skip steps but rather double down on improving your software solutions through usability, accessibility, and user testing. Bring tools like Figma and Maze to your software teams and include heuristic usability evaluations as required skills, UX designers must complete on their products.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_140a)

Product usability and accessibility are achieved over time and only through UI design prototyping and user testing. Prototypes are for testing, and testing allows us to improve usability. By using established standards and best practices, you as UX designers, can demonstrate expertise but through prototyping and user testing, you can build usable, useful, and accessible products.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_141a)

- Digital prototypes are a starting place for improving usability and accessibility.

- UX designers should practice usability heuristics and constantly improve their own expertise.

- UX designers should learn many user testing tools and work toward streamlining the data collection process so that they can prove their solutions are usable, useful, accessible, and hopefully delightful user experiences.
