# Chapter 11: UX, UI to Front-end or Back-end

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_142a)

Building usable and accessible software through prototyping and user testing is the beating heart of creating systems that respond to your users’ needs. From the last chapter, you are gaining a grasp on the challenge of making software solutions usable and accessible at their core. At this point, recognizing your part in making usable and accessible products is also deeply connected to your consuming what makes good software as well as using the software yourself. This chapter aligns the reader with how a software design team is focused on making and how we get ideas into the world. You will be exposed to software project management while you get experience generating business and UX key performance indicators (KPIs). Finally, you will align a feature with the larger business and communicate its value.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_143a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Recap of agile development
  - Starts with the agile manifesto

  - Agile development sprints

- Agile software team best practices
  - Usable, useful, accessible, and delightful

  - Software recall activity

- Product management
  - Product management lifestyle

  - Writing business and UX design KPI activity

  - Design and development decomposition

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_144a)

After completing this chapter, you will gain practice in applying software project management and building software planning activities. Practice allows you to evolve your burgeoning software skills. You will be exposed to agile software development, gain experience with evaluating software, and practice writing software KPIs. This chapter will continue to build upon your software skills and improve your understanding of software development.

[Recap of agile development](#contents.xhtml_sc2_145a)

Now that you have spent time learning about software and the things that go into UX design and designing usable, useful, accessible, and successful software, let us talk about how software teams function in many technology-driven organizations. You may or may not have heard of agile or agile development. Agile development is a series of product management procedures and approaches that ultimately allow a software team to respond to change. The core of agile frameworks has been in development since the 1980s; however, agile methodologies have helped accelerate Silicon Valley and countless software-driven companies.

| |

|----|

| Note: There are many books, articles, courses, and software certifications associated with agile and its connecting methodologies. This recap is not a comprehensive overview but should point you in the direction of some familiarity so that, as a UX designer, you understand how you might fit into a software team. In many cases, you will learn a lot about the agile process through working with an agile team. |

[Starts with the agile manifesto](#contents.xhtml_sc3_146a)

The agile manifesto ([https://agilemanifesto.org/](https://agilemanifesto.org/)) was published in 2001 and has helped guide software teams who are committed to designing and developing software ever since. Agile development is the outcome and combination of many software development processes that proceeded it, such as Extreme Programming, Scrum, Dynamic Systems Development Method (DSDM), Adaptive Software Development, Crystal, Feature-Driven Development, Pragmatic Programming, and other documentation-driven, heavyweight software development processes. Agile is aligned with a teamwork ethos that attempts to reduce long arduous development cycles and replace them with nimble, effective teams that can consume requirements quickly, iterate their software through prototyping, and focus on customers through user testing. The agile software designed a time-boxed team planning and production process built on sprints:

!.png)

Figure 11.1: Agile plus design

Agile and UX design are sympatico, especially when the design and development team members are willing to work together and feed each other information. When you work with an agile software team, you too will be applying UX design inside some version of agile. Grasping the values of agile and aligning them with the values of UX design and human-centered design is a key factor in allowing designers and developers to see eye to eye. Let us start by grasping what underpins agile.

(replicated from [https://agilemanifesto.org/](https://agilemanifesto.org/))

Agile development has the following four values:

- Value 1: Individuals and interactions over processes and tools:
  - Designers and developers both need to focus on their crafts but can solve problems faster when collaboration is at the heart of making successful software.

- Value 2: Working software over comprehensive documentation:
  - Designers and developers can arrive at working software faster via prototyping and iterating solutions together rather than focusing on documentation of subpar solutions.

- Value 3: Customer collaboration over contract negotiation:
  - Human-centered design focuses on customers

- Value 4: Responding to change over following a plan:
  - Design can iterate and pivot fast when they know development can, too.

As a UX designer, hopefully, you can see how these values can align with the design process. Design and development should not be at odds but rather be more like dance partners who tradeoff who leads. Hopefully, everyone is trying to build great software, and as a UX designer, you can contribute the design and human-centered ethos to your team’s agile cycles. The values shared by design and development are not that far apart and can collaborate. Design and development can achieve successful agile cycles by applying principles from the agile development manifesto.

The agile development manifesto has the following 12 principles:

- Our highest priority is to satisfy the customer through early and continuous delivery of valuable software.

- Welcome changing requirements, even late in development. Agile processes harness change for the customer's competitive advantage.

- Deliver working software frequently, from a couple of weeks to a couple of months, with a preference to the shorter timescale.

- Businesspeople and developers must work together daily throughout the project.

- Build projects around motivated individuals. Give them the environment and support they need and trust them to get the job done.

- The most efficient and effective method of conveying information to and within a development team is face-to-face conversation.

- Working software is the primary measure of progress.

- Agile processes promote sustainable development. The sponsors, developers, and users should be able to maintain a constant pace indefinitely.

- Continuous attention to technical excellence and good design enhances agility.

- Simplicity—the art of maximizing the amount of work not done—is essential.

- The best architectures, requirements, and designs emerge from self-organizing teams.

- At regular intervals, the team reflects on how to become more effective, then tunes and adjusts its behavior accordingly.

These principles are not only relevant to development roles but also for designers as we all hopefully participate equally in a software team’s success. In my experience, when all members of a software team are committed to these agile principles, the distinction between design and development roles starts to become less detectable. The measure of team success is then dictated by high-quality, shippable products because each team member wants to produce great software, too. UX design needs development, and vice versa. With agile teams, one can continuously improve not just as a team but also for their product. High-functioning agile teams are not born; they are built over time, and as a UX designer, you should look to show how your design skills and processes can join an agile team.

[Agile development sprints](#contents.xhtml_sc3_147a)

Many agile teams have committed to product management and project planning under time cycles known as sprints. A sprint is a time cycle that is typically between 1-8 weeks, where 2-week sprints is the most common sprint cycle time duration. During a sprint, a software team plans, collaborates, executes, and evaluates software work (Product Requirements/Design/Development Code/QA Testing/etc.):

![A diagram of a software development process Description automatically generated](images/Figure_11.2.png)

Figure 11.2: Agile sprint detail

Software teams can be in a variety of sizes but are made up of team members who occupy project management, design: User research (UR), user experience (UX), user interface (UI), interaction design (IxD), visual design, brand, copywriting (UX writing), etc., front-end (FE) development, back-end (BE) development, artificial intelligence (AI), information and systems architecture (IA), hardware design, etc.), quality assurance (QA) roles, and more. The goal of any agile team is to ship changes and effectively manage the amount of work each team member is responsible for in any given sprint. scrum and or daily stand-ups should monitor and mitigate interdependencies and team bottlenecks. Agile teams typically rely on scrum and participate in daily stand-ups to convene, collaborate, and communicate work progress. Agile teams should focus on changing user and business requirements, design or code blockers, quality outcomes, and more. Agile relies on scrum as it adds a micro and macro planning function to teams. The micro view scrum oversees the management of sprint planning. A sprint is initiated by a team/product owner who pulls items from a product backlog of issues or feature requests. The agile team then evaluates these chosen backlog items, and team members add complexity via a level of effort estimate to work, i.e., pointing and then distributing the work to team individuals. Once a sprint is planned, a team member can get started on their assigned work. Daily an agile team should convene quickly and discuss their progress. Typically, daily stand-ups that should happen early in a team’s workday follow a reporting structure where each team member quickly tells team members their progress:

- What did you do you do yesterday?

- Do you have any blockers?

- What do you plan to do today?

Daily stand-ups are designed to keep teams communicating, collaborating, and maximizing work time versus meetings. Agile micro keeps software team members unblocked, makes sure everyone has work to make progress on, and maintains work product that needs to be shared or exchanged.

At the macro level, agile scrum allows teams to run sprint retrospectives and sprint-to-sprint reviews. Sprint retrospectives occur at the end of sprints and are a way teams can evaluate how effective they have been at achieving each sprint plan and goal. Sprint retrospectives also allow team members to make improvements to team collaboration, air grievances, and express praise for individuals on the team. Agile Sprints are rarely perfect, and only with time can teams identify how to plan effectively, identify requirement changes early, and tune an agile team’s own agile process. Sprint-to-sprint reviews allow product teams to recognize patterns, make planning adjustments, and improve efficiency and collaboration from sprint-to-sprint.

[Agile software team best practices](#contents.xhtml_sc2_148a)

In the end, a software product can be built by a lot of different people, and this is what makes building high-functioning software teams extremely challenging. As a UX designer, you will be part of those teams, so you need to do your part and help build not just better software but a better team as well. Here are some best practices for being a better agile team member:

- Design and development product backlog:
  - A product backlog added to and vetted by design and development helps define, organize, and refine combined work that needs to be done to add value to a product. It also helps guarantee that work is queued up for both design and development and that both sides are prepared to work together. A product backlog that is not somewhat balanced will result in product teams that do not collaborate or worse, leave each other out of the decision-making.

- Communication guidelines:
  - Set communication guidelines to help with communication challenges that can arise when teams work together either in person or remotely. Key to defining where notes and feedback should live as well as appropriate and inappropriate critique or feedback loops.

- Sprint burndown:
  - A metric that helps track the progress of a sprint in real time. It also shows how agile a team is. Designs and developers respond to progress and feedback visuals.

- Continuous integration:
  - Designer and developers both need their focused work time but should collaborate diligently during ideation, complexity exploration, design handoff, and code review. Developers need time to focus on coding, so it is important to integrate shared changes with the team's code.

- Sprint backlog:
  - There should be zero or limited changes in the sprint backlog to keep the team focused and ensure timely delivery.

- Remote teams:
  - Adapt communication methods to remote teams. Real-time communication between distributed team members is critical to getting work done and building relationships.

- Extreme programming:
  - In this procedure, both developers and customers should participate highly. Customers can inspire additional development of a product by showcasing the most useful features

Usability and accessibility are incumbent on the UX designer to care and put in the hard work that makes their solutions successful for as many users as possible. Since the software runs our world, the importance of making your solutions useable and accessible is directly connected to our work.

[Usable, useful, accessible, and delightful](#contents.xhtml_sc3_149a)

Agile software development is not dogma. It has many different manifestations from company to company. Mostly, it is designed to be flexible and adapted by each team that practices the agile process. In some organizations, they call their process agile when it is not. In reality, their software process is more waterfall development or, worse, different between each product manager. Software development is not easy. It is extremely challenging to create software that is usable, useful, accessible, and delightful. At the moment, agile is the best process for a team to achieve these goals, and UX designers have a huge opportunity to push agile teams to achieve these goals. We have to adapt our UX design process inside the agile cycles to help get a software team there. Practicing agile principles and demonstrating you too can walk the walk and talk the talk when it comes to agile will put you in a position to actually work with a software team, and hopefully, you can apply human-centered design and prototyping to push delightful experiences into the hands of your users. The UX maturity model is a strategy that allows UX designers to focus on making software that is usable, useful, desirable, and delightful, as shown in the following figure:

![A diagram of a pyramid Description automatically generated](images/Figure_11.3.png)

Figure 11.3: UX maturity model and favorable products

The UX maturity model aligns with the idea that software experiences are additive. If a piece of software is not usable, is it not useful, desirable, or delightful to your users? Furthermore, a UX designer should focus on the bottom up and build software solutions they know are usable, useful, desirable, and hopefully delightful. Not every software experience will reach the level of delight, and it is incumbent on the UX designer and software team to identify what parts of the software experience should reach the delightful distinction.

For example, the Apple Mastercard Credit Card from Goldman Sachs, the packaging, and the phone setup. The seamless Bluetooth touch setup allows cardholders to sync their phone and physical cards, and activate their cards all in one, creating a moment of delight. The packaging and the quick phone setup deliver on Apple’s easy-to-use software and leave a user feeling confident in using the service:

![A close-up of a credit card AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Figure_11.4.jpg)

Figure 11.4: Apple Credit Card packaging a delightful experience

| |

|----|

| Note: Achieving delight in your software experiences is difficult. When you experience delight from a piece of software you should make a note of it, and record why it achieved that elusive feat. The more delight you experience in your own software experiences than impact your own experiences. |

[Software recall activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_150a)

Let us practice recalling and capturing software that we think works well.

Total time: one to two hours

Setup: Heuristics tool and Google Chrome Extension. The time taken is five minutes.

Capturing examples is essential to improving your software solutions, through the following steps:

1. Open a new FigJam File.

2. Label the file: Software evaluation.

3. On FigJam, create four large equal sections (shift + s).

4. Color each section light grey and label left to right:

a. Useful

b. Usable

c. Accessible

d. Delightful

5. In the Useful section, add screenshots of three to five mobile apps, software apps, or websites that you use and think are highly useful:

a. Screenshot the portion of each application you think is useful, that is, the menu, which is useful for navigation.

b. With each screenshot, add a title of the image, a short description of why this app is useful, and how you use it in your daily life.

c. After adding three to five software applications, review if there are any patterns for what types of software you have determined as useful.

6. Repeat for usable, accessible, and delightful:

a. Focus on capturing the section moments in each software tool.

b. Software tools can repeat between sections, but if they do, make sure to capture new screenshots for each category.

7. Review all four categories to see if there are any patterns or commonalities between each section:

a. Add a summary of what connects each software you choose and reflect on the team that produced those pieces of software.

| |

|----|

| Note: As we have already discussed, if you want to build great software it is informed by consuming and evaluating great software. Understand why it works, and what makes it successful, and then emulate those factors in your own solutions. |

Now that you have some practice looking at software through the UX maturity lens and looking for patterns. You have gained some experience through your collection and analysis. Hopefully, you are seeing patterns in your software choices that lead to being usable, useful, accessible, and delightful, along with why a UX designer needs to recognize and be able to repeat these qualities in the software solutions they design themselves. In the real world, many people are responsible for creating software but a UX designer is likely responsible for achieving delightful experiences. Get good at evaluating software systems through the UX maturity model, and ultimately, you too will build them yourself.

[Product management](#contents.xhtml_sc2_151a)

We have been discussing agile development because a large number of software teams apply some version of these ideas and principles for producing effective software in our world. At the core of agile is about managing a product that is designed by people, and therefore, product management is people management. As a UX designer, you are part of a software team and need to apply human-centered skills alongside your developers and business stakeholders. Over time, you will likely work with larger teams and be responsible for larger products and numbers of people. When managing products a goal is to create a clear rationale for what needs to be accomplished, why it matters to the business, and who is going to be responsible for achieving it.

| |

|----|

| Note: There are many books, articles, courses, and management careers. This discussion will not be comprehensive. A UX designer should understand how they can be managed as well as how they manage themselves. In many cases, you will learn a lot about product management by participating in it. |

Product management is the supervision of planning, design, development, deployment, and maintenance of products and services. In many cases, product management involves the oversight of the entire lifecycle of a product from ideation through to delivering the product or service to market. Product management can be done in many ways, but for software teams, it typically requires implementing agile development and then evaluating and measuring a team’s execution. Over time software teams and product management processes gain more and more maturity as the team members who create the software also improve.

[Product management lifecycle](#contents.xhtml_sc3_152a)

Product management requires a cycle, and the following product life cycle looks very similar to the UX design process, which is a good thing as your role will likely be in service of improving a product:

!

Figure 11.5: Product management lifecycle

Let us look at the product life cycle in detail:

1. Product vision:

a. Create a product vision, a 2-5 year view that addresses a long-term opportunity for users and the business. Help guide a product towards a north star for the medium and long-term time horizon.

2. Product/problem framing:

a. Identifies problems (business and user) and defines success.

3. User and competitive research:

a. Learn from your competition, learn from your customers, evaluate their needs, and reveal their values.

4. Design and development:

a. Synthesize vision, generate ideas, and funnel research into designs and prototypes.

5. Pilot solutions:

a. Test solutions with users in real-time with real customers through beta releases or working prototypes to help refine and improve solutions before launching to all users.

6. Launch/deploy solutions:

a. Release your solutions to all your users in a seamless and well-communicated method to allow solutions to be learned and used effectively by all your users.

7. Measure:

a. User metrics and data to measure the success of product releases and intake feedback for future planning.

Over time, you might use your UX design skills to move into product management. In my experience, the best product managers are those who can dive in and get their hands dirty with the team because they know how to make and not just manage. Understanding product management can be a useful way to understand how to improve a piece of software. At the core of a lot of management is data. How can the business measure the success of a team, product, or person? Quantifiable measurements are typically called key performance indicators (KPIs), and these KPIs are associated with how well a business, team, or individual is meeting goals. KPIs can be wildly different depending on the business or software team in many cases, they align with the 4 Ps of marketing:

!

Figure 11.6: Product, Price, Place, Promotions

The four Ps of marketing are as follows:

- Product:
  - KPI: Number of items sold.

- Price:
  - KPI: Sales revenue, cost of a campaign, quality of the lead, etc.

- Place:
  - KPI: Revenue by store, volume by state, etc.

- Promotions:
  - KPI: Website traffic numbers, return on investment (ROI)

In many cases, UX designers should be versed in identifying KPIs and helping business stakeholders clarify KPIs or help write KPIs for products or services. In some cases, businesses’ UX and design are not measured effectively which leads to a misperception of the value UX design can bring to a team.

For example, let us take a team where the development team had KPIs for how many pull requests (PRs) they should commit per sprint but the UX designers had no measurable KPIs for their contributions. It led to design being under-invested in by the business because they were not measuring the amount of work a UX designer contributed per team.

Peter Drucker, the well-known management consultant, educator, and author, would say, If you can’t measure it, you can’t manage it.

The short of it is that UX designers should help define KPIs for the UX designers as that would have likely helped our product and improved the investment the business was willing to make in design. The more you as a UX designer can understand what a business measures, the better you can impact those measurements or guarantee your work is measured as a business value and thus managed by the business. Before we start, let us define what makes a good KPI.

A good KPI is defined by the following:

- A significant measurement of performance and progress.

- Directly tied to business goals. (Object and key results (OKRs) or other.

- Clear and useful for cross-departmental collaboration.

- Reasonably achievable and contains a set of meaningful benchmarks.

Sample KPI: Add 950 new customers by the end of FY2025 (Financial Year)

Sample bad KPI: Add new customers in new markets (lacking details and data)

Quality factors: This KPI has the following:

1. A target: 950 new customers.

2. A timeframe: 1 year/12 months.

3. A data source: Email registrations, customer relationship management (CRM), or new credit cards.

4. Frequency: trackable month over month.

Good KPIs should not be vague, abstract, or complex in measurement. The goal of any KPIs is that both the business and the software team know what is a priority. If KPIs are difficult to understand, disconnected from business priorities, or complex, they will likely go unfulfilled or worse, unmeasured and that can hurt the business. To practice this, let us go write some KPIs of our own for practice.

[Writing business and UX design KPI activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_153a)

Let us practice writing some KPIs that align with the UX tasks and that can be valued by businesses.

Total time: one hour.

The following activity is a writing exercise, so choose any software that allows you to write quickly and share:

1. Open a Figma file or Google Doc.

2. Create a Desktop frame (1440 x 1024).

3. Add a frame on the left ½ the screen:

a. Label the frame Business KPIs.

b. Add a text box (T).

c. Write 3x Business KPIs for Kava Coffee.

d. Write a Kava Coffee KPI with a target, a timeframe, a data source, and a frequency.

4. Add a frame for right ½ the screen:

a. Label Business UX design KPIs.

b. Add a text box (T).

c. Align with your Business KPIs.

d. Write a measurable task a UX designer can do to accomplish the business KPI.

e. Rewrite the Business KPI with the UX task.

5. Review and share your KPIs.

| |

|----|

| Note: These KPIs will be hypothetical but be sincere and put on your business hat to try and understand what might be meaningfully measured so that you can translate those into UX design measurables. |

[Design and development decomposition](#contents.xhtml_sc3_154a)

Agile development and product management go hand in hand, and the better each team member adheres to the micro and macro views of their software, the better they can continue to add value. Whether that be through improving business, development, and design KPIs or by helping agile teams achieve high-quality velocity. It is only truly achievable when entire teams can collaborate, know how they can add value, and can internalize the KPIs that are meaningful to their product and to the business. Agile teams can share work between product, design, and development more seamlessly and can effectively apply decomposition to product management. Decomposition is the design and development process where large, complex ideas, features, or software problems are broken down into smaller, more manageable, and distributable parts. Since an agile software team operates on sprints, a large problem or complex feature may not be able to be achieved in a single sprint and likely will take multiple sprints. Decomposition allows a development team to break apart a design solution and sequence the development process so that understanding, programming, and code dependencies can be achieved. The UX/UI designer and developers get together to discuss the software changes and define complexity:

![A cartoon of a person and a couple of people AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Figure_11.7.png)

Figure 11.7: Design/Development decomposition is about collaboration and defining complexity

For example, a UX designer may have a new solution for a search feature that was identified through their user research. The search functionality is mocked up into a prototype but is not real; however, it is a good proxy for communicating the complexity to a development team. For the search to be included, a back-end developer needs to decomp the search request. This might require a change in the database or how the search application programming interface (API) is queried. Next, the front-end (FE) developers need to review the UI design and see what will need to be created or pulled in from an existing design system. FE development can only complete their work with back-end (BE) Development has completed theirs since FE consumes BE to create a user interface. The decomposition process allows a product manager and the design and development team to plan their agile sprints more effectively by breaking apart changes in the product, that is, code, to be manageable and sequence-able work items for each team member. Agile development is an effective way to solve software problems but is only as good as the product, design, and development decomposition process. As a UX designer, my goal is to think big but also be able to break the software vision apart into achievable pieces that both improve a software solution and improve business and product KPIs.

Easy right? In reality, it is not. Getting a software team to build great solutions is very hard, but it is a noble goal, which is why you are this far in this book in the first place.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_155a)

Throughout this chapter, we have been exploring how building usable and accessible software through prototyping and user testing is at the core of agile development. Any software design team that focuses on making great progress from sprint to sprint is also the teams that get their ideas into the world. UX design is an essential factor in any software team, and the more you infuse your human-centered skills into product management and business measurement, the better software we all get to use.

In the next chapter, we will discover ways that design and development work as a team. We will discuss ways design and development can improve handoff with the use of tools and how design and development can use processes like continuous integration.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_156a)

- Agile development is how software teams work. Therefore, UX designers who join software teams must also learn to adapt and play along without sacrificing the values that the human-centered design process can bring to the team.

- Product management is deeply connected with UX design. As a team member, you can support a product manager effectively.

- KPIs are not just for business and management. They are important to understand and modify so that UX design is measured and invested in by a business.

# Chapter 12: UX Design and Development

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_157a)

Gaining experience with agile software design from the last chapter has exposed you to many strategies, but to gain experience that will require some in-person work on a software team. Unfortunately, this is hard to do in a book. However, in this chapter, we will expose you to how UX designers and developers can work together to create great software. This chapter is a proxy test, and you will become aligned with how a software design team is focused on getting ideas into the world. This chapter catapults you into how to get prototypes into real products. You will be exposed to UX design development handoff strategies and focus on how to deliver UX design work effectively. An activity to generate handoff materials will give you practice on how to communicate your prototypes to developers, and finally, we will focus on the continuous integration process.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_158a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Design handoff process
  - Design handoff workflow

- Focusing on change in software

- Delivering agile software work
  - Loom video walkthrough activity

- Continuous integration and design

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_159a)

After completing this chapter, you will get practice applying software development handoff. You will be exposed to design/development handoff best practices, improve on communicating software changes via Loom, and practice continuously communicating UX design work. The design and development handoff experience you will gain in this chapter will continue to be built upon to improve your software skills and help you grow your agile design knowledge.

[Design handoff process](#contents.xhtml_sc2_160a)

As previously mentioned, software design is a team effort that leads to an exchange of ideas, work products, and design and development assets that ultimately get developed into software solutions and deployed into the hands of your users. This exchange is supposed to move solutions forward and keep software teams productive and efficient. Every software team will have slightly different rules for design handoff, but for the most part, design handoff happens when a UX designer has completed an expression of a software user interface from a given set of requirements and aligned it with a product plan. Design handoff has gone through many changes over time due to rapid changes in tools UX designers have used to create software. Back in the day, we used to craft software user interfaces using Adobe Photoshop, and handoff would include cutting up PNGs from the files and annotating assets so that developers could produce our UI’s design in code. This was slow, arduous, and left a lot to desired from a feedback and communication perspective, as shown in the following figure:

!

Figure 12.1: Design/development cycle

Photoshop created pain on UX designers and developers’ workflows and this workflow pain is directly responsible for the creation of software tools like Figma or Framer. Today our software design tools are more aligned with creating in the service of designs and developers together versus the old paradigm that separated tools and compartmentalized team members. UX design and development work together and tools like Figma with Dev mode are used to try and patch and smooth out the design to development handoff. Design handoff in today’s software environment is also aligned with agile principles and attempting to make handoff efficient, focused on feedback, and trying to reduce the need to document changes but rather making clear, concise, and useful design and development documentation, is it seamless? No. However, the current class of design and development tools seems to consider their designer and developer user workflows and agile cycles.

The reason UX design handoff is important on software teams is that design and development may not be working on features simultaneously. UX design is likely working ahead of developers in some instances and in others, iterating on existing features. UX design and development can work in parallel; however, many times, they are staggered where design works ahead of development and, with the help of product management, creates solutions and prototypes that the development team can pick up and produce into code. With the help of product management, designers and developers have clear handoff expectations.

In agile software development, this typically happens at the beginning of sprints, during planning. A UX designer can make sure their solutions are effectively documented and vetted by the business as well as their users so that the development teams can code the solutions and reduce the need for changes to the code. Agile teams should not fear changing requirements, but they should attempt to reduce the expense of modifying too much code or, worse, burning out developers with unforeseen changes.

Many developers I have work in the past have looked to me for some level of certainty in what they are coding. The best developers I have worked with were equally dedicated to shipping high-quality code and were relying on UX design to sweat the requirements and deliver accurate and precise solutions in turn they produced code outputs that reflected the quality we were all looking to achieve. In software development quality in requirements can translate to quality in bugless, working software. Here is an example of a design handoff workflow:

!

Figure 12.2: Design/Development workflow diagram

[Design handoff workflow](#contents.xhtml_sc3_161a)

Design can go through a lot of change over time and developers are not always as experienced with how UX design creates versions or establishes what is done. Design handoff is deeply connected to communicating the source of truth to developers and communicating what changed and why. The design handoff is a workflow is as follows:

1. Define the Final versions of the design. I prefer to use a Red/yellow/green light flagging method.

a. Note: Do not call file names Final or Screen-Name-Final, apply versioning, and communicate the latest version.

2. Establish clear and logical organization of screens and specs:

a. Label screen names that mirror the IA sitemap logic with versions and organize the screen in a left-to-right flow, that is, 01-Homepage-v2.3.

3. Create clear and easy-to-understand documentation:

a. Keep the user flow diagrams and storyboards in the same Figma files as pages with links and annotation notes so the developers do not have to hunt for the context information.

4. Expose the design system:

a. This includes annotating design components, aligning on labelling between design component names and development components names, as well as linking design system documentation.

5. Share the completed designs into a flow and click through the prototype:

a. Included loom video walkthroughs of your solutions and include use cases documentation or feature storytelling to communicate context and help reduce the need for in-person handoff meetings.

6. Reserve time to walk developers through the design files and establish question and feedback expectations:

a. Communicate jobs to be done and use tag comments to communicate with specific team members.

Handoff workflows change on every team, but its core handoff should reduce questions between design and development and attempt to streamline, align team members, and produce coded solutions that are the mirrors of the designs. When interviewing for UX design roles, it is recommended to ask questions about how the team does handoff.

Let us look at some handoff questions:

1. Can you tell me how your team does design/dev handoff?

2. Can you share an example of how your team managed a change in requirements?

3. Are your developers using design tools?

4. What do you think could be improved in your design/dev handoff?

5. What is your designer-to-developer ratio?

It is useful to remember that when interviewing as a candidate, you are also interviewing the company and the team you might join. Asking process questions can be an enlightening way to get a view of how a team functions. If they hesitate or are restrictive about how design and development work together, that could be a sign of caution. As a UX designer, you should be looking to demonstrate how you can work with development for the betterment of the product and the company.

[Focusing on change in software](#contents.xhtml_sc2_162a)

When learning and improving the designer and developer relationship, it is important to remember that a UX designer is an agent of change. For changes in a product to happen effectively, a UX designer must focus on the why and produce designs that accurately define how a change will occur. By focusing on why a product is changing, a UX designer can deliver what a developer needs for that change to occur for their users and businesses. In some cases, changes are made to user interface elements, whether that be changes in components, improved aesthetics, or rearrangement of elements. These are polished changes and are about refining an existing set of user experience and user interface decisions. Refinement changes can be expressed through articulating the design of screens and sharing design specifications that address an existing set of codes. Polishing changes should also include refinements in software documentation and scalable design systems used by product designers, UX designers, UI designers, and developers.

In other cases, changes can happen architecturally, including structural changes, modifying information architecture, creating new workflow, and/or designing new features. In this case, the new designs need to articulate why this new path is necessary and how the bigger changes can be achieved to the existing system. Structural change can result in larger modifications to the way a user interface works or how elements in a design system are designed or coded. Software systems can be made to be more efficient, work better, perform better, and be better for software teams and their users. Any structural change should be made with scale and effectiveness in mind. Software systems are living, breathing products and software teams should be improving them continuously, however, when architectural changes occur in our software, we should also be mindful that users will need to be made aware of the change and educated about the updates to avoid frustrated users and help smooth out the hard-earned improvements.

In either case, a UX designer should focus on what is changing and how that change has value for the users. When a UX designer is aware of the value they are bringing to users and to a business, they can articulate that value to users as well. Prototyping is a relevant strategy for either type of software change but is more highly leaned on in the architectural change space as bigger, more sequenced changes have much more impact on a user.

For example, tweaking the color on a button and its call to action (CTA) to reach a WCAG AAA rating of 7 to 1 contrast ratio might not require a prototype, but showing how that same button is used to improve an order button would require a prototype to articulate this type of change definition. Whether a change is big or small, a UX designer can modify their work and handoff elements that are built into prototypes to demonstrate their value. Smaller iterations to a user interface can be accomplished through communication with developers and larger changes should apply prototyped user flows. In either case, what changes need to be designed, developed, and maintained over time, and folding big and small changes into sharable, testable prototypes is best. The success of handing off an experience through prototyping can be understood by the speed at which teams engage in decision-making, how questions are asked, and, importantly, how cross-functional teams collaborate on the delivered software work.

As a UX designer, your role will encompass communicating with developers about the level of change that needs to be brought to a system. Changing a button might not seem like a big change, but in reality, buttons are used system-wide and, therefore, should be taken with great care. When a UX designer is allowed to focus on what is changing and why, they can determine the broader impact of changes will have on a system. Updates to user interface elements like buttons are not arbitrarily changed but rather designed correctly in the first place. When a button is improved through establishing WCAG AAA standards, a UX designer improves the overall accessibility of a product, and the reasoning can be quickly communicated to a developer and thus justifiable to the business. When done in an agile manner, it can quickly be created into a developer task, planned into a sprint, and implemented into the software code. The change might seem small, but has huge implications for how a software team functions, how they share in the responsibility of improving a product, and how they close tickets and ship meaningful solutions. A UX designer is then measured on how many improvements they can make and how quality is maintained. In the end, we want changes to improve our products. If UX designers track the quality markers of a product and measure them over time, both for the business and for their users, then they can show how their products are improving.

The truth is that if you as a UX designer can align your value and what you change in a piece of software to what a business values and tracks, then you can prove your value and help drive the business forward. Even better is if you can help a business start tracking and measuring user-centered data, which is ultimately directly aligned with the value you can bring to a business.

[Delivering agile software work](#contents.xhtml_sc2_163a)

When a UX designer focuses on the why, they can create a clear path to the delivery of work. Our software tools (Figma, Jira, etc.) are all in service of helping a UX design team and development team deliver software products to market. UX designers can build prototypes that users can validate before they get coded or brought to market, and when improved through user testing, a developer can code a high-quality, stable version. Is all software designed with this high level of quality? No, and this is why you are needed in the world. UX designers are in a quality control position, and we should want only the best solutions and prototypes to make their way into the hands of your users. By improving your prototyping process and sharing the why behind your changes with developers, you can deliver the work as well. It takes time to understand the ways to deliver great products, and we should lean on our tools to help us build better software. UX design over time includes using better communication tools like Slack or Figma. Using more asynchronous communication tools like Loom or Zoom and improving our agile ethos into better storytelling and empathy for our development counterparts as well as our users.

For example, in a previous Sr. UX role, I was supporting a development team of 15 people. At first, our team participated in a lot of team meetings because our VP of Product wanted to be involved in a lot of decision-making. All these in-person meetings resulted in a development team with members who were burning time in meetings where they could solve problems and code. Loom video recording was brought to the team, and videos of prototype progress were sent to him and our developers. These videos helped reduce our team meetings and allowed us to work more autonomously, which is part of agile software tasks in the first place. The result was our team’s development velocity sped up and our VP of Product still got to keep in the loop without the need for as many team meetings.

In order to practice delivering work in an agile method, let us practice creating asynchronous videos that capture our prototypes for sharing with developers so you, too, can use these tools to reduce meetings.

[Loom video walkthrough activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_164a)

Let us practice using hand off tools.

Total time: one-to-two hours

Setup Loom tool and Google Chrome Extension (5 minutes) through the following steps:

1. Go to: [https://www.loom.com](https://www.loom.com/).

2. Create an account.

3. Install the Loom Chrome extension:

[https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/loom-%E2%80%93-screen-recorder-sc/liecbddmkiiihnedobmlmillhodjkdmb](https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/loom-%E2%80%93-screen-recorder-sc/liecbddmkiiihnedobmlmillhodjkdmb)

4. Open your Kava Coffee prototype in Figma

5. Add a frame at the beginning of your flow:

a. Add a prototype flow title.

b. Add a short description/story of your users and what problem they are solving through the prototype flow.

6. In Figma prototype mode, change the flow starting point to your new frame:

a. Connect your title frame to your prototype flow.

b. Hit play and click through your prototype quickly.

7. Outline a quick walkthrough script:

a. Define a walkthrough time (5-10 minutes).

b. Outline your prototype flow. Intro \| Goal \| Job to be done \| Prototype Walk through \| Recap \| End

Record a walkthrough of your prototype (5-10 minutes) through the following steps:

1. Hit play on your prototype.

2. Start a Loom video:

a. Turn on your face.

b. Make sure audio is being captured.

3. Click through your prototype and follow your script outline:

a. Make sure to speak clearly.

b. Run through the user flow from start to finish.

c. Add voice-over (VO) to important aspects of your prototype flow or aspects of the designs you think need emphasis.

4. Stop and save the recording:

a. Rewatch the walkthrough video from Loom. Rewatch at a faster rate (1.5 or 2x speed).

b. Likely, there will be mistakes, miscues, or missed descriptions of prototype elements.

5. Record your Loom video:

a. Repeat steps 2-5 until your Loom video is polished and feels sharable with a developer.

6. Share your prototype walkthrough with another person, UX designer, or developer:

a. Your Loom video feedback needs to be seen by other people for you to improve it. Get used to sharing Loom videos, prototypes, and design solutions with lots of people.

| |

|----|

| Note: Loom is one of many asynchronous tools that exists, but I have used Loom to great success with my agile software design teams in the past. It has helped remove unnecessary meetings with developers and help me in my prototype communication and presentation skills which all require practice. |

[Continuous integration and design](#contents.xhtml_sc2_165a)

Now that you have some practice packaging your prototypes and solutions for developers through asynchronous procedures, let us discuss the importance of continuous integration. continuous integration (CI) is the practice of integrating code changes frequently, automatically testing them, and ensuring that an application remains stable while performing effectively by the development team. CI is a process associated with agile software development because each sprint code is completed, updates can be published to staging, and the design and product can ensure the solutions are effective and push changes to their users. CI is typically more common with web-based software solutions as browser technologies support this deployment strategy more easily since the software functions inside the browser. This is true for websites, web apps, and other cloud-based software tools. UX design is related to CI because we supply software teams with what should be changed and why, designs can also be involved in testing the final coded solutions. The design and development cycle is a symbiotic one and requires sharing back and forth. UX design can start the process by producing a prototype and handing it off to developers. The development team will then produce a coded version of the prototype and share it back with the UX designers to make sure the intentions are adhered to in the coded solution. Designers should be testing the code from developers and giving feedback where needed to improve the final solution. A prototype from Figma can be highly useful for getting close to the final intention of software, but it is still an abstraction. The software is not real until it is coded and in the hands of your users. CI with UX design can help speed up getting software solutions into your users’ hands faster. The back and forth between design and development allows for solutions to be produced at higher quality and helps UX designers and developers work closer together to achieve the best solutions.

| |

|----|

| Note: Software delivery strategies have a wide range of solutions and there is a lot of technical documentation, books, articles, and other best practices associated with these procedures. UX designers should be aware of how software is pushed to actual users because it can have big implications for a user’s experience, as how changes are communicated to users and ultimately adopted by them is a big part of our jobs. |

CI concepts can also be applied to conventional software versioning and software teams can apply changes to code inside a staging environment before pushing to all their users. When a software team has completed a significant number of improvements to the software, they can package all the changes together and deliver a new version of the software through a software update. If you have ever updated your computer to iOS, you might be familiar with the software versioning process. You can find which version of software you are using in the software settings or about this computer. Many software suites rely on a semantic versioning process. It is typically a three-decimal number, that is, 1.1.1. The first number is a major version. The second number is for Minor updates, and the third number is for security patches.

For example, this book is currently being written on a MacBook Pro 13in M1—Running MacOS Sonoma 14.2.1. So, this means the macOS has gone through 14 major versions with two minor updates and one patch.

![A screenshot of a computer Description automatically generated](images/Figure_12.3.png)

Figure 12.3: Design/Development workflow diagram

The versioning process has a very different meaning from software team to software team, but as a UX designer, you could be working on current versions of the software or be part of teams that are two or three versions ahead of the current version. By understanding your role in designing software, you can help guide future versions of the software you work on to be better. Part of being on a software team is also understanding the release cycles your software is operating under. It is generally not recommended to update and change a user’s software tools when they are likely to be using them. This typically leads to software updates occurring at night or early morning. In many software cases, users are involved in requesting an update, and other updates happen without their users’ explicit request. As a UX designer, we want our changes to be used, and that can require educating our users about the changes that occur during software updates. A huge part of doing UX design well is informing our end users about what is changing and why, as well as providing software education to make new features or fixes to flawed solutions more explicit to our current users. Getting this all right is difficult, but when it is done well, your end users are happier, your software is used more, and your software teams improve their ability to engage in agile and CI more effectively.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_166a)

In this chapter, we have learned about the design and development handoff experience, and how it is a process that you will improve upon as your agile design and development knowledge grows. Besides being a better software team member, you can make sure your software products are usable and accessible because you have handed off high-quality prototypes that have been vetted by users and infused with the human-centered ethos we have discussed. By using established standards and best practices with design and development handoff, you will improve as a UX designer, and your software teams will get faster and more productive, but you must work at it and care about improving the design and developers process.

In the next chapter, we will delve into iterating software and how UX designers can apply changes to a software product early and often with their development partners and improve their software products over time.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_167a)

- Design handoff is a learned process by both UX designers and developers.

- UX designers should practice considering their users both internal team members as well as their end-users.

- UX designers should bring sound communication skills and storytelling to the development handoff process and improve handoff quality over time.
