# Chapter 13: Iterations in Design and Development

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_168a)

Gaining experience with agile software design from the last chapter has exposed you to many strategies, but gaining experience will require some in-person experience on a software team. We will dive deeper into the value of UX design iteration and solving problems iteratively. Through practice, we will engage with shifting requirements and learn to justify a change. Finally, the reader will get practice in considering the time required to plan a feature by developing a feature roadmap.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_169a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Iteration equals learning

- Agile and changing requirements
  - Justifying a requirement change activity

- Timeline and feature rationale
  - Creating a roadmap in FigJam activity

- UX design involves early development

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_170a)

After completing this chapter, you will get practice applying software development iteration and feature planning. You will be exposed to requirement iterations, justifying changes through requirements, and will practice planning out a feature roadmap. The design and development iterative process practice that you will gain in this chapter will continue to be built upon to improve your software skills and help you grow your agile design knowledge.

[Iteration equals learning](#contents.xhtml_sc2_171a)

Software design is a team effort, and because of this, there is an exchange of ideas, work product, design, and development assets that ultimately get produced into software solutions that are deployed into the hands of your users. The agile development exchange is supposed to move solutions forward and keep software teams productive and efficient. Every software team will have slightly different rules for how iterations and changes are consumed but every software solution will go through change. Our tools change because our users change, and the teams that build software learn from their users over time. Due to this change, design and development apply iterations by learning from our sketches, prototypes, or deployed products through user research and user testing. There is no way to create perfect software, but we can improve and polish our user experiences through iteration. As a UX designer, you should be seeking out as many ways to learn about your systems as possible because design is fundamentally iterative. The iterative design process should repeat until a goal is reached, and then it should keep going. Designers who help define the goals of software have a clearer path to achieving these goals. As we have been discussing, software goals should be human-centered and informed through user research and genuine user needs. As UX designers, we should use iterative processes to reach our defined goals, but we should also recognize that goals can change. If the goal changes, so should the software; this is a cause-and-effect relationship. This is an oversimplified description, but if a UX designer can be involved, it is the end-to-end cause-and-effect relationship, then they too can impact the system and not just be at the whip of effects brought on by change. Iterative cycles are essential to improving any system:

![A diagram of a product refinement AI-generated content may be incorrect.](images/Figure_13.1.png)

Figure 13.1: Iterative cycle

Software teams can make the mistake of solving a problem that is not focused on a goal. They mistook a short-term business need for a goal and neglected to align that business’s need with a user’s need. The team was able to create the user interface for the business, but it quickly became apparent through user testing that users were not interested in the user interface addition. On software teams, UX designers should not only be valued for what they make, but they can also create great value by saying no to things or, even better, by quickly failing, as we discussed previously. There are a few ways to grasp how iterative a software team is. A great question to ask a software team is what features did not make it and why, or what does your product graveyard look like? A UX designer's role can be about building things that never make it to market so that they never make it to market and lose money. Over your career, this might lead to you having a product, idea, or application graveyard.

!

Figure 13.2: Product graveyard

| |

|----|

| Note: For example, I once worked on Theranos. The infamous blood testing company was run by convicted fraud Elizabeth Holmes in 2018. The doctor/patient blood result application we worked on was interesting and had the potential to be a good application, but was of course predicated on the blood testing technology being successful, which turned out to be a false. Needless to say, this work died on the vine. Theranos’ implosion was a swift lesson for everyone in Silicon Valley that innovation needs to work before you can build and scale the ideas. The hype machine that valued Theranos at 9 billion in 2014 without a working prototype is mindboggling, and in my experience, we all got wrapped up in hoping it would work rather than proving it does. UX designer careers are long, and not everything you work on will be successful. However, be mindful and keep your radar dishes up for red flags including toxic management, innovation chasing, and companies that do not value the hard work that goes into making good products. |

Hopefully, you do not have too many projects in your product graveyard, and if you continue to learn from your failures, you will also have plenty of exciting and valuable projects make it into the world as well.

For example, after experiencing the failure and shelving of the Theranos work, I also had the opportunity to help Paul Allan’s Vulcan Productions redo their film ventures website, which won a Webby award and also helped a few climate-conscious films reach more people. There is lots of room for helping software improve the world and all of it requires iteration.

When you design under the mindset that you will learn along the way, a UX designer and development team can build trust. The process builds a support mechanism for creating only solutions that are proven to be successful. Iterative learning is foundational to agile development, and UX designers should seek out the conditions that allow them to learn from their users and their businesses. If you are in an organization that is not allowing UX designers to learn and iterate, then consider finding a new one or doing the hard work to show the business why learning from their users and iterating their products is the way to go.

[Agile and changing requirements](#contents.xhtml_sc2_172a)

As we have discussed, agile and UX design can work well together because they are adept at addressing change. The agile development process was created because a business cannot predict the future or accurately plan for every need over a long project timeline. Simply, teams that solve problems need the flexibility to accomplish this and do so without disruptive friction or unrealistic expectations from the business. Changes happen, and as long as software teams can consume and plan for the changes, it does not become as disruptive or frustrating to the software team. Are changing requirements all good for the product? No, and in some cases, the software development teams should have a say in what requirements can change and which should not. For example, the foundational technology should not be arbitrary or able to be changed easily, if the software is coded using React, it should not be switched to PHP and HTML in the requirements suddenly. A change of this order of magnitude would be highly disruptive to the development teams and possibly require a large number of code rewrites or, worse, development efforts and hiring to be rerouted as well. Changes can come from many places, but not all changes are created equal. A UX designer should be involved in the change management process. As UX designers move up in any organization, they should be informing company strategy and hopefully guiding changes that come from the business through a human-centered lens and keeping businesses from being too change-happy. Although agile software development is used to adapt to changing requirements, this does not mean software teams should also just lay down and be blown in any direction. UX design gives the opportunity for a business to intake changes meaningfully and be strategic with the software team about which change is planned, and why changes are done.

A challenge with discussing changing requirements is that it can be difficult to encapsulate the range of what software changes might look like. Changes can take many forms, whether it be something simple like needing a different call-to-action (CTA) title. i.e., a copy change to larger changes like needing to update our database schema to support an AI search chatbot. How and when to take on small changes versus big changes is largely determined by the software team and the product manager, but UX design has a huge responsibility for making sure those changes do not negatively affect the user’s experience. In the best cases, UX design is intimately involved in orchestrating changes and guiding a business toward when and where changes should occur. Every software team will apply change differently, but every piece of software goes through change. To be better at changing requirements, there are a few tips that can help along the way.

Let us look at some tips for managing changing requirements:

- Embody the ethos of agile: Start by helping educate agile principles to the C-suite and business stakeholders. Next, mandate agile competency at the management level to embody flexibility and plan for change. Adaptability is key, so building software teams that know that requirements are bound to evolve but will embrace the agile process, allowing changing requirements to go over more smoothly. Incentive design and development to work together.

- Measure the impact of changing requirements: When new requirements or features are created, make sure to measure it. Apply the changes to existing project timelines, budgets, and most importantly resources. Work with both design and development early in the process to determine the level of effort required and the potential risks involved. Make sure to communicate the impact and level of risk a requirement change will bring.

- Communicate early and often: Do not be cagey about changes rather, be transparent in communication about why changes are necessary. Use design storytelling to convey the changes and their implications and ensure everyone is on the same page as to why the change is happening.

- Collaborate and share the load: Involve your software team early and often in brainstorming sessions, ideation opportunities, whiteboarding sessions, et cetera. Design and development should tackle any changing requirements together to determine the best way forward while ensuring the project's success.

- Re-prioritize once a requirement change occurs: Changing requirements move around priorities, make sure teams are clear about the priority of work, and reorder existing requirements if necessary. Identify the critical parts that need immediate attention and consider the impact on the project's overall goals.

- Realign the design and development plans to facilitate requirement changes: Adjust the design and development plan, including the timeline, the resources, and the technology accordingly to incorporate the changes. Changing requirements might call for re-sequencing tasks, allocating additional resources, or revisiting dependencies.

- Focus on scope changes: Assess the impact any new change will have on the project scope and consider if any adjustments are necessary. Requirement changes are not always from feature A to feature B but are also adding, or modifying existing requirements; for example, showing six most recent posts to showing 12 of the most trending posts.

- Implement version control and document the changes: Establish a version control process to manage the evolving requirements effectively. This ensures that changes are documented, evaluated, approved, and implemented in a controlled manner and can be reset back in case the requirements change again.

- Q/A test, validate, user test, and iterate: Complete the agile process by validating any changes through rigorous QA testing to ensure they meet the desired quality standards. Place changes in front of the user for user testing, including usability, accessibility, and interaction standards.

- Agile reflection and team improvements: After the requirement changes conclude, reflect on the team experience and gather insights. Identify lessons learned, both positive and negative, to enhance future agile management practices.

Remember, handling changing requirements and new features mid-development requires adaptability, effective communication, collaboration, and a willingness to embrace change. UX designers can help an agile team by greasing the wheels from user research through to prototyping and testing. By approaching change requirement situations with flexibility and a problem-solving mindset, you can navigate the challenges and deliver successful products. UX designers can also improve and smooth out the potentially disruptive nature of changing requirements through ideation, prototyping, and testing, as well as working with business stakeholders to make strategic changes that result in similar outcomes with less risk. The reality is software requirements change, whether it be from the business or from your users. Being able to adapt to changing requirements is key to building great software:

![A signpost with many directions Description automatically generated](images/Figure_13.3.png)

Figure 13.3: Design/Development changing requirements

Changing requirements are a function of a changing world and changing technology. With all this talk of changing requirements, let us evolve our experience and practice considering a change to our previous work.

[Justifying a requirement change activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_173a)

Let us practice justifying a requirement change.

Total time: One-two hours

Using your Kava Coffee solution:

1. Open up your Kava Coffee Prototype.

2. Duplicate your Figma prototype page and rename the page Kave-Coffee-requirement-change-v2.

a. Prompt: Time to put on your business hat…. Let us say you want to increase your coffee store’s inventory by also selling an assortment of teas.

3. Create a new business requirement:

a. Add a 900 x 200px frame above your homepage.

b. Label the frame new business requirement.

4. Divide your frame into six columns with two rows:

a. Add column labels: Reference \#, Requirement Summary, Business Impact, Originator, Objectives Served, Level of Effort estimate.

b. Sample:

[TABLE]

5. Write a requirement summary for the preceding prompt:

a. Make sure to consider the business impact.

b. Make up the originator or be that person.

c. Apply LOE for Design and Dev. (none/low/med/high/extreme)

6. Iterate your Kava solution prototype to address the new business requirement:

a. Version your screens and reconnect your prototype when completed.

| |

|----|

| Note: Practicing changing requirement scenarios can also build your skills in managing and adapting to changes. As a UX designer in an agile environment, I do not recommend being too rigid with your solutions as rigid mindsets will be frustrated by changing business requirements. |

Requirement changes can come in many forms, from verbal requests from clients and stakeholders to email requests and updated requirement documentation put together by business analysts or other technical team members. However, once the requirement change is delivered, it is recommended to document any business requirement change and store all requirements, new and old, into a shared business requirements document (BRD). The BRD document can change, but should reflect the requests from stakeholders for product managers, designers, and developers to see and analyze once a software product has been completed. As a UX Designer, you might not be responsible for creating a BRD, but you will be required to consume one and modify one if changes occur along the way. Here are a few questions you can ask about your organization’s BRDs to help you:

- How are they designed and who are they for?

- How specific are the requirements?

- Are requirements categorized or placed in a hierarchy?

- How likely are requirements going to change?

- When requirements change, how are they updated or altered?

There are a lot of ways a BRD and agile software development can be documented, from Google Docs and Spreadsheets to product management software like Atlassian Jira, or Monday.com. Agile software tools are attempting to solve some of these agile requirement-shifting conundrums in the form of a rigorous software development documentation process. There are considerable benefits of agile teams all using similar software tools to manage the software design and development process. Whether your software team is part of a small start-up or a large corporation, the necessity for designers and developers to communicate and manage changing requirements will remain a key factor of agile development and something teams should constantly improve over time. Go back to the agile principles. Management tools are useful but only to the point where they do not get in the way of team communication and collaboration. Remember, the first principle of agile is individuals and interactions over processes and tools. If a software team becomes over-reliant on tools like Atlassian Jira to run their team, then they will get software that mirrors what Atlassian Jira can accomplish and not necessarily what a designer and developer can do when they get together without the interference of the tool. Atlassian Jira can be quite useful but be mindful of software teams who use it in ways that work against agile in the first place. You will get a lot of experience with software documentation over your career so dive in and work on your skill that allows you to be adaptable and roll with changing requirements.

[Timeline and feature rationale](#contents.xhtml_sc2_174a)

Once business requirements are defined and determined not to be shifting much, software teams can start designing and developing the software features. The way a design and development team tackles these software features can be determined in several different ways, but typically, the designers and developers create a hierarchy and plan what goes first, second, and third based on agile sprints. The team will organize and architect the software requirements into a plan that is based on the logic of a software system and then follow it up with more complex features. Designers and developers need a timeline and deadlines to create their corresponding contributions, and the goal of any software team is that all team members have work they can do. For example, front-end development can get started writing baseline code (grids, buttons, color systems, media query rules, etc.) before a UX designer or UI designer ever specifies what that user interface will look like. Development should not be blocked by design, especially at the beginning, where there is much foundational work to be established. Once a UX designer has produced some software artifacts, then development can combine powers and collaborate more specifically on refining the foundational code. Foundational code can be aspects that define a back-end and front-end of data via a code schema. Code schema is a markup of structured data that helps search engines understand a page's content and how users should see it. Schema markup is also known as structured data and is written in a language that search engines can understand, like HTML. Before an interface can be created, there is a lot to define on the data side of a system. UX designers should be aware of what developers have started to build and attempt to work into their foundations as a way to keep the hand-off of features more effective but also not be a blocker to keeping developers working. As a UX designer having knowledge of the code systems your developers are working with and the database frameworks they are deploying to manage data and page content will allow you to collaborate more effectively with the development team. For example, I once worked on a business-to-business (B2B) enterprise software product that was managing customer account information. After spending a short working session with the back-end development team, we quickly learned that our customer’s data was not centralized into one database, and to visualize a customer’s profile, purchase history, and rewards required three unique API calls at three different query speeds. Our customer service software was slow and hard to navigate by our agents, not because we did not have the structured data, but because of the way the back-end had been mashed together. The speed at which UX and front-end could consider a solution was squeezed by the back-end. Identifying technical constraints is essential to improving a user’s experience but also allowed us to pushback on the back-end dev team. Fortunately, we got them to prioritize centralizing the data, which ultimately fixed the query speed problem and allowed us to fix the customer service agents’ speed problem. As we have been discussing, great software is not the responsibility of any one team. UX, design, front-end, back-end, and customer service, all have to work together to deliver the expected experience to the user.

Similarly, communicating the timeline of both design and development is hyper-critical as the agile process. Mostly because in software systems, multiple things happening at once, but team members should not be blocked or, worse, required to scrap work for radically different directions. Of course, requirement changes can happen, but design and development should be able to readjust the timelines and map out how that new line of work can be achieved. Planning in agile is a tricky process as the design and developers are looking to achieve their sprint commitments. When requirements change, so do sprint commitments; design and development are both responsible for shifting gears and reassessing completed work to date. In many cases, design can salvage from completed screens and pivot the work quickly through versioning. Development can do similar versioning processes as well as archive development branches to make incomplete work not just get lost. Either way, changing requirements have ripple effects on an agile software team’s work output. The better software team gets at communicating the ways changing requirements impact their timelines, the better they are at communicating their value. Timelines are a function of planning sprints and ramp-up to product roadmaps. A product roadmap is an overview of the vision, priorities, direction, and progress of a product over time. A roadmap is a plan of action that aligns a software team or larger organization to how the software will be achieved. Roadmaps are typically timeline-based to visualize teams and initiatives typically visualized as a Gantt timeline, as shown in the following figure:

![A diagram of a product development Description automatically generated](images/Figure_13.4.png)

Figure 13.4: Roadmap Gantt diagram

Roadmaps are typically Gantt sheet-based visuals, which means they are meant to represent time either in business quarters or as sprints. UX design will likely have to follow a roadmap, but hopefully, you will be able to impact a roadmap and help plan and scope portions of a software product from a design perspective. Roadmaps are difficult to practice in the abstract, but we will get our hands into one to work on your experience as well as practice how to articulate changing requirements and how they impact timelines and roadmap documentation.

[Creating a roadmap in FigJam activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_175a)

Let us practice road mapping our new feature from the changing business requirements.

Resource:

[https://www.figma.com/file/98PwoftCybZC2a7L9azGVi/Agile-Roadmap-Sample?type=whiteboard&node-id=0%3A1&t=5OLdXJdcP9AKANh7-1](https://www.figma.com/file/98PwoftCybZC2a7L9azGVi/Agile-Roadmap-Sample?type=whiteboard&node-id=0%3A1&t=5OLdXJdcP9AKANh7-1)

Total time: Two to three hours

Using your Kava Koffee solution:

1. Open a new FigJam file.

2. In the widgets, stickers, templates, and more, click the +.

a. Using Search type roadmap \| Select agile roadmap, or copy the FigJam file shared previously.

b. Add the template to your FigJam workspace. (There are many FigJam templates, so do not assume they are all good. Choose one to practice)

c. Review the Agile Roadmap template and prepare to modify it.

Modify the Agile Roadmap FigJam Template:

3. Define two workstreams:

a. Top: Product/Design/UX (you)

b. Bottom: Development (FE/BE)

4. Update the two-week sprint roadmap timeline:

a. Change dates.

b. Modify time to match what you think design and dev will need.

5. Add tasks and descriptions:

a. Add a task description of the design tasks for your new feature from the changing business requirements.

b. Add task description of the dev tasks.

6. Use arrow tools and sticky note (s) to connect actions in Workstream 1 to Workstream 2 and make sure there is overlap or handoff from design to development.

7. Add stamps, comments, or other subtasks.

8. Review your Kava Koffee roadmap for the new feature:

a. Share your roadmap.

| |

|----|

| Note: FigJam templates are useful for speed and collaboration. The more UX designers can fold into product roadmaps the stronger they can help influence and improve a product strategy. |

[UX design involving early development](#contents.xhtml_sc2_176a)

When a UX designer focuses on the why, they can create a clear path to delivery of the work, but they cannot do it alone, as we have been discussing. The reality is UX design is just one piece of the puzzle, and we can have more impact when we guide solutions forward by involving development early in the process. When considering solutions to a defined problem it is recommended to bring multiple points of view to the table. Involving development in the early stages of problem-solving can improve an agile team’s ability to find scalable solutions faster. A UX designer can consider a large number of implementations that can improve a user’s experience, but they likely have gaps in their understanding of the systems or the level of complexity some possible solution might bring on. Including development into the solution process early can help shine a light on the level of complexity a possible solution might require. Similarly, the development side might have suggestions or possible solutions that a UX designer failed to see. As we have been discussing, UX design is not the only team member who can solve problems, but we can assist in the early stages of solution-making. There are a lot of possibilities, and the UX designer’s role is to navigate as quickly as possible those options. Involving development early can be a great feedback loop to the question: what if we did X? Development can intake the options in ideation whiteboarding sessions or brainstorming workshops and accept or help iterate the options.

For example, when developers were involved early in the ideation process, it was found that quickly being able to sketch a solution on a whiteboard or in FigJam allowed them to join the conversation more productively. By reviewing quick sketches, the developer was allowed to fold into solutions with productive feedback like, that new profile view is going to be hard…we would need a BE database schema update, or we do not have a component for this UI pattern yet or Why do not we use a predictive search here? All this discussion is on work that is not even planned yet but is part of defining the possible solutions early. Another benefit to involving development early is that they can contribute solutions a designer might not have considered.

Design and development might have different skill sets but we can communicate and talk through ideas and articulate solutions as a team. If you work with a developer who does not prefer to draw, try including them in an early ideation session where they as a strong coder review ideas you as a UX designer sketch. The developer can fold in by discussing a solution and you as the seasoned sketcher, can help visualize any ideas or concepts discussed as a team. Quickly, your developer will recognize that a UX designer is there to help articulate what the system can or cannot do. It can also help developers feel more comfortable about what is going to happen on the system. In short order, the software team can become aligned on a direction for timeline interaction and quickly take the sketches produced as a team early in the process and produce them into a coded prototype with the help of the UX designer and the developer. Over time, design teams can create a nice relationship with development teams and as a software team collective, you can start to understand how UX design can help development and vice versa. The goal is to get high-quality software into the world, and getting UX design and development all on the same page is hard, but worth the effort. The beauty of the designer/developer relationship is that we all generally want to make great software. We need each other and when there is mutual respect for what we each bring to the software design process, it can produce solutions that otherwise would not exist. Hopefully, the software solutions that are manifested by design and development teams are human-centered, usable, accessible, and delightful user experiences.

As a UX designer, you cannot prevent change. On the other hand, we have unique skill sets in human-centered design and visual storytelling that align us as unique operators in the world of change. Changing requirements are a natural outcome of the world and of people, and therefore, we should build our skills to respond to those changes quickly. By working with development early and often, we can extend our change resilience to the entire software team and ultimately guide our software solutions to the best products for our users and our businesses. With this, we are able to recognize when changing requirements are good or bad for our users or businesses. Our role is and will always be aligned with quality control, and being able to intake changes that are meaningful and help our products move and respond to our users is how we keep doing it sprint after sprint.

[Conclusion](#contents.xhtml_sc2_177a)

Software in an agile environment requires a lot of design and development strategies, and gaining experience will require some in-person experience on a software team. The more familiarity you have with how the software team manages and maintains successful software, the better. As we have discussed in this chapter, changing requirements is an inevitable part of building software and the better you get at agile and working with your development partners the better your software can be when changes occur. As a UX designer, it is incumbent on you to improve your planning.

In the next chapter, you will have an opportunity to recap our learnings throughout this book and dip our toes into the future of UX design, including artificial intelligence and scalable tools for continuous learning.

[Points to remember](#contents.xhtml_sc2_178a)

- Design and development need to deal with changing requirements together.

- UX designers should practice articulating changing requirements.

- UX designers should practice planning for change and oversee the process.

- UX designers need to work with developers early in the process, and as changing requirements occur, to solve software problems.

# Chapter 14: Recap and Future of User Experience

[Introduction](#contents.xhtml_sc2_179a)

To conclude this chapter, we will get cosmic and gestate about the future of our industry and where the winds of change are blowing. You will be exposed to broader ideas and technological changes pushing the UX design industry. Through all the shifting sand, we will reflect on what we have learned and what is required of a UX designer now and in the future. To wrap up this book, we will expand on the ever-changing role of computer and computer-generated content, practice working with AI, and computer AI-driven assistant technologies. To take our skills into the real world, we will end with a discussion of the limits of any profession as well as the limits that software and computers play.

[Structure](#contents.xhtml_sc2_180a)

The chapter covers the following topics:

- Recap
  - UX design skills covered so far

  - UX design recap and reflection activity

- Future of UX design
  - AI computer assistance

  - AI-driven content changes

  - ChatGPT AI persona activity

- Sticky notes and the limits of UX design and software

[Objectives](#contents.xhtml_sc2_181a)

After completing this final chapter, you will get practice predicting the future as you are exposed to a recap of your skills, where you will reflect on what you have learned. We will dive into the future possibilities for UX design and discuss AI and computer-generated content as well as practice working with AI-driven assistant technology. To wrap up and move you into the real world, we will end with a discussion of the limits of any profession as well as the limits software and computers play. The software design practice is about continuously improving and growing our agile design knowledge, and we will send you off with these skills and more to gain.

[Recap](#contents.xhtml_sc2_182a)

If you have made it this far, then congratulations.

| |

|----|

| Note: I sincerely appreciate your efforts and as someone who in his lifetime has completed a few books, I know the risk as well as the satisfaction that comes from getting to the end. |

Hopefully, this book has been compelling enough for you to make it this far. Luckily, as an educator and author, I am comforted by the knowledge that whether you completed the book or not, along the way, you may have learned something like UX design concepts, skills, and discussions that might sink into your own experiences. A wonderful thing about UX design, as we have been discussing throughout this book, is that it is a learning profession, and learning never stops. I am helping people and extending a learning ethos that hopefully lives on in you. Now, throughout this book, we have discussed many things but let us recap a few key things you should be taking into the future.

[UX design skills covered so far](#contents.xhtml_sc3_183a)

As a reminder… YOU ARE NOT THE USER! As a UX designer, a software business will look to you for solutions, and it will be readily available for you to not learn from your user. Do not do this. Do the hard work and involve your user from start to finish. Look at the following skills:

- Problems are slippery:
  1.  Focus user research and business attention on the why before solving anything.

  2.  Software solutions can be designed and developed in many different ways, but a lot of time and effort can be wasted if a team and business do not clearly understand the problems. UX design can make sticky hands to gripping problems and not let them get away.

- UX designers as systems thinkers need qualitative and quantitative data for strategic decisions:
  1.  There are many pieces of a software system and UX designers can use their skills with logic, structure, storytelling, and more to help a business find success.

- UX design and development should fail fast through prototyping:
  1.  UX designers and developers want similar things, that is, high-quality software that solves our users’ needs and makes our businesses money. We can both get there by making fast, prototyping first, and keeping our user validating the process from start to finish.

- UX design should support agile software systems by making usable and accessible software:
  1.  Software that is usable but not accessible is neither. UX design and development should maintain caring about all their users.

- Human-centered design is user-tested design at every stage:
  1.  Designing software with human-centered methodologies means applying those concepts from beginning to end. It is not enough to just care about your user at the beginning. You should infuse empathy for your colleagues and business leaders, too.

- UX Design and development have common goals and participate in agile equally:
  1.  Software is a team endeavor and when design and development share the load they can produce great solutions. It is not a competition, software is a collaboration.

- UX design and development teams should involve each other early and hand off work efficiently and effectively:
  1.  Solve the right problem the right way. Development can help design find the right way, and design can focus on the right problem.

- Software design and development will go through changes so stay agile:
  1.  Requirements are not written on stone tablets, so build collaboration skills that allow software teams to intake changes meaningfully through prototyping and testing.

- UX design and development are not the only problem solvers. Fold in your organization and apply new tools along the way, including AI:
  1.  Software has changed the world but has also created its own problems. Work with the entire system to improve it and lean on new tools to improve accuracy, efficiency, and quality.

Some of these skills are easily attainable but most will require great practice. At this point, all the things we have done in the book are just practice. The funny thing about practice is that it leads to experience, and experience leads to knowledge, which then feeds more practice. This continuous learning process is part of the UX design practice knowledge loop.

![A diagram of a learning process Description automatically generated](images/Figure_14.1.png)

Figure 14.1: UX design practice is a knowledge loop

The act of learning is fundamentally the act of design. Whether you know it or not, by participating in the act of designing software, you are making something, and thus, you can learn from it. At first, you will likely not make great things. However, that is the beauty of it by learning about UX design and agile software processes, you are setting yourself up to make improvements and build better experiences for users.

[UX design recap and reflection activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_184a)

Let us practice reflecting.

Total time: One hour

FigJam resource: [https://www.figma.com/file/9SQ8ODXpRmXPDUZNpHW5jl/UX-Book---Recap-and-Reflect-Template-(duplicate-me)?type=whiteboard&node-id=0%3A1&t=Qbe1BjQLQpvv3hWV-1](<https://www.figma.com/file/9SQ8ODXpRmXPDUZNpHW5jl/UX-Book---Recap-and-Reflect-Template-(duplicate-me)?type=whiteboard&node-id=0%3A1&t=Qbe1BjQLQpvv3hWV-1>)

Open the FigJam recap/reflect template:

1. Duplicate the FigJam file.

a. Rename the page Your-name-Final-Recap-Reflect-UXBook.

2. Reflect on victories and learning moments:

a. Using sticky notes (S) (Green), add all the victories you can remember.

b. Add a ranking or scale using the Stamp (E) tools.

c. Add sticky notes (yellow/red) to all learning moments you want to improve on.

Next, give recognition to other people, resources, YouTube videos, etc. that helped you along the way, and add screenshots, links, and sticky notes to give recognition.

3. Finally, we take some time to think about ideas to improve on or continue to learn deeper:

a. Using either mind-mapping or sticky notes, capture all the concepts, ideas, or skills you want to improve or keep learning more about.

b. Share your reflection with someone close to you.

[Future of UX design](#contents.xhtml_sc2_185a)

Now that you have reflected on our time together, let us discuss what the future brings.

| |

|----|

| Note: Would it not be ironic if I told you the future right now!? I cannot do that, and neither can anyone else. The future is opaque for a reason, and all we can do is recognize our role in the larger systems and build our design skills so we can adapt to the future more quickly. |

The quicker our skills change, the more we can learn, and the more we can improve our user’s experiences. The future is a by-product of time, and none of us are Doc Brown from Back to the future movies. Since we do not have a DeLorean time machine, we cannot go back, and we cannot go forward. All we can do is affect the now. If you have soaked up anything along the way, you might recognize that software has the opportunity to impact people who then impact the now, who then impact the future. If UX designers care and infuse human-centered problem-solving into the core or solutions with the help of developers, then maybe, just maybe, we can get better software solutions. What makes UX design such a fascinating vocation is that we are not expected to predict the future, but we do have a responsibility to design for it.

![A drawing of a person in a suit Description automatically generated](images/Figure_14.2.png)

Figure 14.2: Peter Drucker – Austrian American Management Consultant

The best way to predict the future is to create it

- Peter Drucker, Austrian American management consultant

Creating is at the foundation of all UX design and software design teams do; therefore, we should continue to improve our own human-centered, agile development, software building, community reflecting, collaboration improvement, accessibility and usability making, problem-solution, design and development framework executions, business acumen, and much more. There is a lot to learn, and luckily, we get to do it as a team. You will not be responsible for knowing it all. On the other hand, staying open to how teams change, and learning from your users will prepare you for helping in the now, and in the future.

The future of the UX design profession is also undefined, which is a good thing, but design and development will remain. They will look very different than they are today, but the skills used to build things have always changed. Just go talk to an older designer about using Flash or a developer about HTML v1. Our systems and tools are always changing and it is incumbent on us to guide the direction these systems, tools, and the skills that predicate them go. Some new exciting tools are already starting to shift and change how UX Designers and software are produced, including generative artificial intelligence (AI), AR/VR, and large language models (LLMs). There are and will be many books written about AI topics but as a UX designer, you should find a way to experiment with them and use them in your creative processes. We hope you, as a UX designer, are willing to keep learning and practicing with different software tools. The reality is, if a UX designer is not willing to learn new software tools, then they are likely not that good at designing software themselves. A UX designer must continue to experiment, experience, and ultimately learn to control and command the array of tools available to us both to keep us effective and efficient and to help UX designers create the future of software itself. Change is inevitable, and we UX designers must stay at the tip of the spear.

[AI computer assistant](#contents.xhtml_sc3_186a)

Software is what elevates our computers to the level of utility. Google Maps to banking software allows us to go places, buy things, and keep the systems moving and our societies humming. Software, at the minimum, should be usable. When software is done well, it is also accessible to all users, and when it is good, it is a delightful user experience for all its users. The goal of anyone making software should be to make what their users want. If we fundamentally hold that software is for humans, then we should make sure our software is infused with humanity, from research to UX design to UI design and through to the data that is gathered from a developed piece of software.

Computer software has a huge impact on making our hardware useful, usable, accessible, and delightful. This has also led to improved computer processing power, which has ushered in the scalability of artificial intelligence. AI software is used to manage, crunch, and make sense of large amounts of data as well as other applications. AI can be integrated into existing software tools like auto-correct or spell check or can be autonomous systems, like robots or chatbots, automated customer service emails, etc. AI can be divided into five branches within computer science and is a rapidly growing field. Let us look at them in detail:

- Natural language processing (NLP): The ability through which computers understand text and speak words in the same way human beings can. Products like Alexa, Siri, or Google Translate all rely on being able to intake voice and produce a result.

- Machine learning (ML): This is the idea of training a computer. It focuses on data and algorithms to mirror the way that humans learn, allowing computers to accomplish tasks and improve accuracy. Concepts like facial recognition, password protection, recommendation engines, etc.

- Neural networks (Artificial neural networks (ANNs) or simulated neural networks (SNNs): The idea is about connecting computer systems that are inspired by the human brain and are related to ML. By allowing computer networks to mimic the way that biological neurons signal to one another in the human brain, neural networks allow ML and deep learning algorithms to work. Neural networks are predicated on the internet and how systems share and spread information, including ideas of Open Ledger of cryptocurrency and batch rendering, etc.

- Expert systems (ES): Computer applications developed to focus on and solve complex problems in a particular domain. Expert systems use the knowledge base of a domain and bring that knowledge to bear on the facts of a particular situation. These include areas such as crypto trading/mining, high-risk credit decisions, advertising decision-making, and manufacturing decisions.

- Robotics: An interdisciplinary field that artfully deploys design, computer science, engineering, and human interaction to impact manufacturing, construction, operations, and other applications. Robotics’ goal is to design machines that can help and assist humans.

AI is supported by hardware and software and is going to continue to be integrated into systems that help humans access information faster, address time-sensitive needs, and eliminate tedious, dangerous, or other tasks better suited for computers or robots than humans. AI has a looming risk of creating computer systems that blatantly ignore, override, or change human decisions; however, if designed with humanity and aligned with human needs, those threats can be left to Hollywood or sci-fi to delve into.

[AI-driven content changes](#contents.xhtml_sc3_187a)

Today, we interact with computers like it is oxygen. The computer has been a dominant force in our day-to-day existence, and the ways we interact with computers have changed over time. Computers and software are fundamentally supported by the ways humans can interact with each other in the world, and in many cases, our users are not interacting with an interface they are consuming content. The user interface is just a means to an end. A vehicle for the stuff our users want. AI is rapidly becoming that interface. AI is itself a computer, and although the interface might look and feel different, is it still synonymous with the dominant computer paradigm we have been so thoroughly discussing and trying to become experts in. AI can also assist in how content changes as well as how content gets generated, which is what makes it so viable as a tool for UX designers. AI has the potential to change our current computer interaction paradigms, but for now, it will be integrated into our current user experiences.

Remember, handling changing requirements and new features mid-development is what agile requires. Teams that are adaptable, effective communicators, willing to collaborate, and willing to embrace change. AI is also changing, and we can use AI tools to capture what is changing and why more effectively. UX designers can help an agile team by greasing the wheels from user research through to prototyping and testing. AI tools can assist in speeding up user research, product analysis, feature development, and the software prototyping that is so vital in achieving success. By approaching changing requirements with the assistance of AI, you can navigate the challenges and deliver successful products. UX designers can also improve and smooth out the potentially disruptive nature of changing requirements through ideation, prototyping, and testing as well as working with business stakeholders to make strategic changes that result in similar outcomes with less risk.

[UX design and machine learning](#contents.xhtml_sc4_188a)

Machine learning is the idea of training a computer. It focuses on data and algorithms to mirror the way that humans learn, allowing computers to accomplish tasks and improve accuracy. Concepts like facial recognition, password protection and recommendation engines, etc. Machine learning is deeply connected with neural networks (NNs) \[Artificial neural networks (ANNs) or simulated neural networks (SNNs)\] which is the idea is about connecting computer systems that are inspired by the human brain and are related to ML. By allowing computer networks to mimic the way that biological neurons signal to one another in the human brain, neural networks allow ML and deep learning algorithms to work. Neural Networks are predicated on the internet and how systems share and spread information, including ideas of the Open Ledger of cryptocurrency and batch rendering, etc. ML can also encompass expert systems (ES)—Computer applications developed to focus on and solve complex problems in a particular domain. Expert systems use the knowledge base of a domain and bring that knowledge to bear on the facts of a particular situation. These include areas such as crypto trading/mining, high-risk credit decisions, advertising decision-making, and manufacturing decisions.

If you have been paying any attention to technology companies, the focus and constant beat of productivity with AI is all the rage. Due to new advances in server processing technology from Nvidia, and scaling of LLMs like ChatGPT, and generative AI, like Midjourney, Visual Electric AI, or Nex.art, the opportunities for applying AI in our software-driven world are plentiful. However, like the land grabs of the early 20th century, there might be an opportunity, but that also comes with a lot of chaos and risk, and there are entire books dedicated to these topics, which if you are interested, go pursue them.

[UX design and AI tools](#contents.xhtml_sc4_189a)

AI is best when it is used as a tool. Just as desktop publishing modified type settings for newspapers and magazines for graphic designers in the 1980s-90, or photography scaled image making from painters at the turn of the century, AI is a new set of creative processes that can help produce more quality when executed through the lens of craft and human-centered focused designers. The goal is that quality is emitting from the designers and artists and not from the AI. As designers, we should apply these tools to our process not as a cheat code or a speed modifier but as a depth gauge.

For example, I have used [www.visualelectric.com](http://www.visualelectric.com/) AI during workshops with students and clients to help speed up our UX scenario-building and storyboarding process. By sketching simple stick figures, which most clients and early UX design students can do, we can then rely on AI to render those quick sketches into d

To investigate and produce higher levels of quality across large spans of inquiry and not just a speed exercise. Speed can be a good thing but only when it is aligned with improving quality through the lens of an artist and designer. The UX designer's role is to keep a keen eye on the factors in our industry that catalyze change and shake our boats. Generative AI does all these things. UX designers should focus on quality and craft, and we should believe that AI can amplify these qualities in all of us, but especially UX designers. We need to continue to focus on using our tools, our thinking, and our time to improve not only the outputs of AI but how we as problem solvers use the tools themselves to improve people's lives.

[ChatGPT AI persona activity](#contents.xhtml_sc3_190a)

Let us practice using an AI (ChatGPT) to assist in a UX design task.

Total time: 1 hour

Prompt: Can you help me create a user persona whose purpose is to support local businesses and create a community? Please act out the user persona according to the information I provide next.

Refer to the following steps:

1. Go to ChatGPT: [https://chat.openai.com/](https://chat.openai.com/) or other LLM AI and signup/login.

2. Copy the preceding prompt into the message input form: and hit return.

3. Follow along with ChatGPT. Answer any questions ChatGPT has for you:

a. Getting specific with ChatGPT can help it produce better results.

b. Hit Return.

4. Review the User Persona details as well as the Persona Scenario:

a. Ask Chat GPT to tweak any details.

b. Copy the details to place the copy into Figma to build a user persona.

5. Next, create a user persona file in Figma:

a. Create a new frame (1440x900).

b. Using the type tool, paste the user persona copy from ChatGPT.

c. Add an image of your user.

d. Cut up and move around the persona copy from ChatGPT, clean up and make your user persona presentable.

6. Review the User Persona and share it with a friend or colleague:

a. See if they can tell if ChatGPT produced some of the work or not.

b. Get feedback about your user persona and make iterations.

7. Continue to practice with ChatGPT to improve your prompts and allow it to assist in the creation of user personas and so much more. Rinse and repeat as needed.

| |

|----|

| Note: ChatGPT and other AI tools are not replacements for doing the work. They speed up a UX designer, and in many cases, the outputs will need to be finetuned, iterated, and fact-checked by a real person. Do not take content from ChatGPT as a final product or total truth. These tools are helpful but be careful to not allow AI to make you the UX designer lazy or replace you as the “thinker” because ChatGPT and other AIs can not think. They should assist you and speed you up but not replace your own intuition and understanding of the problems and the people for whom you are designing. |

There is no replacement for hard work from a UX designer, and if ChatGPT is fed bad prompts or stereotyped content as a starting place for who a user persona is, it will only amplify those flaws. Use AI as a tool like Figma or any other piece of software that helps you execute software solutions. AI can be quite useful, or it can require much more maintenance and attention. AI tools should be scrutinized with much more analysis and be used sparingly at first until a UX designer or developer can learn how to use them effectively. AI tools should not necessarily be feared unless an AI is built to fully replace your role. However, in most cases, there is no AI sophisticated enough to solve UX design and UI design problems. Can AI replicate and mirror existing UX design or UI design artifacts like user personas, user stories, or UI specs? Absolutely, and in many cases, this is a good thing because AI can take on tedious, time-consuming, and often laborious tasks, which will free up UX designers and UI designers to spend more time with their real users or move solutions faster into markets. Speed is not the only value multiplier of AI. AI allows designers to extend their existing skills in research, problem analysis, drawing, writing, visual thinking, content creation, prototyping, and testing into increasingly complex problem-solving spaces. AI has its own set of design and software problems and needs UX designers to develop software that makes AI useful in the first place. Generative AI can assist a designer in many ways, and the opportunity to integrate it into existing software or design and create new novel applications is quickly approaching. We hope that the skills we have practiced throughout this book can and will be applied by you in these uncharted spaces. Remember, AI is not a replacement for doing human-centered design but rather an amplifier of a UX designer’s existing skills. Go, experiment, and see which AI tools help you create. Use AI with a clear vision so that the AI tools do not undermine or diminish a software team’s collaboration and agile abilities. As UX designers, we also need to make sure our tools are not unraveling the economic incentives that make UX design possible in the first place. There are many wicked problems when it comes to AI including but not limited to intellectual property rights, fair usage, authenticity, wage theft, and more. Start advocating for your tools, AI or any other, to ethically support the creative, design, and development professionals, and start supporting tools that have missions to amplify our communities and not replace or undermine them altogether.

[Sticky notes and the limits of UX design and software](#contents.xhtml_sc2_191a)

Perhaps the present is less opaque with the skills you have acquired throughout this book, and we hope it can lead you to create great software with a team of equally dedicated designers and developers. However, UX design is not a boundless, always-rising, always-improving, always-growing profession. Design, creativity, collaboration, development, and software all have their limits. Just because you are a UX designer and can help use your skills in solving problems for users does not always mean that the solution will make it to market or that your stick note ideas will be valued by your organization. Software cannot stop human nature but rather is a catalyst for changing human behavior itself.

When big tech firms lay off tens of thousands of employees with the touch of a button, we have a software problem. When social media can become an eco-chamber of groupthink, conspiracy theories, hate, and violence, then we have a software problem. When technology is built to be ephemeral, resulting in the draining of limited precious resources, we have a software problem. When a software company is incentivized only by money and for the extraction of wealth for a limited few at the top, we have a software problem. When AI servers are churning through electricity that is warming our planet with greenhouse emissions, we have a software problem, and the list goes on and on. The limits of our design, development, and technology spaces can get quite pessimistic quickly when all you see are the negative outcomes of problems. On the other hand, the wicked problems that are lurking in our software, organizations, communities, governments, and societies are equally addressable through the human-centered design process. Many of them are addressable through software as well, which leads me back to the beginning. Every profession has its limits, and our job is to push the limits to improve the world.

The limits of our software profession should not be pessimistic, but rather, we have the opportunity to take all those challenges and combat them with better systems. The skills we have been practicing throughout this book are not one-off solutions but an arsenal of processes that can be applied deep into the future, and when applied with care and craft, we can improve the world.

[Conclusion and send-off](#contents.xhtml_sc2_192a)

Effective design has always been a lofty goal, from physical products to digital ones. From computer systems to robotic delivery systems, the ways we devise and build effective systems are and will always be changing, and designers can be equipped to address this change. Along with how a group of people (Business, product, UX design, UI design, and FE/BE engineering) get aligned and produce useful, usable, accessible, and hopefully delightful systems is what you should look out for and continue to seek in your future teams. Luckily, there are plenty of tools and online resources to continue to help you grow your UX design skills and the number of software teams that follow the ethos we have been discussing has increased. Keep practicing and go design and develop human-centered software that makes our world a better place.

If you have liked this conversation and know other UX designers or not who would benefit from the things we have covered in the book, please share it.

| |

|----|

| Note: I genuinely hope that my book is not read and then doomed to an existence of getting dusty on a shelf or worse relegated to the landfill. On the other hand, like the design process itself, I hope this book is shared, modified by you, the reader, and then passed onto the next user, who will make their modifications, thus allowing this book to participate in the design process too. Please pass it along and do not be afraid to share your knowledge. |

Remember, never stop learning. Go forth and make systems, (software and beyond) better through UX design.
